Trapping Techniques (20) -

Practice Land Trapping Techniques Before the Season
Standard Source: Student manual 2005
To ensure proficiency and safety when using body-gripping traps, follow these steps:

1. Review local regulations regarding the use of body-gripping traps to understand legal requirements and restrictions.
2. Study trapping techniques...
To ensure proficiency and safety when using body-gripping traps, follow these steps:

1. Review local regulations regarding the use of body-gripping traps to understand legal requirements and restrictions.
2. Study trapping techniques through books, videos, or classes to learn proper placement and handling methods.
3. Practice setting traps in a controlled environment, such as a safe area away from public access or non-target animals.
4. Familiarize yourself with trap mechanics, including how they operate and how to release captured animals safely.
5. Develop skills in identifying target species to avoid capturing non-target wildlife, especially pets.
6. Understand the ethical responsibilities of trapping, including minimizing animal suffering and ensuring humane handling.

Important Considerations:
• Always prioritize safety when practicing with live traps.
• Never practice in areas where non-target animals may be present unless you have taken measures to prevent accidental capture.
• Ensure that all equipment is in good working condition before use.
land trapping, body-gripping traps, practice techniques, animal welfare, trap safety
Constructing a Deadfall
Standard Source: Deadfalls and Snares
Materials Needed:
• A sturdy pole or log
• Strong rope or chain
• A heavy weight (e.g., rock, log)
• A trigger mechanism (e.g., stick, string)
• Bait (optional, e.g., food, scent attractant)

Steps to Construct a Deadfall:
1. **Select the...
Materials Needed:
• A sturdy pole or log
• Strong rope or chain
• A heavy weight (e.g., rock, log)
• A trigger mechanism (e.g., stick, string)
• Bait (optional, e.g., food, scent attractant)

Steps to Construct a Deadfall:
1. Select the Location: Choose an area where animals are likely to pass, such as near trails, feeding areas, or water sources.
2. **Set Up the Main Structure:
- Place the pole horizontally across two sturdy supports (e.g., trees) at a height that allows the animal to trigger it.
- Secure the ends of the pole to the supports using rope or chain.
3. Attach the Weight:
- Hang a heavy weight from one end of the pole, ensuring it is positioned so that when triggered, it will fall and strike the target.
4. **Create the Trigger Mechanism:
- Use a stick or string to create a tripwire or pressure plate that activates the deadfall when stepped on.
- Ensure the trigger is sensitive enough to activate with minimal disturbance but not so sensitive that it triggers accidentally.
5. Test and Adjust:
- Test the deadfall by simulating an animal's movement to ensure it functions properly.
- Make adjustments as needed for sensitivity, positioning, or effectiveness.
6. Optional Baiting (for Bait Set Deadfalls):
- Place bait near the trigger mechanism to attract animals to the area and increase the likelihood of triggering the deadfall.

Notes:
• Different types of deadfalls may be tailored for specific animals, such as otters or martens, by adjusting location, height, and sensitivity.
• The "Pinch Head" variation involves a more refined trigger mechanism to ensure precise activation.
deadfall, trapping, hunting, pole deadfall, trigger mechanism, bait set, otter deadfall, marten deadfall, pinch head
Creating a Cubby Set for Mink and Muskrats
Standard Source: Student manual 2005
Objective: Construct a cubby set to trap mink and muskrats on sloping banks where pocket sets are not feasible.

Steps:
1. Identify a sloping bank with tracks indicating furbearer activity.
2. Build the cubby using rocks, logs, or old...
Objective: Construct a cubby set to trap mink and muskrats on sloping banks where pocket sets are not feasible.

Steps:
1. Identify a sloping bank with tracks indicating furbearer activity.
2. Build the cubby using rocks, logs, or old boards, creating a small enclosure.
3. Make a depression at the entrance of the cubby to place the trap.
4. Place bait or lure at the back of the cubby to attract animals.
5. Insert a foothold trap into the depression and bed it firmly.
6. Use a submersion trapping technique with either a slide wire or tangle stake for secure deployment.

Notes:
• Ensure the trap is stable and does not wobble.
• Cubby sets are particularly useful in areas where the bank slopes too much for traditional pocket sets.
cubby set, mink trap, muskrat trap, sloping bank, foothold trap, submersion technique, slide wire, tangle stake
General Considerations for Trapping Animals
Standard Source: USMC Winter Survival Course Handbook
General Considerations for Trapping Animals

Knowing a few general hints and tips will make the trapping of animals easier and considerably more effective. The eight general considerations to take game are:

1. Know your game: Knowing the...
General Considerations for Trapping Animals

Knowing a few general hints and tips will make the trapping of animals easier and considerably more effective. The eight general considerations to take game are:

1. Know your game: Knowing the habits of the animal you want to trap or snare will help increase your chances. Such things as when and where they move, feed, and water will help you determine where the set can be most effectively placed.

2. Keep things simple: You don't have time in a survival situation to construct elaborate sets and they do not necessarily do a better job.

3. Place sets in the right place: Animals will travel and stop in certain locations. That is where to build sets.

4. Cover up your scent: Animals will avoid a set which smells threatening or unusual to them (i.e., human scent or P.O.L.’s from equipment or clothing).
- Man leaves a scent through the pores of the skin by the sweat glands. Use an odorless contact glove when building a set. It may take up to three days for your scent to dissipate if made without gloves.
- Certain boot soles and clothing may leave a scent, generally this can be detected by the human nose. If noticed, attempt to mask the scent with smoke from your fire.

5. Use the right type of set: Certain sets work better than others do for a particular animal.

6. Use the correct equipment: Using the correct equipment is paramount to success. This includes the weight of the lifting device in proportion to the animal’s weight, the cordage or wire to hold the animal’s strength, and trigger tension.

7. Check sets: Check your sets twice daily: morning and evening. Checking your traps less than twice a day can allow your game to escape, rot, or be taken by other predators.

8. Lure your sets: Lures will add to your chances of success. Certain lures are appropriate at certain times of the year, depending upon the animal desired.
- Bait Lures:
- MRE peanut butter, cheese spread, or jelly.
- Dead carcasses.
- Dead rodents.
- Gland or Territorial Lures:
- Animal urine mixed with beaver castors or animal glands.
- Curiosity Lures:
- Single feather, bird wing, piece of fur tied and suspended under a tree limb, allowed to freely move with the breeze.
trapping, scent masking, lure types, animal habits, survival trapping techniques
General Techniques to Kill Animals Using the SICK Acronym
Standard Source: USMC Winter Survival Course Handbook
General Techniques to Kill Animals Using the SICK Acronym

A general technique is the method in which the trap is intended to kill the animal. The acronym SICK is useful in remembering these techniques:

• S - Strangle: This method...
General Techniques to Kill Animals Using the SICK Acronym

A general technique is the method in which the trap is intended to kill the animal. The acronym SICK is useful in remembering these techniques:

• S - Strangle: This method strangles the animal, such as a snare.
• I - Impale: This method pushes a stake through the animal, such as a spiked dead fall.
• C - Crush: This method crushes the animal, such as a deadfall for a chipmunk.
• K - Knock: This method knocks a larger animal unconscious, such as a deadfall.
SICK acronym, killing techniques, trapping methods, survival hunting
Selecting Effective Lures for Trapping Based on Season and Animal Behavior
Standard Source: Student manual 2005
### Selecting Effective Lures for Trapping Based on Season and Animal Behavior

Overview:
Lure selection depends on the time of year, target species, and animal behavior.

Key Considerations:
• Food lures are most effective in the early...
### Selecting Effective Lures for Trapping Based on Season and Animal Behavior

Overview:
Lure selection depends on the time of year, target species, and animal behavior.

Key Considerations:
• Food lures are most effective in the early part of the trapping season when animals are actively seeking food.
• Gland lures become more important later in the season as animals begin looking for mates.
• Curiosity lures may work at any time, especially when the animal is not hungry or searching for a mate.

Specific Applications:
• Urine lures are particularly effective for trapping foxes and coyotes. These animals mark their territory by urinating on objects, which triggers territorial responses in other animals.
• Urine can be used to attract animals to your trap set by mimicking natural marking behavior.

Best Practices:
• Use a combination of lure types depending on the season and target species.
• Avoid overusing any single type of commercial lure, as animals may become accustomed to its scent and avoid it.
• Consider making your own lures for increased effectiveness and variety.

Additional Notes:
• The use of bait, lure, and urine should be tailored based on the time of year, location, and target species.
• Non-target species or domestic animals may be attracted to your set, which can reduce trapping success and create problems.
• Refer to Appendix A for guidance on selecting appropriate lures and baits for specific furbearers.
lure selection, trapping season, food lure, gland lure, curiosity lure, urine lure, fox trap, coyote trap, selective trapping
Set Up a Channel Set for Trapping Muskrat, Mink, Otter, and Beaver
Standard Source: Student manual 2005
Channel Set:

• Identify underwater paths called channels used by muskrat, mink, otter, and beaver.
• Place a submerged body-gripping trap at the bottom of the channel.
• If the channel is too wide, use sticks or brush to narrow the path and...
Channel Set:

• Identify underwater paths called channels used by muskrat, mink, otter, and beaver.
• Place a submerged body-gripping trap at the bottom of the channel.
• If the channel is too wide, use sticks or brush to narrow the path and guide the animal into the trap.
• Use a blocking pole across the top of the trap to make the animal dive below it.
• Secure the trap with stakes and sticks to position it correctly in the channel.
channel set, body-gripping trap, muskrat trapping, mink trapping, otter trapping, beaver trapping

Float Set:

• Set traps on logs or homemade platforms.
• Attach traps on a chain or wire.
• When the muskrat is trapped, it will enter the water and the weight of the trap will pull it under.
• Place branches or sticks over the top of the trap to keep ducks or other birds from stepping on it.

Set Up a Scent Mound Set for Trapping Beaver
Standard Source: Student manual 2005
Scent Mound Set:

• Locate or create a beaver scent mound made of mud marked with castor.
• Hide beaver-sized foothold traps under 3 to 4 inches of water using a securely staked sliding wire submersion rig.
• If no natural scent mound is found,...
Scent Mound Set:

• Locate or create a beaver scent mound made of mud marked with castor.
• Hide beaver-sized foothold traps under 3 to 4 inches of water using a securely staked sliding wire submersion rig.
• If no natural scent mound is found, make one and mark it with castor lure.
• This set is most effective in late season.
scent mound set, beaver trapping, castor lure, sliding wire submersion rig, trap positioning

Standard Source: Student manual 2005
Obstruction Set:

• Bed a foothold trap in shallow water using a tangle stake or sliding wire submersion rig.
• No bait or lure is needed for this set.

Set Up an Under Ice Beaver Bait Set
Standard Source: Student manual 2005
Under Ice Beaver Bait Set:

• Ensure the ice is safe and have someone with you for assistance if needed.
• Chop a hole in the ice near a beaver den, following state regulations regarding minimum distance from the den.
• Attach the trap and bait...
Under Ice Beaver Bait Set:

• Ensure the ice is safe and have someone with you for assistance if needed.
• Chop a hole in the ice near a beaver den, following state regulations regarding minimum distance from the den.
• Attach the trap and bait to a long pole and push it deep into the mud under the water.
• The pole should extend well out of the ice so that a trapped beaver cannot reach the hole to breathe.
under ice beaver set, bait trap, ice safety, long pole setup, beaver trapping
Setting a Climb Out Set (Feedbed Set) for Aquatic Furbearers
Standard Source: Student manual 2005
Objective: Create a climb out set to trap muskrats, beaver, otter, and nutria near their feeding areas.

Steps:
1. Identify distinct trails or slides where animals climb out of the water to feed.
2. Place a foothold trap just under the...
Objective: Create a climb out set to trap muskrats, beaver, otter, and nutria near their feeding areas.

Steps:
1. Identify distinct trails or slides where animals climb out of the water to feed.
2. Place a foothold trap just under the water at the point where the slide enters the land.
3. Use either a tangle stake or sliding wire submersion rig for secure deployment.
4. No bait or lure is needed for this set, as animals are naturally drawn to feeding areas.

Notes:
• This technique is especially effective for muskrats and beavers near water’s edge.
• Ensure the trap is submerged correctly and does not wobble in the water.
climb out set, feedbed set, muskrat trap, beaver trap, otter trap, foothold trap, submersion rig, tangle stake, sliding wire
Setting a Muskrat Den Set (Bank Hole Set)
Standard Source: Student manual 2005
Objective: Create a den set to trap muskrats near their underwater dens.

Steps:
1. Look for signs of muskrat activity such as chewed vegetation floating on water, or lodges made from cattails or reeds in shallow marshes.
2. Identify...
Objective: Create a den set to trap muskrats near their underwater dens.

Steps:
1. Look for signs of muskrat activity such as chewed vegetation floating on water, or lodges made from cattails or reeds in shallow marshes.
2. Identify the openings around the base of the lodge where muskrats enter and exit.
3. Use a body-gripping trap for this set, as it is most effective for den sets.
4. Place small sticks in the upper jaws of the trap to keep it upright in water.

Notes:
• Muskrat dens are typically located just below the surface of water on stream or lake banks.
• Ensure that the trap is positioned correctly at the entrance of the den.
muskrat den set, bank hole set, body-gripping trap, muskrat trapping, lodge set, underwater den, cattail lodge
Setting a Trail Set (Blind or Natural Set)
Standard Source: Student manual 2005
Objective: Create an effective trail set for trapping furbearers like muskrat, raccoon, mink, beaver, and otter.

Steps:
1. Identify a narrow spot on the trail near water’s edge where furbearers regularly travel.
2. If no natural narrow spot...
Objective: Create an effective trail set for trapping furbearers like muskrat, raccoon, mink, beaver, and otter.

Steps:
1. Identify a narrow spot on the trail near water’s edge where furbearers regularly travel.
2. If no natural narrow spot is found, use logs or rocks to narrow the path.
3. Dig a shallow depression in the bank at the chosen location.
4. Place a foothold trap in the depression and bed it firmly into the mud.
5. Use either the sliding wire or tangle stake technique to make it a submersion set.
6. Do not use lure or bait for trail sets, as they are effective without them.

Notes:
• Ensure the trap is stable and does not wobble.
• Use small sticks in water to guide animals toward the trap if needed.
trail set, natural set, blind set, foothold trap, submersion technique, sliding wire, tangle stake, muskrat, raccoon, mink, beaver, otter
Using Knowledge of Furbearers to Select Trapping Locations
Standard Source: Student manual 2005
### Using Knowledge of Furbearers to Select Trapping Locations

Overview:
Understanding the habits, habitat preferences, and food sources of furbearers is essential for selecting effective trapping locations.

Key Considerations:
• Each...
### Using Knowledge of Furbearers to Select Trapping Locations

Overview:
Understanding the habits, habitat preferences, and food sources of furbearers is essential for selecting effective trapping locations.

Key Considerations:
• Each species of furbearer has specific habitat requirements, diet, and behavioral patterns.
• Use this knowledge to identify areas where target animals are likely to be found.

Best Practices:
1. Research Target Species:
- Study the preferred habitats, food sources, and movement patterns of the species you are targeting.
2. Choose Strategic Locations:
- Set traps in areas that align with the natural behavior of the target animal (e.g., near food sources, travel corridors, or den sites).
3. Avoid High-Risk Areas:
- Avoid setting traps in locations where non-target animals are likely to be present.
4. Use Environmental Cues:
- Look for signs such as tracks, scat, and feeding areas that indicate the presence of target species.

Additional Notes:
• Selecting appropriate trapping locations increases your chances of success while minimizing unnecessary disturbance to other wildlife.
• Refer to Appendix A for detailed information on the habits and preferences of different furbearer species.
furbearer habitat, trap location selection, animal behavior, food sources, selective trapping
Using Selective Trapping Techniques to Avoid Non-Target Species
Standard Source: Student manual 2005
### Using Selective Trapping Techniques to Avoid Non-Target Species

Overview:
Selective trapping involves making sets that target specific furbearers while minimizing the risk of capturing non-target animals or pets.

Key Considerations:
-...
### Using Selective Trapping Techniques to Avoid Non-Target Species

Overview:
Selective trapping involves making sets that target specific furbearers while minimizing the risk of capturing non-target animals or pets.

Key Considerations:
• Each time you set a trap, make it with the intention of catching a specific furbearer.
• Take steps to prevent accidental capture of non-target species or domestic animals.

Best Practices:
1. Understand Furbearer Behavior:
- Know the habitat preferences, food sources, and habits of the target species.
- Use this knowledge to choose optimal locations for trapping.
2. Use Selective Equipment:
- Choose traps and sets that are designed to catch specific animals.
- Avoid using broad-spectrum lures or baits that may attract non-target species.
3. Monitor Traps Regularly:
- Check traps frequently to ensure they are functioning properly and not attracting unintended animals.
4. Minimize Bait Attraction:
- Use bait sparingly, especially in areas where non-target animals may be present.
- Consider using scent lures that are specific to the target species.

Additional Notes:
• The presence of non-target animals at your trap site reduces your chances of successfully catching the animal you want.
• Selective trapping techniques can increase your success rate and reduce conflicts with other wildlife or domestic animals.
selective trapping, non-target species, trap set design, furbearer behavior, habitat selection, bait usage

Traps And Snares (11) -

Construct a Squirrel Pole Using Simple Loop Snares
Standard Source: FM 21 76 1 Survival Evasion and Recovery Multiservice Procedures
To construct a squirrel pole:

1. Use simple loop snares.
2. Make the noose opening slightly larger than the animal's head:
- 3-finger width for squirrels.
- Fist-sized for rabbits.
3. Once tightened, the wire locks in place, preventing...
To construct a squirrel pole:

1. Use simple loop snares.
2. Make the noose opening slightly larger than the animal's head:
- 3-finger width for squirrels.
- Fist-sized for rabbits.
3. Once tightened, the wire locks in place, preventing reopening and the animal’s escape.
4. Placement of snares:
- Set as many as possible.
- Avoid disturbing the area.
- Use funneling (natural or improvised) to guide animals into the trap.
squirrel pole, loop snares, trapping, hunting

Constructing a Deadfall Snare
Standard Source: Deadfalls and Snares
Materials Needed:
• A sturdy tree or post
• Strong rope or wire
• A trigger mechanism (e.g., a stick or branch)
• A weight (e.g., a stone or log)
• A platform or trap floor

Steps to Construct the Deadfall Snare:
1. Select a Location:...
Materials Needed:
• A sturdy tree or post
• Strong rope or wire
• A trigger mechanism (e.g., a stick or branch)
• A weight (e.g., a stone or log)
• A platform or trap floor

Steps to Construct the Deadfall Snare:
1. Select a Location: Choose an area where animals are likely to pass, such as near trails, watering holes, or feeding grounds.
2. Set Up the Tree or Post: Securely anchor a sturdy tree or post in the ground so that it is vertical and stable.
3. Attach the Rope or Wire: Tie one end of the rope or wire to the top of the tree or post. Run the rope down to the ground, ensuring it is taut but not overly tight.
4. Create a Trigger Mechanism: Use a stick or branch as a trigger. Place it horizontally across the rope at a height that will be activated by an animal stepping on it.
5. Attach the Weight: Tie the other end of the rope to a weight, such as a stone or log. Ensure the weight is heavy enough to pull down the tree or post when triggered.
6. Install the Platform or Trap Floor: Place a platform or trap floor beneath the tree or post so that it will fall onto the animal when activated.
7. Test the Snare: Gently test the trigger mechanism by stepping on it to ensure that the weight pulls down the tree or post and activates the trap.
8. Monitor Regularly: Check the snare regularly for signs of an animal being caught, and be prepared to retrieve the animal promptly to avoid unnecessary suffering.

Important Notes:
• Ensure the trap is placed in a location that minimizes the risk of accidental activation by humans or non-target animals.
• Use caution when handling the trap to avoid injury from the falling tree or post.
• Always follow local laws and regulations regarding trapping and hunting.
deadfall snare, trap construction, hunting techniques, animal trapping, survival skills

Constructing a Baited Snare
Reference Source: Traps
Steps to Construct a Baited Snare:

1. Prepare the Trigger Mechanism: Construct as for a spring snare but use the release mechanism shown.
2. Secure the Bait Support: Ensure the bait support is only lightly driven into the ground so it...
Steps to Construct a Baited Snare:

1. Prepare the Trigger Mechanism: Construct as for a spring snare but use the release mechanism shown.
2. Secure the Bait Support: Ensure the bait support is only lightly driven into the ground so it can fly away with the snare when triggered.
3. Attach Snare to Trigger Bar: Attach the snare to the trigger bar and connect the trigger bar to a sapling.
4. Set Location: Use this trap on game trails or in gaps through rocks or hedges.
5. Check Regularly: Check the trap regularly to ensure it is functioning properly and prevent undue suffering.
baited snare, trap construction, survival
Constructing a Balance Log & Square-Face Release Trap
Reference Source: Traps

Steps to Construct a Balance Log & Square-Face Release Trap:

1. Prepare the Balance Log: Use a forked stick with sharpened ends and one fork suitably baited.
2. Support the Cross-Bar: Support one end of a cross-bar using the balance log, with the other end resting on a fixed support.
3. Use Heavy Weight: Place heavy logs or rocks on the bar to hold it in place.
4. Set Location: Set the trap in areas where it is unlikely to be accidentally triggered by humans.
5. Check Regularly: Check the trap regularly to ensure it is functioning properly and prevent undue suffering.

Steps to Construct a Leg Snare:

1. Prepare the Fork or Sticks: Push a natural fork or two sticks tied together into the ground.
2. Attach Line to Toggle: Tie the line from a sapling to a wooden toggle and pass the toggle under the fork.
3. Bait the Trap: Attach bait to a separate stick and place it in position so that when the game takes the bait, the stick falls away, releasing the toggle.
4. Check Regularly: Check the trap regularly to ensure it is functioning properly and prevent undue suffering.

Constructing a Snare for Small Game
Reference Source: Traps
Steps to Construct a Snare for Small Game:

1. Select Materials: Use strong, springy wood for triggers and string or wire for the snare loop.
2. Cut the Loop: Make a loop with the string or wire that is approximately the width of a...
Steps to Construct a Snare for Small Game:

1. Select Materials: Use strong, springy wood for triggers and string or wire for the snare loop.
2. Cut the Loop: Make a loop with the string or wire that is approximately the width of a fist.
3. Position the Snare: Set the loop four fingers above the ground and one hand’s width from an obstruction on the trail.
4. Secure Anchoring: Ensure the snare is securely anchored, using twigs to support the loop in position if necessary.
5. Check Regularly: Check the snare regularly to prevent escape and undue suffering to the animal.
snare, small game, trap construction, survival

Procedure for Cleaning Waxed or Dipped Foothold Traps:

1. Identify parts to clean: Focus on the end of the dog and the pan notch.
2. Remove wax or dip: Use a file to clean wax or dip off these parts.
3. Ensure proper function: Make sure that the trap will stay set after cleaning.

Important Notes:
• If you do not remove the dip or wax, the trap will not stay set.
• This step is essential for ensuring the effectiveness of the trap.

Trap Tuning Procedures
Standard Source: Student manual 2005
Trap tuning involves several key adjustments to ensure your traps are effective and safe:

### Steps for Trap Tuning:
1. File the trap jaw: Use a metal file to smooth down any sharp or rough edges on the jaws to prevent injury to animals and...
Trap tuning involves several key adjustments to ensure your traps are effective and safe:

### Steps for Trap Tuning:
1. File the trap jaw: Use a metal file to smooth down any sharp or rough edges on the jaws to prevent injury to animals and improve performance.
2. Level trap pans: When the trap is set, the pan should be level with the jaws. If it's too high or low, bend the frame in or out below the 'dog'. Bend it out to raise the pan; bend it in to lower the pan.
3. Adjust pan tension: This is measured by the amount of weight needed on the trap pan to fire the trap. Most new traps have pan tension adjustment screws:
- Increase tension by turning the screw clockwise (to the right).
- Decrease tension by turning the screw counterclockwise (to the left).
- Example: Two pounds of pan tension is a good setting for fox.
- You can use a commercial testing device or create a makeshift one using a tennis ball can, liquid soap container, or PVC pipe filled with sand to match your desired weight.
4. Adjust pan throw: This refers to the distance the pan must be depressed to fire the trap:
- Use a shorter pan throw when tuning for high pan tension.
- To adjust, file some metal off the end of the notch where the dog fits. The shape of this notch determines how far the pan must drop before firing.
5. Inspect and maintain components:
- Replace weak springs immediately.
- Ensure swivels and hooks are strong and operate freely without binding.
- Weld trap hooks if trapping large animals like coyotes.
- Check chains for worn links and replace as needed.
6. Comply with regulations: Make sure your traps are tagged to meet state requirements.

### Additional Tips:
• The dog or notch should be squared off, not rounded, to prevent the trap from releasing too easily.
• For body-gripping traps, adjust the trigger if there is excessive play by crimping it with pliers or a vise until the ends are flush.
• Experienced trappers may shape triggers for selective trapping of certain species (see 'Selective Trapping Techniques' in Chapter 6).

Note: Always inspect and tune your traps before the start of each season to ensure they function properly and safely.
trap tuning, trap maintenance, pan tension, pan throw, trap jaw filing, trap inspection, trigger adjustment, hunting safety

Cleaning New Traps Before Use
Reference Source: Student manual 2005
Purpose: Removing the light oil coating from new traps to prepare them for rusting, dyeing, and waxing.

Procedure:
1. Insert a nail between the jaws of each trap to open them slightly.
2. Place all traps in a large wash tub.
3. Fill the tub...
Purpose: Removing the light oil coating from new traps to prepare them for rusting, dyeing, and waxing.

Procedure:
1. Insert a nail between the jaws of each trap to open them slightly.
2. Place all traps in a large wash tub.
3. Fill the tub with water and powdered automatic dishwasher detergent.
4. Boil the traps in the soapy water for 30 minutes.
5. Remove the traps from the water and rinse them thoroughly until they are clean.
6. Hang the traps outside to allow a light coating of rust to form on the metal. This process may take 1 to 2 weeks.
cleaning traps, trap maintenance, removing oil from traps
Dyeing Traps Using Logwood Powder or Crystals
Reference Source: Student manual 2005
Purpose: Coloring traps for camouflage and protection.

Procedure:
1. Once the traps are lightly rusted, place them in a large washtub over a fire or propane cooker.
2. If using commercial logwood dye, follow the package instructions for...
Purpose: Coloring traps for camouflage and protection.

Procedure:
1. Once the traps are lightly rusted, place them in a large washtub over a fire or propane cooker.
2. If using commercial logwood dye, follow the package instructions for mixing water and logwood ingredients.
3. Bring the solution to a boil, then add the traps.
4. Let the traps soak in the solution for 30 to 60 minutes. The longer they soak, the darker the color will be.
5. If the traps are heavily rusted, remove the washtub from heat and let the traps soak for 1 or 2 days.

Important Note: High temperatures can weaken trap springs, so it is best to lower the water temperature to a simmer after adding the traps. A propane cooker allows easier control of heat compared to an open fire.
dyeing traps, logwood dye, trap coloring

Using Petroleum-Based Dips for Coloring and Protecting Traps
Reference Source: Student manual 2005
Purpose: Providing a fast and simple method to color and protect traps without using heat.

Procedure:
1. Ensure the traps are pre-rusted before applying this method.
2. Follow the instructions on the petroleum-based dip product for...
Purpose: Providing a fast and simple method to color and protect traps without using heat.

Procedure:
1. Ensure the traps are pre-rusted before applying this method.
2. Follow the instructions on the petroleum-based dip product for mixing with unleaded gas or lantern fuel.
3. Dip the pre-rusted traps into the solution, making sure not to dip padded jaw traps (avoid submerging the pads).
4. Hang the traps outside in a well-ventilated area to dry.

Note: Petroleum-based dips provide a harder and more even coating, especially when used with proper air temperature conditions.

Waxing Traps Using Boiling Water and Wax
Reference Source: Student manual 2005
Procedure for Waxing Traps Using Boiling Water and Wax:

1. Prepare the wax: Submerge traps in boiling water. Place trap wax in the water and let it melt. The wax will float.
2. Apply wax to traps: Using a hooked stick, slowly and...
Procedure for Waxing Traps Using Boiling Water and Wax:

1. Prepare the wax: Submerge traps in boiling water. Place trap wax in the water and let it melt. The wax will float.
2. Apply wax to traps: Using a hooked stick, slowly and carefully lift the traps out of the water one at a time. The traps should come out with a thin, even coat of wax.
3. Remove excess wax: Shake the traps to remove excess wax.
4. Adjust if necessary: If the wax is too thick or too heavy, put the traps back in the water and allow them to heat a bit longer before removing them.

Important Safety Notes:
• Trap wax is flammable. Wax can catch fire, or cause severe burns if it splashes on you.
• Always read and follow manufacturer’s directions.
• Ensure that the wax and container are free of odors, as furbearers have a keen sense of smell.
waxing traps, trap maintenance, boiling water, wax application
Why Bodygripping Traps Should Not Be Waxed
Reference Source: Student manual 2005
Purpose: Avoiding potential damage to bodygripping traps.

Explanation:
• Waxing bodygripping traps is not recommended because it can interfere with the function of the trap’s mechanism, potentially causing malfunctions or reducing...
Purpose: Avoiding potential damage to bodygripping traps.

Explanation:
• Waxing bodygripping traps is not recommended because it can interfere with the function of the trap’s mechanism, potentially causing malfunctions or reducing effectiveness.
• It may also affect how the trap interacts with the target animal, increasing the risk of injury to both the animal and the trapper.

Recommendation:
• Use alternative methods such as dyeing or petroleum-based dips for coloring and protection instead of wax.
waxing traps, bodygripping trap safety

Constructing a Fish Trap in Shallow Water or on Rocky/Coral Shores
Standard Source: Animals For Food
Materials Needed:
• Stones or natural materials
• Natural rock pools (for rocky shores)
• Coral reefs (for coral islands)
• Sandbars and ditches (for sandy shores)

Steps to Construct the Fish Trap:
1. Choose Location: Select a location...
Materials Needed:
• Stones or natural materials
• Natural rock pools (for rocky shores)
• Coral reefs (for coral islands)
• Sandbars and ditches (for sandy shores)

Steps to Construct the Fish Trap:
1. Choose Location: Select a location at high tide for building the trap, and build it at low tide.
2. Use Natural Features: On rocky shores, use natural rock pools. On coral islands, use natural pools on the surface of reefs by blocking the openings as the tide recedes. On sandy shores, use sandbars and the ditches they enclose.
3. Build Trap Structure: Build the trap as a low stone wall extending outward into the water and forming an angle with the shore.
fish trap, survival, fishing, natural features
Constructing a Gill Net from Parachute Suspension Line
Standard Source: Animals For Food
Materials Needed:
• Parachute suspension line or similar material
• Trees for tying the net
• Prusik knots or girth hitches
• Guidelines (optional)

Steps to Construct the Gill Net:
1. Remove Core Lines: Remove the core lines from the...
Materials Needed:
• Parachute suspension line or similar material
• Trees for tying the net
• Prusik knots or girth hitches
• Guidelines (optional)

Steps to Construct the Gill Net:
1. Remove Core Lines: Remove the core lines from the parachute suspension line.
2. Tie Easing Between Trees: Tie the easing between two trees.
3. Attach Core Lines: Attach several core lines to the easing by doubling them over and tying them with prusik knots or girth hitches.
4. Determine Mesh Size: The length of the desired net and the size of the mesh determine the number of core lines used and the space between them.
5. Tie Core Lines in Pairs: Starting at one end of the easing, tie the second and third core lines together using an overhand knot. Continue this pattern (fourth and fifth, sixth and seventh, etc.) until you reach the last core line. You should now have all core lines tied in pairs with a single core line hanging at each end.
6. Start Second Row: Start the second row with the first core line, tie it to the second, the third to the fourth, and so on.
7. Regulate Mesh Size: To keep the rows even and regulate mesh size, tie a guideline to the trees on the opposite side of the net you are working on. Move the guideline down after completing each row.
8. Continue Tying Rows: Continue tying rows until the net is the desired width.
9. Strengthen Net: Thread a suspension line easing along the bottom of the net to strengthen it.
10. Use the Gill Net: Use the gill net as shown in Figure 8-20.
gill net, fishing, survival, trap making, parachute suspension line
Making and Using a Spear for Fishing
Standard Source: Animals For Food
Materials Needed:
• Long, straight sapling
• Knife or sharpened metal (optional)
• Jagged piece of bone or sharpened wood (optional)

Steps to Make the Spear:
1. Cut Sapling: Cut a long, straight sapling.
2. Sharpen Tip: Sharpen the...
Materials Needed:
• Long, straight sapling
• Knife or sharpened metal (optional)
• Jagged piece of bone or sharpened wood (optional)

Steps to Make the Spear:
1. Cut Sapling: Cut a long, straight sapling.
2. Sharpen Tip: Sharpen the end to a point or attach a knife, jagged piece of bone, or sharpened metal.
3. Optional Spreader: Split the shaft a few inches down from the end and insert a piece of wood to act as a spreader. Then sharpen the two separated halves to points.

Steps to Use the Spear:
1. Find Fish Gathering Area: Find an area where fish either gather or where there is a fish run.
2. Insert Spear Point into Water: Place the spear point into the water and slowly move it toward the fish.
3. Impale Fish: With a sudden push, impale the fish on the stream bottom.
4. Hold Fish Securely: Hold the spear with one hand and grab and hold the fish with the other. Do not try to lift the fish with the spear as it will probably slip off and you will lose it.
5. Avoid Throwing Spear: Do not throw the spear, especially if the point is a knife. You cannot afford to lose a knife in a survival situation.
6. Be Alert to Refraction: Be alert to the problems caused by light refraction when looking at objects in the water.
spear fishing, survival, fishing tools

Materials Needed:
• Light source (e.g., flashlight or fire)
• Machete or similar weapon

Steps for Chop Fishing:
1. Choose Location: Use an area with a good fish density.
2. Attract Fish with Light: At night, use a light to attract fish to the area.
3. Strike Fish with Back of Blade: Armed with a machete or similar weapon, gather fish using the back side of the blade to strike them.
4. Avoid Sharp Side: Do not use the sharp side as you will cut them in two pieces and end up losing some of the fish.
chop fishing, survival, night fishing

Materials Needed:
• Lime (commercial or homemade by burning coral or seashells)
• Green husks from butternuts or black walnuts

Steps to Use Fish Poison:
1. Use Lime: You can get lime from commercial sources and in agricultural areas that use large quantities of it. You may produce your own by burning coral or seashells. Throw the lime into the water.
2. Use Nut Husks: Crush green husks from butternuts or black walnuts. Throw the husks into the water.

1. Sharpen the Stick: Sharpen a long stick to make a spear.
2. Add Barbs: Add barbs to the spear to make it more effective at catching fish.

Fishing with a Hook and Line
Reference Source: Traps
Steps to Fish with a Hook and Line:

1. Improvise Hooks: Use wire, pins, bones, wood, or thorns to make hooks.
2. Bait the Hook: Use bait native to the fish’s own water, such as berries, insects, parts of a fish, ants, worms, grubs, and...
Steps to Fish with a Hook and Line:

1. Improvise Hooks: Use wire, pins, bones, wood, or thorns to make hooks.
2. Bait the Hook: Use bait native to the fish’s own water, such as berries, insects, parts of a fish, ants, worms, grubs, and other insects.
3. Check Fish Stomach Contents: Examine the stomach contents of a caught fish to determine what it has been feeding on.
4. Use Flies: Add tied flies to your kit for additional bait options.
5. Leave Lines Out Overnight: Leave lines out overnight and check them before first light.

Turkey Hunting (9) -

Select and Use Legal Hunting Equipment for Turkey Hunting in Washington
Standard Source: wdfw01803
Procedure:

1. Ensure your hunting equipment is legal under Washington State regulations.
2. Choose from the following legal turkey hunting equipment:
- Shotgun (with a full or extra full choke)
- Muzzleloading shotgun
-...
Procedure:

1. Ensure your hunting equipment is legal under Washington State regulations.
2. Choose from the following legal turkey hunting equipment:
- Shotgun (with a full or extra full choke)
- Muzzleloading shotgun
- Archery equipment
- Crossbow
3. If using a shotgun, ensure it is capable of holding three or fewer shells.
4. If your shotgun can hold more than three shells, confirm that the manufacturer has provided a magazine plug to limit capacity to three rounds.
5. Ensure your shotgun is equipped with an interchangeable choke tube if possible, allowing you to adjust for different hunting conditions.
6. If your shotgun does not have interchangeable chokes, use turkey-specific ammunition loads designed for less tight chokes.
7. Use #4 shot or smaller, such as #6 shot, when hunting turkeys.

Additional Tips:
• The larger the number of shot (e.g., #6), the smaller the pellets.
• Always check your equipment before heading out to ensure compliance with local regulations.
legal hunting equipment, turkey hunting, shotgun regulations, choke tubes, shot size
Shot Placement for Harvesting Turkeys
Standard Source: wdfw01803
To ensure humane and effective shot placement when harvesting turkeys:

With a Shotgun:
1. Use a head/neck shot as the preferred method to avoid meat loss.
2. Aim for the middle of the neck, where the feathers start to appear.
3. Ensure...
To ensure humane and effective shot placement when harvesting turkeys:

With a Shotgun:
1. Use a head/neck shot as the preferred method to avoid meat loss.
2. Aim for the middle of the neck, where the feathers start to appear.
3. Ensure there are no obstructions (e.g., branches or twigs) between you and the turkey that could affect the projectile’s trajectory.
4. Practice with your hunting equipment before the hunt to build confidence in your shot placement.

With Archery/Crossbow:
1. If using guillotine-style broadheads (designed to decapitate the bird), aim as you would with a shotgun, targeting the middle of the neck.
2. For regular broadheads, use these target areas based on the turkey’s position:
- Broadside: Aim for the vitals just below the wing joint.
- Strutting and facing away: Aim for the base of the tail.
- Facing head-on: Aim just above the beard.
3. Ensure there are no obstructions (e.g., branches or twigs) between you and the turkey that could affect the projectile’s trajectory.
4. Practice with your equipment before the hunt to build confidence in your shot placement.
shot placement, turkey hunting, shotgun, archery, crossbow, humane harvest
Turkey Hunting Techniques
Standard Source: wdfw01803
Best time to hunt:
• Spring: When male turkeys (toms) are displaying to female turkeys (hens) in a bid to become their mate.
• Fall: When turkeys are gathering for the upcoming winter months and generally feeding and moving throughout...
Best time to hunt:
• Spring: When male turkeys (toms) are displaying to female turkeys (hens) in a bid to become their mate.
• Fall: When turkeys are gathering for the upcoming winter months and generally feeding and moving throughout the day.

Key strategies:
1. Use locator calls to determine if there are toms or jakes (immature tom) in an area during spring.
2. Toms will often respond with a shock gobble to any loud, sudden noise during spring.
3. Once the season opens, use locator calls or hen yelps to locate a tom during spring.
4. Set up your blind or find a location to easily conceal yourself in areas with food sources like meadows or small openings during both seasons.
5. Sit with your back to a tree, stump, or hillside for protection and camouflage during spring.
6. Place a decoy about 20 yards from where you will be sitting if using one during spring.
7. Use turkey calls such as hen yelps, clucks, and purs to draw in toms during spring.
8. During fall, look for travel corridors and feeding areas used by turkeys.
9. Set up an ambush point on travel routes between roost trees and feeding areas during fall.
10. Set up similar to spring hunting techniques, but focus on flocks of hens and their offspring or small groups of toms during fall.
11. Use soft hen calls and juvenile turkey ‘kee kees’ to coax hens closer to your ambush point during fall.
12. Listen to the birds and imitate the calls they are making during fall.

Harvest limits:
• During spring, you can harvest up to 3 turkeys.
• During general fall seasons, you can harvest up to 4 turkeys.
• An additional bird may be harvested if you have a fall special permit.
turkey hunting, spring turkey hunting, fall turkey hunting, locator calls, hen yelps, decoy placement, harvest limits
Using Turkey Decoys Safely
Standard Source: wdfw01803
Procedure:

1. Choose the appropriate decoy: Select a hen, jake, or tom decoy based on your hunting strategy.
2. Position the decoy safely: Set the decoy at least 20 yards away from your blind and ensure there is a minimum 50-yard line...
Procedure:

1. Choose the appropriate decoy: Select a hen, jake, or tom decoy based on your hunting strategy.
2. Position the decoy safely: Set the decoy at least 20 yards away from your blind and ensure there is a minimum 50-yard line of sight between you and any other hunters.
3. Avoid carrying uncovered decoys: Always cover or store decoys when not in use to prevent accidental exposure to turkeys or other hunters.
4. Be aware of surroundings: Look before moving and call out to approaching hunters instead of waving your hand.

Notes:
• Decoys can attract, distract, challenge, or instill confidence in turkeys.
• Use decoys strategically to increase your chances of a successful hunt.
turkey decoy, hunting safety, turkey calling

Types of Calls:
• Friction calls (e.g., box calls, diaphragm calls)
• Air-operated calls (e.g., mouth calls, slate calls)

Purpose:
• Most calls are used to simulate the calls of a hen turkey.
• A few can mimic the gobbling of a mature tom but should be used sparingly to avoid attracting other hunters.

Tips for Using Calls:
1. Practice calling regularly to improve your technique and timing.
2. Read the manufacturer’s instructions carefully before using any call.
3. Use calls in conjunction with natural turkey sounds for better results.
4. Be mindful of other hunters when using tom gobble calls.

Locate and Identify Roosting Areas for Turkeys
Reference Source: wdfw01803
Procedure:

1. Look for trees that are at least 30-100 feet tall during the summer months.
2. During winter, identify largest trees in a grove, as turkeys prefer these for roosting.
3. Observe if turkeys tend to roost near the **top of...
Procedure:

1. Look for trees that are at least 30-100 feet tall during the summer months.
2. During winter, identify largest trees in a grove, as turkeys prefer these for roosting.
3. Observe if turkeys tend to roost near the top of the tree as high as comfortably possible.
4. Avoid hunting at the roost site directly, as this may disrupt normal turkey behavior.
5. Set up your hunting position 70 or more yards away from a roost site.
6. Use calls such as locator calls, soft clucks, and yelps to attract turkeys to your location.

Additional Tips:
• Roosting areas are often used repeatedly, especially in winter.
• If you find many droppings below a tree, it may be a roost site.
roosting areas, turkey hunting, tree identification, winter roosts, summer roosts

Optics Designation:
• The designation on optics (e.g., 8 x 42) indicates the magnification and objective lens size.
- The first number (8) is the level of magnification.
- The second number (42) is the diameter of the objective lens in millimeters.

Choosing Optics:
• A larger objective lens allows more light to enter, resulting in a clearer image but may make the optics bulkier.
• Consider testing different models at a sporting goods store before purchasing.

Usefulness for Turkey Hunting:
• While not essential, binoculars or spotting scopes can be helpful for locating turkeys, observing their movements, and watching them go to their roost.
binoculars, spotting scope, optics, turkey hunting

Identify Turkey Droppings for Gender Determination
Reference Source: wdfw01803
Procedure:

1. Examine turkey droppings found in the area.
2. Compare the shape and size of the droppings to known examples:
- Hen droppings are usually a single pile.
- Tom droppings are elongated, forming a “J” shape and are...
Procedure:

1. Examine turkey droppings found in the area.
2. Compare the shape and size of the droppings to known examples:
- Hen droppings are usually a single pile.
- Tom droppings are elongated, forming a “J” shape and are approximately two inches long.
3. Note the color variation between hen and tom droppings if present.
4. Use this information to determine the gender of turkeys in the area.

Additional Tips:
• Finding multiple droppings may indicate that turkeys frequently use the area.
• If you find a large number of droppings below a tree, it could be a roosting site.
• Take note of the location for future reference and hunting strategy.
turkey droppings, gender determination, hen droppings, tom droppings, hunting signs

Definition: A hole or depression dug in the ground where a trap is placed.

Action Steps:
1. Choose a suitable location for the trap bed, ensuring it is concealed and safe from disturbance.
2. Dig a hole or create a depression in the ground large enough to accommodate the trap.
3. Ensure the trap bed is level and stable to prevent tipping or displacement of the trap.
4. Place the trap inside the trap bed according to manufacturer instructions.
5. Cover the trap bed with natural materials (e.g., leaves, dirt) for camouflage.

Construct and Use Traps & Snare for Hunting
Standard Source: USMC Winter Survival Course Handbook
Objective: Construct and use traps or snares to catch small game in survival situations.

Steps:
1. Choose the appropriate trap or snare based on the type of animal you are targeting (e.g., a loop snare for small mammals, a pit trap for...
Objective: Construct and use traps or snares to catch small game in survival situations.

Steps:
1. Choose the appropriate trap or snare based on the type of animal you are targeting (e.g., a loop snare for small mammals, a pit trap for larger animals).
2. Select a suitable location that is likely to be frequented by the target animal (e.g., near food sources, along trails, in dense vegetation).
3. Prepare the ground or structure of the trap by digging a hole or placing logs and branches to create a stable base.
4. Construct the trap using materials such as rope, wire, or natural fibers to ensure it is secure and functional.
5. Adjust the loop size and ground clearance to match the target animal’s size and movement patterns.
6. Use bait (e.g., food scraps, berries) to attract the animal to the trap.
7. Ensure that the trap is concealed from view but still accessible for checking and maintenance.
8. Check the trap regularly to ensure it is functioning properly and to avoid unnecessary suffering to the trapped animal.

Notes: Traps and snares are effective tools for obtaining food in survival situations, but they must be used responsibly and ethically.
traps snare construction, hunting tool, survival food

Cable Devices (9) -

Attach Cable Devices to Anchoring Systems
Standard Source: Student manual 2005
Cable devices should be attached to steel stakes or earth anchors for secure anchoring. An alternative is to pass a heavy gauge wire through a swivel on the end of the cable and make a loop around a tree.

**Steps for Attaching Cable Devices to...
Cable devices should be attached to steel stakes or earth anchors for secure anchoring. An alternative is to pass a heavy gauge wire through a swivel on the end of the cable and make a loop around a tree.

Steps for Attaching Cable Devices to Anchoring Systems:
1. Choose an appropriate anchoring system (e.g., steel stake, earth anchor, or tree loop).
2. Attach the cable device to the anchoring point using a swivel or other secure method.
3. Ensure that the anchoring system is strong enough to hold an animal that can pull against it using all four legs.
4. Test the anchoring system by applying pressure to ensure it is secure and does not fail under stress.

Notes:
• Anchoring systems are essential for ensuring the safety of both the trapper and the animal being trapped.
• Always follow local trapping regulations and guidelines for the proper use of anchoring systems.
anchoring systems,cable devices,trapping,hunting,animal welfare
Create and Use Stops on Cable Loops
Standard Source: Student manual 2005
Trapping regulations often require the use of a “stop” to prevent a cable loop from closing below a certain diameter. Heavy gauge wire, steel nuts, or crimped ferrules can be used to make stops.

Steps for Creating a Stop:
1. Select a material...
Trapping regulations often require the use of a “stop” to prevent a cable loop from closing below a certain diameter. Heavy gauge wire, steel nuts, or crimped ferrules can be used to make stops.

Steps for Creating a Stop:
1. Select a material suitable for making a stop (e.g., heavy gauge wire, steel nut, or crimped ferrule).
2. Attach the stop to the cable loop at a point where it will prevent the loop from closing below the required diameter.
3. Ensure that the stop is securely fastened and positioned so that it does not interfere with the function of the trap or cable loop.

Types of Stops:
• Maximum Loop Stop: Prevents larger animals from entering the device.
• Minimum Loop Stop: Prevents the device from closing around a non-target animal’s foot (e.g., if a deer steps in the cable loop, the minimum loop stop keeps the cable from closing tight enough to hold it).

Notes:
• Stops are essential for compliance with trapping regulations and for animal welfare.
• Always follow local trapping regulations and guidelines for the proper use of stops.
stops,cable loops,trapping,animal welfare,hunting
Attach Swivels to Cable Anchoring Systems
Standard Source: Student manual 2005
Swivels are used in cable device anchoring systems to keep the animal from twisting and kinking the cable.

Steps for Attaching a Swivel:
1. Choose an appropriate swivel that is compatible with the cable system being used.
2. Attach the swivel...
Swivels are used in cable device anchoring systems to keep the animal from twisting and kinking the cable.

Steps for Attaching a Swivel:
1. Choose an appropriate swivel that is compatible with the cable system being used.
2. Attach the swivel to the end of the cable or to the anchoring point (e.g., steel stake, earth anchor, or tree loop).
3. Ensure that the swivel is securely fastened and positioned so that it allows for free movement without twisting or kinking the cable.
4. Test the swivel by applying pressure to ensure it moves freely and does not interfere with the function of the trap or cable system.

Notes:
• Swivels are essential for preventing damage to the cable and ensuring the proper functioning of the trap.
• Always follow local trapping regulations and guidelines for the proper use of swivels.
swivels,cable anchoring,trapping,hunting,animal welfare
Identify Cable Device Equipment and Materials
Standard Source: Student manual 2005
### Identify Cable Device Equipment and Materials

• Multi-strand steel cable - Used in modern cable devices.
• 7x7 Cable and 7x19 Cable are common types of stranded steel cables used in cable devices.
• Relaxing Lock (A) - A...
### Identify Cable Device Equipment and Materials

• Multi-strand steel cable - Used in modern cable devices.
• 7x7 Cable and 7x19 Cable are common types of stranded steel cables used in cable devices.
• Relaxing Lock (A) - A component that allows the cable to move in either direction. It tightens when an animal pulls against it and relaxes when tension stops.
• Non-Relaxing Lock (B) - Keeps a cable from loosening after an animal is caught. It closes the loop tighter when pulled but does not relax when tension stops.
• J-Hook Breakaway Device (C) - A component that allows for controlled release of an animal.
• End Ferrule (D) - Used to secure the end of the cable.
• Maximum Loop Stop (F) - Prevents the loop from becoming too large, ensuring proper function and safety.
• Inline Swivel (G) - Allows the cable device to rotate freely, reducing twisting and potential damage.
• End Swivel (H) - Similar to inline swivel but located at the end of the cable device.
• Trap Tag (I) - A tag used for identification purposes on the trap or cable device.
• Stabilizer Tube (E) - Helps maintain the shape and integrity of the cable device during use.
• Deer Stop (J) - Designed to prevent larger animals, such as deer, from being caught in a non-relaxing lock by breaking at a given strength.
cable devices,equipment identification,trapping materials,hunting tools
Install Breakaway Devices on Cable Systems
Standard Source: Student manual 2005
Breakaway devices are parts of a cable system that allow an animal to escape from the loop if it pulls against it with sufficient force. Ferrules, S-hooks, and J-hooks are examples of breakaway devices.

Steps for Installation:
1. Choose the...
Breakaway devices are parts of a cable system that allow an animal to escape from the loop if it pulls against it with sufficient force. Ferrules, S-hooks, and J-hooks are examples of breakaway devices.

Steps for Installation:
1. Choose the appropriate breakaway device based on the type of animal being trapped (e.g., deer, livestock).
2. Attach the breakaway device to the cable system at a point where it will allow the animal to escape if sufficient force is applied.
3. Ensure that the breakaway device is securely fastened and positioned so that it does not interfere with the function of the trap or cable loop.
4. Test the breakaway device by applying pressure to ensure it releases properly when needed.

Notes:
• Breakaway devices are essential for animal welfare, as they prevent injury or entanglement if an animal is unable to escape on its own.
• Always follow local trapping regulations and guidelines for the proper use of breakaway devices.
breakaway,devices,cable system,trapping,animal welfare,hunting
Install Cable/End Ferrules on Cable Systems
Standard Source: Student manual 2005
An end ferrule, also called a cable end, is crimped on the end of a cable to keep the strands from unraveling. A ferrule can also serve as a breakaway device.

Steps for Installing an End Ferrule:
1. Choose an appropriate ferrule that is...
An end ferrule, also called a cable end, is crimped on the end of a cable to keep the strands from unraveling. A ferrule can also serve as a breakaway device.

Steps for Installing an End Ferrule:
1. Choose an appropriate ferrule that is compatible with the cable being used.
2. Slide the ferrule over the end of the cable and crimp it securely to prevent the strands from unraveling.
3. If using the ferrule as a breakaway device, ensure it is positioned so that it will release properly when sufficient force is applied.
4. Test the ferrule by applying pressure to ensure it functions correctly and does not interfere with the function of the trap or cable system.

Notes:
• Ferrules are essential for maintaining the integrity of the cable and preventing damage to the trap.
• Always follow local trapping regulations and guidelines for the proper use of ferrules.
ferrules,cable ends,trapping,hunting,animal welfare
Set a Cable Device
Standard Source: Student manual 2005
### Set a Cable Device

1. Select the location - Choose a spot where animals frequently travel, such as narrow trails or paths.
2. Position the device - Place it in the center of the line of travel so that the targeted furbearer will walk...
### Set a Cable Device

1. Select the location - Choose a spot where animals frequently travel, such as narrow trails or paths.
2. Position the device - Place it in the center of the line of travel so that the targeted furbearer will walk into it.
3. Use appropriate cable size - Follow state trapping regulations for required cable sizes (e.g., 3/32” is a popular choice).
4. Secure the components - Ensure all parts, such as swivels, ferrules, or mechanical features like springs and foothold-like pans, are properly attached to prevent twisting, damage, or malfunction.
5. Use a relaxing lock (if applicable) - If using a non-powered cable device with a relaxing lock, ensure it is set correctly so that animals can be released unharmed if they do not pull against the device.
6. Ensure proper loop closure - The device should use its mechanical feature to place and tighten the cable around the captured animal’s body or limb (for powered devices) or ensure the loop is properly sized and positioned for safe capture (for non-powered devices).
7. Check for proper function - Ensure that all components are working correctly, and the loop of the cable device is properly sized and positioned to capture the targeted furbearer without causing unnecessary harm.
cable device, trapping, non-powered, powered, animal control, furbearer

Standard Source: Student manual 2005
### Use a Non-Relaxing Lock in Cable Devices

1. Attach the non-relaxing lock - Ensure it is properly connected to the cable device.
2. Position the loop correctly - Place the loop so that it will close around the targeted furbearer’s body...
### Use a Non-Relaxing Lock in Cable Devices

1. Attach the non-relaxing lock - Ensure it is properly connected to the cable device.
2. Position the loop correctly - Place the loop so that it will close around the targeted furbearer’s body or limb when they move forward.
3. Ensure secure closure - The non-relaxing lock will keep the cable from loosening after an animal is caught, ensuring the device remains closed and functional.
4. Use for long-term trapping - Non-relaxing locks are suitable for use in situations where the trap may remain set for extended periods without requiring frequent checks.
5. Check for proper function - Ensure that the non-relaxing lock is working correctly and that the loop of the cable device is properly sized and positioned to capture the targeted furbearer without causing unnecessary harm.
non-relaxing lock,trapping techniques,hunting setup

Fur Processing (8) -

Proper Fur Handling and Preservation Techniques
Standard Source: Student manual 2005
Procedure:

1. Remove the furbearer from water traps:
- Rinse the animal clean of any dirt, mud, or vegetation.
- Shake off excess water from the animal.
- Stroke the fur from head to tail with your hand to remove as much water as...
Procedure:

1. Remove the furbearer from water traps:
- Rinse the animal clean of any dirt, mud, or vegetation.
- Shake off excess water from the animal.
- Stroke the fur from head to tail with your hand to remove as much water as possible.
- If dry snow is available, roll the animal in it to absorb water.

2. Avoid freezing surfaces if below freezing:
- Do not lay a wet animal on ice or metal surfaces, as this can cause guard hairs to freeze and damage the pelt when removed.

3. Dry the fur before skinning:
- If the fur is still wet when you get home, hang it up by the head or forelegs in a cool place to dry.
- Use a fan to circulate air and decrease drying time.
- Ensure pelts are completely dry before being skinned and placed on a stretching frame.

4. Sell unskinned furs if necessary:
- In warm weather (above 40°F), sell unskinned animals daily.
- In cooler weather (below 40°F), sell every two to three days.
- Selling unskinned furs will result in a lower price, but it is less work for you and more work for the buyer.

5. Handle land-trapped furbearers carefully:
- If an animal is trapped on land and already dry, brush or comb the pelt to remove burrs or dirt.
- Land furbearers may carry external parasites (e.g., fleas, ticks, mites), so keep carcasses in a place that won’t contaminate your house, clothing, or vehicle.
fur handling,pelt preservation,drying furs,selling unskinned furs,fur processing
Understanding Cased and Open Furs in Fur Preparation
Standard Source: Student manual 2005
Procedure:

1. Cased furs:
- This method is used for most furbearers (except beaver and sometimes badger).
- The process involves making a cut from the top of one foot pad along the inside of one back leg to the top of the foot pad on...
Procedure:

1. Cased furs:
- This method is used for most furbearers (except beaver and sometimes badger).
- The process involves making a cut from the top of one foot pad along the inside of one back leg to the top of the foot pad on the other back leg.
- Remove the pelt by turning it inside out, similar to removing a sweater or sweatshirt, pulling it over the head and down over the legs and body.

2. Open furs:
- This method is used for beaver and sometimes badger.
- Make a cut on the underside of the animal from its chin to the base of its tail.
- Remove the fur this way, similar to taking off a coat.
cased furs,open furs,fur preparation,skinning techniques
Explain Why Tails of Some Furbearers Are Split and Left on the Pelt While Others Are Removed
Reference Source: Student manual 2005
Furbearers with furred tails should have their tails split from the underside with a knife, and the tail bone removed. A tail-stripper can be used for this purpose. The **de-boned, furred tail should remain attached to the...
Furbearers with furred tails should have their tails split from the underside with a knife, and the tail bone removed. A tail-stripper can be used for this purpose. The de-boned, furred tail should remain attached to the pelt.

Furbearers without furred tails should have their tails cut from the pelt at the hairline during skinning, and then discarded.
tail processing, furbearer tail removal, fur preparation, tail-stripper, pelt handling
Proper Use of Fleshing Board and Fleshing Tools
Reference Source: Student manual 2005
Purpose: A fleshing board is a narrow wooden or fiberglass beam used to hold a pelt while removing meat or fat still attached to the skin. If not removed, this meat or fat can rot and spoil the pelt.

Tools: Use a **double-handled draw...
Purpose: A fleshing board is a narrow wooden or fiberglass beam used to hold a pelt while removing meat or fat still attached to the skin. If not removed, this meat or fat can rot and spoil the pelt.

Tools: Use a double-handled draw knife, a single-handled scraper, or other types of fleshing tools for this process.
fleshing board, fleshing tools, pelt preparation, fur processing, fat removal
Proper Use of Wire and Wooden Stretchers
Reference Source: Student manual 2005
Purpose: A wire or wooden stretcher is used to hold a pelt in place as it dries so that it does not shrink or shrivel.

Procedure:
1. Place the pelt over a stretching board or wire frame, with the fur-side in.
2. Ensure the pelt...
Purpose: A wire or wooden stretcher is used to hold a pelt in place as it dries so that it does not shrink or shrivel.

Procedure:
1. Place the pelt over a stretching board or wire frame, with the fur-side in.
2. Ensure the pelt is centered on the board or frame, with the forelegs and belly on one side and the eye holes, ears, and back on the other side.
3. Pull the pelt snug but not too tight to avoid damage.
4. If using a wooden stretching board, secure the pelt in place with tacks or push pins near the base of the tail and back legs.
5. For wire frames, use the two metal arms with prongs that hold the base of the pelt taut.
wire stretcher, wooden stretcher, fur drying, pelt preparation, stretching board
Understand the Process of Tanning in Fur Processing
Reference Source: Student manual 2005
Tanning refers to treating a hide to make it into leather.

• This process involves:
- Soaking the hide in chemicals or natural substances (such as tannins).
- Removing hair and fat from the hide.
- Preserving the hide to prevent decay.
-...
Tanning refers to treating a hide to make it into leather.

• This process involves:
- Soaking the hide in chemicals or natural substances (such as tannins).
- Removing hair and fat from the hide.
- Preserving the hide to prevent decay.
• Types of tanning include:
- Vegetable tanning using plant-based materials.
- Chrome tanning using chromium salts.
- Brain tanning using animal brains or oils.
• Tanning is essential for producing durable and long-lasting leather products.
tanning process, fur processing, leather production, hide treatment
Explain the terms 'market fur in' and 'market fur out'
Reference Source: Student manual 2005
Market Fur In means that the fur side of the pelt is on the inside when the pelt is sold. Market Fur Out means that the fur should be on the outside of the pelt, with the skin on the inside. The preparation method depends on the...
Market Fur In means that the fur side of the pelt is on the inside when the pelt is sold. Market Fur Out means that the fur should be on the outside of the pelt, with the skin on the inside. The preparation method depends on the species of furbearer, and it is important to consult your local fur buyer for their specific requirements.

Most fur buyers are willing to explain proper handling techniques since this ensures better quality and higher profits for both parties. Do not hesitate to ask for guidance.
market fur in, market fur out, fur processing, pelt preparation, furbearer species
Recognize and Avoid Singed Fur in Otter Pelts
Reference Source: Student manual 2005
Singed fur refers to the metallic sheen on otter fur caused by curled tips of the guard hairs.

• This damage can occur from:
- Excessive dry heat.
- Direct sunlight.
- Stroking dry fur.
- Contact with freezing metal.
- The otter...
Singed fur refers to the metallic sheen on otter fur caused by curled tips of the guard hairs.

• This damage can occur from:
- Excessive dry heat.
- Direct sunlight.
- Stroking dry fur.
- Contact with freezing metal.
- The otter itself during the late season.
• To avoid singed fur:
- Handle pelts carefully to prevent exposure to extreme temperatures.
- Store pelts in a cool, dry place away from direct sunlight.
- Avoid using metal tools that may cause damage when handling fur.
singed fur, otter pelt processing, fur care, wildlife harvesting

Scout Hunting Areas for Success
Standard Source: Basics of Waterfowling
Objective: Improve hunting success by scouting potential areas.

Procedure:
1. Scout the area before hunting to identify likely locations where waterfowl may be found.
2. Use scouting to find spots with **access to food, water, and...
Objective: Improve hunting success by scouting potential areas.

Procedure:
1. Scout the area before hunting to identify likely locations where waterfowl may be found.
2. Use scouting to find spots with access to food, water, and cover, such as wetlands or agricultural fields.
3. Scouting increases your chances of success by allowing you to avoid crowded areas and find new opportunities.
4. Hunters who scout typically take three times more ducks than those who do not.
scouting,hunting success,duck hunting,strategy

Objective: Improve hunting success by using decoys effectively.

Procedure:
1. Study how ducks interact on the water to understand decoy placement and behavior.
2. Choose a decoying technique that matches your hunting location,...
Objective: Improve hunting success by using decoys effectively.

Procedure:
1. Study how ducks interact on the water to understand decoy placement and behavior.
2. Choose a decoying technique that matches your hunting location, method, and target species.
3. Set up decoys in areas with open water, vegetation cover, or other features that attract birds.
4. Leave some spots open to encourage natural movement and interaction among the birds.
decoys,hunting strategy,bird attraction,placement

Procedure:
1. Understand that pass shooting is time-intensive and requires identifying travel corridors where ducks move between feeding and roosting...
Objective: Successfully hunt waterfowl using the pass shooting technique.

Procedure:
1. Understand that pass shooting is time-intensive and requires identifying travel corridors where ducks move between feeding and roosting sites.
2. Focus on hunting during early or late parts of the day, when birds are more active.
3. Scout to determine flight paths and identify areas with open water extending from land or vegetation for cover.
4. Be prepared to observe and understand bird behavior in these corridors to increase success.
pass shooting,hunting technique,bird movement,strategy

Procedure:

Objective: Locate geese by understanding their habitat preferences.

Procedure:
1. Recognize that geese can inhabit a wide range of environments, from frozen tundra to urban areas like downtown Chicago.
2. Note that Canada geese, in particular, are highly adaptable and can survive in almost any environment with access to food and water.
3. Be aware that other goose species may be less likely to adapt to urban settings.
4. Use this information to identify potential hunting locations based on available resources.

Turkey Calling (7) -

Using a Box Call
Standard Source: wdfw01803
Procedure:

1. Understand the call structure: The box call is a box with a movable lid that creates sound when moved.
2. Position the call: Hold the box call in one hand, ensuring the lid can move freely against the edges of the box.
3....
Procedure:

1. Understand the call structure: The box call is a box with a movable lid that creates sound when moved.
2. Position the call: Hold the box call in one hand, ensuring the lid can move freely against the edges of the box.
3. Create pressure on the lid: Apply pressure to the lid and slide it across the edges of the box to produce sound.
4. Adjust for desired sound: Vary the speed and pressure applied to the lid to create different turkey calls (gobbling, yelping, etc.).

Notes:
• The box call is ideal for producing a wide range of turkey sounds.
• It requires practice to master the technique.
box call, friction call, turkey calling, hunting equipment
Using a Diaphragm Call
Standard Source: wdfw01803
Procedure:

1. Understand the call structure: The diaphragm call is a flexible membrane that vibrates when blown into to produce sound.
2. Position the call: Hold the diaphragm call in your mouth, ensuring it is fully sealed with your...
Procedure:

1. Understand the call structure: The diaphragm call is a flexible membrane that vibrates when blown into to produce sound.
2. Position the call: Hold the diaphragm call in your mouth, ensuring it is fully sealed with your lips.
3. Blow into the call: Use your breath to create vibrations in the diaphragm, producing turkey sounds (gobbling, yelping, etc.).
4. Adjust for desired sound: Vary the pressure and speed of your breath to produce different turkey calls.

Notes:
• This call is hands-free and allows you to aim while calling.
• It requires significant practice to master and produce realistic turkey sounds.
diaphragm call, turkey calling, hunting equipment

1. Understand the call structure: A gobble tube is shaken to produce gobbling-type sounds.
2. Shake the tube: Hold the tube in one hand and shake it vigorously to create sound.
3. Adjust for desired sound: Vary the speed and intensity of shaking to produce different turkey calls (gobbling, yelping, etc.).

Notes:
• This call is effective for attracting mature toms but may also attract other hunters.
• It requires practice to use effectively.
gobble tube, turkey calling, hunting equipment
Using a Locator Call
Standard Source: wdfw01803
Procedure:

1. Understand the purpose: A locator call is used to locate turkeys without drawing them to your location.
2. Choose the appropriate sound: Use loud and sudden sounds (such as crow, owl, or hawk calls) to attract turkeys.
3....
Procedure:

1. Understand the purpose: A locator call is used to locate turkeys without drawing them to your location.
2. Choose the appropriate sound: Use loud and sudden sounds (such as crow, owl, or hawk calls) to attract turkeys.
3. Use the call strategically: Make noise in areas where turkeys are likely to be during spring months.
4. Observe turkey behavior: Listen for gobbling or other responses from turkeys to locate their position.

Notes:
• Locator calls are useful for finding flocks before setting up a hunt.
• Avoid overusing the call, as it may alert turkeys to your presence.
locator call, turkey calling, hunting equipment
Using a Pot and Peg Call
Standard Source: wdfw01803

2. Position the call: Hold the call in one hand with the striker positioned against...
Procedure:

1. Understand the call structure: The pot and peg call consists of a striker (a pen-like stick) and a face that creates friction when struck.
2. Position the call: Hold the call in one hand with the striker positioned against the face of the call.
3. Strike the face: Use the striker to create friction by sliding it across the face of the call, producing sound.
4. Adjust for desired sound: Vary the pressure and speed of the strike to produce different turkey calls (gobbling, yelping, etc.).

Notes:
• The materials used on the striking surface can affect the quality and tone of the sound produced.
• Practice is recommended to achieve a realistic turkey call.
pot and peg call, friction call, turkey calling, hunting equipment

1. Understand the call structure: The push button call is mounted on a shotgun or crossbow and has a button that produces sound when pressed.
2. Mount the call: Ensure the push button call is securely attached to your hunting equipment.
3. Press the button: Use your fingers to press the button, which will produce a yelping sound.
4. Adjust for desired sound: The push button call typically produces a limited range of sounds but can be effective in certain situations.

Procedure:

1. Understand the call structure: A wingbone call is made from turkey wing bones or plastic and requires use of your mouth to produce sound.
