Shooting Techniques (6) -

Proper Shot Placement for Ethical Harvesting
Standard Source: wdfw01807
Step 1: Only take a shot when you are 100% confident in your ability to make an ethical and humane kill.

Step 2: Practice with your hunting equipment before the season begins.

Step 3: Know exactly where to aim for effective shot...
Step 1: Only take a shot when you are 100% confident in your ability to make an ethical and humane kill.

Step 2: Practice with your hunting equipment before the season begins.

Step 3: Know exactly where to aim for effective shot placement.

Step 4: Be aware of any obstructions (e.g., branches, twigs) that may affect projectile trajectory.

Step 5: Refer to visual aids or diagrams provided in field guides to understand optimal shot positions.
shot placement,ethical harvesting,hunting techniques,projectile trajectory
Properly Shoot and Handle Furbearers
Standard Source: Student manual 2005
Shooting furbearers:

• Aim between the eyes and slightly upward for a brain shot to ensure a quick, humane kill.
• After confirming the animal is dead, carry it a short distance away and lay it with its head downhill if possible. This...
Shooting furbearers:

• Aim between the eyes and slightly upward for a brain shot to ensure a quick, humane kill.
• After confirming the animal is dead, carry it a short distance away and lay it with its head downhill if possible. This allows it to bleed out while you reset your trap set.
• When transporting the animal home, place it on newspapers to keep the fur clean.
• During skinning:
- Wrap paper towels around the head and secure them with duct tape to contain any blood.
- Remove the towels after skinning and rinse off any remaining blood with cold water.
• To dry the pelt, hang it in front of a fan overnight.

Important note:
Use selective trapping techniques to avoid catching pets or unintended animals.
shoot furbearers, humane killing, animal handling, skinning technique
Avoid Head-on Shot for Archers
Standard Source: wdfw01807
Avoid Head-on Shot for Archers

• This shot is generally not recommended.
• The chance of hitting vital organs is lower compared to other shots.
• The only viable shot would be a heart shot, but it carries significant risks.
• A head-on shot...
Avoid Head-on Shot for Archers

• This shot is generally not recommended.
• The chance of hitting vital organs is lower compared to other shots.
• The only viable shot would be a heart shot, but it carries significant risks.
• A head-on shot may rupture the intestines, stomachs, and other areas with digestive juices.
• Archers should avoid this shot as the sternum will likely deflect the arrow.
head-on shot, hunting techniques, archery
Avoid Quartering to Shot for Archers
Standard Source: wdfw01807
Avoid Quartering to Shot for Archers

• This shot is more difficult due to increased bone areas that can be hit.
• Archers should avoid this shot as the probability of hitting a rib or shoulder is higher.
• The animal may see you draw and...
Avoid Quartering to Shot for Archers

• This shot is more difficult due to increased bone areas that can be hit.
• Archers should avoid this shot as the probability of hitting a rib or shoulder is higher.
• The animal may see you draw and release, leading to it moving when the bowstring is released ("jumping the string").
• Muzzleloader and rifle hunters may not have an issue since bullets can pass through bone.
quartering to shot, archery, hunting techniques
Estimate Range to Target 'On the Fly'
Standard Source: Basics of Waterfowling
Objective: Learn how to estimate range to a moving target while in the field.

Steps:
1. Observe the size and movement of the target (e.g., a bird or decoy) relative to known reference points (e.g., trees, shrubs, or other objects).
2. Use...
Objective: Learn how to estimate range to a moving target while in the field.

Steps:
1. Observe the size and movement of the target (e.g., a bird or decoy) relative to known reference points (e.g., trees, shrubs, or other objects).
2. Use your peripheral vision to gauge how quickly the target is moving across your field of view.
3. Compare the target's apparent size and speed with your prior experience or training materials (such as a waterfowl identification guide).
4. Adjust your estimate based on environmental factors such as wind, light conditions, and terrain.

Details:
• Practice regularly to improve accuracy in range estimation.
• Use tools like rangefinders if available for calibration during training sessions.
• Be aware that distance perception can be affected by atmospheric conditions or the target's flight pattern.
range estimation, on the fly shooting, hunting accuracy, waterfowl identification
Improve Wing Shooting Technique Using the 'Swing Through' Method
Standard Source: Basics of Waterfowling
Objective: Improve wing shooting technique using the proven "swing through" method.

Steps:
1. Position yourself in a safe area with a shotgun and appropriate ammunition.
2. Focus on the target (e.g., a moving object or decoy) as it...
Objective: Improve wing shooting technique using the proven "swing through" method.

Steps:
1. Position yourself in a safe area with a shotgun and appropriate ammunition.
2. Focus on the target (e.g., a moving object or decoy) as it approaches.
3. Begin swinging your shotgun smoothly in the direction of the target's movement, maintaining eye contact with the target throughout the swing.
4. Continue the swing through the point where the target would be if it continued its path, ensuring that you maintain proper follow-through.
5. Practice this technique repeatedly to develop muscle memory and improve accuracy.

Details:
• Use a safe backstop or range for practice.
• Ensure proper eye dominance and alignment with the shotgun.
• Consider using a training aid such as a moving target or laser sight for feedback.
swing through method, wing shooting technique, shotgun training, hunting skills

Trapping Methods (6) -

Create a Pocket Set for Muskrats and Mink
Standard Source: Student manual 2005
To create a pocket set for muskrats and mink:

1. Choose the location: Find a bank that is straight up and down at the waterline.
2. Dig the pocket: Dig a pocket into the bank at a level where the bottom will be about two inches below the...
To create a pocket set for muskrats and mink:

1. Choose the location: Find a bank that is straight up and down at the waterline.
2. Dig the pocket: Dig a pocket into the bank at a level where the bottom will be about two inches below the water. The pocket should extend one to two feet into the bank and angle upward.
3. Prepare the pocket: Ensure the pocket is about six inches in diameter for muskrats or mink.
4. Place bait or lure: Put the bait or lure above water level at the back of the pocket.
5. Set the trap: Set a body-gripping or foothold trap of the correct size for the animal you plan to catch. The trap can be placed at the mouth of the hole in case the animal doesn’t want to go all the way inside.
6. Avoid dog interference: If dogs may be a concern, do not use meat or fish bait, or raccoon gland lures. Instead, place the trap well inside the pocket or make the set under cover such as low-hanging branches or exposed tree roots.
7. Shave the bank: Shave both sides of the bank to create a smooth surface that makes it easier for the animal to enter the pocket.
pocket set,muskrat,mink,trapping methods,water set
Create a Runway Set for Muskrats Using a Colony Trap
Standard Source: Student manual 2005
To create a runway set for muskrats using a colony trap:

1. Identify the location: Find an area where muskrats frequently travel back and forth in shallow water, creating runways in the mud.
2. Prepare the trap: Use a special type of cage...
To create a runway set for muskrats using a colony trap:

1. Identify the location: Find an area where muskrats frequently travel back and forth in shallow water, creating runways in the mud.
2. Prepare the trap: Use a special type of cage trap called a colony trap, which has a swinging door on each end that opens easily when a muskrat enters and then falls closed after it is inside.
3. Set the trap: Place the colony trap across the runway so that the muskrat will be forced to swim in a circle, wrapping the wire around two stakes. The weight of the trap will pull the animal under the water.
4. Ensure proper water depth: Make sure the water is deep enough to keep the muskrats from sticking their noses out the top of the trap to breathe.
5. Adjust for cold weather: During cold weather, ensure that colony traps are a few inches below the daytime water level because some water sources may freeze at night and cause water levels to drop slightly.
6. Use additional traps if needed: Body-gripping traps can also be used in runways as an alternative or supplement to colony traps.
runway set,muskrat,colony trap,water set,trapping methods
Setting Cable Devices for Beaver in Water
Standard Source: Student manual 2005
Procedure:

1. Set cable loops for swimming beaver in water.
2. The diameter of the loop should be 9 to 10 inches.
3. One-third of the loop should be above the water line.
4. Avoid setting cable devices where animals can become entangled on...
Procedure:

1. Set cable loops for swimming beaver in water.
2. The diameter of the loop should be 9 to 10 inches.
3. One-third of the loop should be above the water line.
4. Avoid setting cable devices where animals can become entangled on fences, trees, rocks, or logs.
5. Cable devices work best in animal trails or blind sets where the animal will encounter it as it travels.
beaver trapping, water cable device, swimming beaver

5. Cable devices work best in animal trails or blind sets where the animal will encounter it as it travels.
fox trapping, coyote trapping, land cable device

Animal Processing (5) -

Field-Dressing Game Birds
Standard Source: The Complete Outdoorsmans Handbook
Steps:
1. Cut a bird-gutting hook from a branch (about the length and thickness of a pencil).
2. Make a small slit near the vent of the bird.
3. **Insert the hook into the body cavity, twist it 3-4 times, and pull out the...
Steps:
1. Cut a bird-gutting hook from a branch (about the length and thickness of a pencil).
2. Make a small slit near the vent of the bird.
3. Insert the hook into the body cavity, twist it 3-4 times, and pull out the entrails.

Alternative Method:
1. Use hands to field-dress birds if near water.
2. Wash hands briefly before continuing.

Notes:
• Field-dressing should be done immediately after retrieving the bird.
• This helps cool the bird quickly and prevents fluids from the viscera from contaminating the body cavity.
• Birds like sage grouse, spruce grouse, and ducks that eat animal matter should have their crops removed for best flavor.
• Field-dressed birds can be left hanging in a cool, breezy place for several days without losing flavor.
• Refrigeration is only needed if keeping the birds for more than a week.
field dressing,bird processing,hunting techniques
Skinning Big Game
Standard Source: The Complete Outdoorsmans Handbook
### Procedure: Skinning Big Game

Steps:
1. Cut off legs below the knee and hock joints.
2. Girdle and peel away the skin.
3. Skin the body quickly.
4. **For bears, make cuts on the inside of the legs and skin out the feet while...
### Procedure: Skinning Big Game

Steps:
1. Cut off legs below the knee and hock joints.
2. Girdle and peel away the skin.
3. Skin the body quickly.
4. For bears, make cuts on the inside of the legs and skin out the feet while keeping the claws attached if making a rug.
5. For mountain goats, leave the hoofs attached to the skin.
6. Skinning is easier when the carcass is still warm.
7. Keep hair away from the meat.
8. Be cautious with pronghorn hair as it can taint the meat slightly.

### Head Skinning (for mounting):
1. If possible, freeze the head or take it to a taxidermist within a few days.
2. If in the bush for several weeks, skin out the head yourself, especially on bears.
3. Skin slowly and carefully around the eyes and lips.
4. Cut ears off flush with the skull.
5. Cut the entire snout off where the septum between the nostrils meets the skull.
6. Scrape all flesh away from the hide.
7. Salt the hide well, rubbing salt into it.
skinning,big game,hunting,processing meat,taxidermy
Skinning Small Game
Standard Source: The Complete Outdoorsmans Handbook
Steps:
1. Skin small game (rabbits and squirrels) while they are still warm.
2. Squirrels become harder to skin once cold.
3. Behead the animal and cut off its feet and tail.
4. **Pinch the skin on the back and make a cut from the...
Steps:
1. Skin small game (rabbits and squirrels) while they are still warm.
2. Squirrels become harder to skin once cold.
3. Behead the animal and cut off its feet and tail.
4. Pinch the skin on the back and make a cut from the root of the tail to the neck.
5. Insert fingers inside the carcass and pull the skin away with the other hand (like pulling a glove off).

Notes:
• Small game can be field dressed immediately after shooting, similar to deer.
• Alternatively, wait until the animal is skinned out before dressing it.
skinning small game,rabbit,squirrel,hunting processing

Proper Storage:
• Avoid air-tight pockets in hunting jackets, especially on warm days.
• Use a game carrier that allows air to circulate around the birds (e.g., belt or over-the-shoulder carriers).

Field-Dressing Tips:
1. Field-dress birds immediately after retrieving them.
2. This helps with cooling and prevents contamination from viscera fluids.
3. For best flavor, dress sage grouse, spruce grouse, and ducks that eat animal matter immediately and remove their crops.
4. Even for other birds, it is preferable to field-dress them right away unless they have been badly shot.
storing game birds,hunting storage,field dressing

Purpose:
To preserve small animal pelts (e.g., rabbit) before tanning.

Steps:
1. Airdry the pelt thoroughly by hanging it in a well-ventilated area away from direct sunlight or heat sources.
2. Alternatively, for hobby tanning, tack the skin flat to a board with the flesh side out and allow it to air-dry completely.
3. If desired, you may also salt cure the hide, following the same procedure as used for large animal hides (see other procedures for details on salting).
4. Ensure that the pelt is completely dry before proceeding with any tanning process.
curing small pelts, preserving fur skins, rabbit pelt processing, hide salting, animal tanning preparation

Procedure:

1. To make plucking easier, boil water in a large stock pot and dip the turkey into it outside to avoid overflow of boiling water.
2. This process lightly cooks the skin, making feathers easier to remove.
3. If you do not have a large stock pot, simply pluck the feathers manually without using hot water.

Procedure:

1. Once you harvest your turkey, notch and affix your tag to the carcass.
2. Immediately after harvesting, validate the tag by cutting out and completely removing the month and day of kill. A slit is not acceptable.
3. Ensure the tag is securely attached to the carcass in a visible manner.
4. Washington tags have little holes on the right-hand side; use this as a good spot to insert a zip tie through and attach the tag very securely to the turkey’s leg.

Traps (4) -

Constructing a Bow Trap
Standard Source: Animals For Food
Procedure:

1. Construct a bow using suitable materials (e.g., flexible branches or wood).
2. Anchor the bow to the ground with pegs, adjusting the aiming point as you secure it.
3. Lash a toggle stick to the trigger stick.
4. Drive two...
Procedure:

1. Construct a bow using suitable materials (e.g., flexible branches or wood).
2. Anchor the bow to the ground with pegs, adjusting the aiming point as you secure it.
3. Lash a toggle stick to the trigger stick.
4. Drive two upright sticks into the ground to hold the trigger stick in place at a point where the toggle stick will engage the pulled bow string.
5. Place a catch stick between the toggle stick and a stake driven into the ground.
6. Tie a trip wire or cordage to the catch stick, routing it around stakes and across the game trail before tying it off.
7. When prey trips the trip wire, the bow releases an arrow that strikes the animal.
8. A notch in the bow helps aim the arrow accurately.
bow trap,hunting traps,constructing traps,arrow setup
Constructing a Figure 4 Deadfall Trigger
Standard Source: Animals For Food
Procedure:

1. Gather three notched sticks for constructing the trigger.
2. Ensure that the notches are precisely cut to hold the sticks together in a figure 4 pattern when under tension.
3. Practice assembling and disassembling the figure 4...
Procedure:

1. Gather three notched sticks for constructing the trigger.
2. Ensure that the notches are precisely cut to hold the sticks together in a figure 4 pattern when under tension.
3. Practice assembling and disassembling the figure 4 before using it in the field; this requires close tolerances and precise angles in its construction.
4. The weight used should be heavy enough to kill or incapacitate the prey immediately.
5. Secure the deadfall setup in a location where prey is likely to pass.
deadfalls,figure 4 trigger,hunting traps,constructing traps
Constructing a Pig Spear Shaft
Standard Source: Animals For Food
Procedure:

1. Select a stout pole about 2.5 meters long for the spear shaft.
2. At the smaller end, firmly lash several small stakes to secure it.
3. Lash the large end of the spear tightly to a tree along the game trail.
4. Tie a length of...
Procedure:

1. Select a stout pole about 2.5 meters long for the spear shaft.
2. At the smaller end, firmly lash several small stakes to secure it.
3. Lash the large end of the spear tightly to a tree along the game trail.
4. Tie a length of cordage to another tree across the trail and attach a sturdy, smooth stick to the other end of the cord.
5. From the first tree, tie a trip wire or cord low to the ground, stretch it across the trail, and tie it to a catch stick.
6. Make a slip ring from vines or suitable material, encircle the trip wire and smooth stick with the slip ring.
7. Place one end of another smooth stick within the slip ring and its other end against the second tree.
8. Pull the smaller end of the spear shaft across the trail, positioning it between the short cord and the smooth stick.
9. When an animal trips the trip wire, the catch stick pulls the slip ring off the smooth sticks, releasing the spear shaft that springs across the trail and impales the prey against the tree.
pig spear shaft,hunting traps,constructing traps,spear setup
Constructing a Bottle Trap for Small Rodents
Reference Source: Animals For Food
Procedure:

1. Dig a hole 30 to 45 centimeters deep, ensuring it is wider at the bottom than at the top.
2. Make the top of the hole as small as possible to limit entry points for prey.
3. Place a piece of bark or wood over the hole,...
Procedure:

1. Dig a hole 30 to 45 centimeters deep, ensuring it is wider at the bottom than at the top.
2. Make the top of the hole as small as possible to limit entry points for prey.
3. Place a piece of bark or wood over the hole, using small stones underneath to hold it up 2.5 to 5 centimeters off the ground.
4. Mice and voles will hide under the cover to escape danger and fall into the hole.
5. They cannot climb out due to the backward slope of the hole's walls.
6. Use caution when checking this trap, as it may be an excellent hiding place for snakes.
bottle trap,hunting traps,constructing traps,mouse trap

Elk Tracking Signs (4) -

Identify Elk Droppings in the Field
Reference Source: wdfw01807
Procedure:

1. Look for droppings that resemble deer pellets, but are slightly larger.
2. Observe the shape: elk droppings typically have an acorn-like appearance, with one end dimpled and the other having a small projection.
3....
Procedure:

1. Look for droppings that resemble deer pellets, but are slightly larger.
2. Observe the shape: elk droppings typically have an acorn-like appearance, with one end dimpled and the other having a small projection.
3. Count the number of pellets: adult elk may leave twice as many pellets as deer, often between 40 to 60 at a time.
4. Note texture differences: when elk have a rich diet, their droppings may form a soft mass, similar to a domestic cowpie but smaller in size.
5. Use this information to identify areas where elk are feeding or resting and plan your hunting approach accordingly.
elk droppings, pellet identification, acorn-like shape, elk feces, feeding area signs
Identify Elk Feeding Areas by Broken Shoots
Reference Source: wdfw01807
Procedure:

1. Look for broken shoots on browse plants, especially in areas where elk are suspected to be feeding.
2. Measure the height of the broken shoots: if they are four feet or higher off the ground, it is likely **elk...
Procedure:

1. Look for broken shoots on browse plants, especially in areas where elk are suspected to be feeding.
2. Measure the height of the broken shoots: if they are four feet or higher off the ground, it is likely elk browsing, not deer.
3. Use this information to identify potential feeding areas that may be suitable for hunting during the season.
4. Ensure you have a good vantage point from which to observe these areas and plan your approach accordingly.
broken shoots, elk feeding signs, elk tracking, browsing height, hunting location identification
Identify Elk Tracks in the Field
Reference Source: wdfw01807
Procedure:

1. Look for cloven hooves with a split-heart shape on soft earth.
2. Measure the size of the tracks: elk tracks are approximately four inches long and three inches wide, larger and more round than deer tracks, but smaller...
Procedure:

1. Look for cloven hooves with a split-heart shape on soft earth.
2. Measure the size of the tracks: elk tracks are approximately four inches long and three inches wide, larger and more round than deer tracks, but smaller and less round than moose tracks.
3. Check for dewclaw impressions in mud or snow, which may be visible up to several inches from the main hoof print.
4. Observe track patterns: elk trails are often several animals wide, especially at transitions between grasslands and brush/woodlands.
5. Note that elk tracks are easier to find than most animals due to their weight, which leaves clear impressions on almost any surface they walk over.
6. Look for large numbers of tracks, which may indicate a passing herd and can be followed easily if the terrain allows.
elk tracks, track identification, split-heart shape, dewclaw impressions, elk trail patterns
Identify and Understand Elk Wallow Sites
Reference Source: wdfw01807
Procedure:

1. Look for mud wallows, which are typically bathtub-sized depressions with low walls of displaced mud.
2. Check for the presence of urine and droppings around the wallow, as these are often scented by bull elk to attract...
Procedure:

1. Look for mud wallows, which are typically bathtub-sized depressions with low walls of displaced mud.
2. Check for the presence of urine and droppings around the wallow, as these are often scented by bull elk to attract receptive cows during mating season.
3. Note that wallows may be musky-smelling, especially if actively used, with fresh hoofprints visible around the perimeter.
4. Identify locations where the ground is wet and muddy, usually near water sources in secluded areas where elk feel safe enough to drop their guard.
5. Recognize that abandoned wallows may fill with water and become breeding sites for amphibians like frogs and salamanders, indicating long-term use by elk.
mud wallows, elk scent marking, mating sites, wallow identification, elk behavior

Elk Hunting (4) -

Identify Elk Bedding Areas
Standard Source: wdfw01807
Procedure:

1. Look for an area of flattened vegetation that is:
- Three to four feet long
- Two to three feet wide
2. These areas are typically found in dense cover or tall grasses.
3. Elk may return to the same spot over many...
Procedure:

1. Look for an area of flattened vegetation that is:
- Three to four feet long
- Two to three feet wide
2. These areas are typically found in dense cover or tall grasses.
3. Elk may return to the same spot over many days, so multiple 'elk beds' can be found in the same vicinity.
4. During winter, look for similarly sized depressions in the snow, often littered with old hairs.
5. If you know about these sites, it can be effective to set up near the site and watch for elk coming back to bed down at dusk or getting up in the morning.
6. Elk may also bed for a mid-day nap.
elk, bedding areas, hunting, identification
Identify Elk Rubs
Standard Source: wdfw01807
Procedure:

1. Look for small saplings and shrubs that appear to have been damaged by a hedge trimmer.
2. These rubs are created when bulls begin to vigorously rub their antlers on shrubs and trees after antler growth ceases in late...
Procedure:

1. Look for small saplings and shrubs that appear to have been damaged by a hedge trimmer.
2. These rubs are created when bulls begin to vigorously rub their antlers on shrubs and trees after antler growth ceases in late summer.
3. This behavior helps rid the antlers of the velvet covering, which begins to dry up and shred as blood supply deteriorates.
4. The rubbing also covers the bone-white antler with plant compounds that oxidize and stain the antlers to their characteristic dark brown color.
5. Antler color can also be influenced by blood and how long the bull stays in the open country where antlers are exposed to sunlight.
6. These rubs occur before the rut begins, and the bulls that made them may be miles away from them, but they indicate that the area has been used by elk.
elk, rubs, hunting, identification
Spot and Stalk Hunting Technique
Standard Source: wdfw01807
Procedure:

1. Survey areas likely to be used by elk, such as open habitats where elk can be spotted at a distance.
2. Use binoculars or a spotting scope to locate a legal elk.
3. Once an elk is spotted, sneak to within range of the...
Procedure:

1. Survey areas likely to be used by elk, such as open habitats where elk can be spotted at a distance.
2. Use binoculars or a spotting scope to locate a legal elk.
3. Once an elk is spotted, sneak to within range of the elk in hopes of getting a shot.
4. This technique is commonly used when animals have been observed by a hunter and the hunter has not been detected.
spot and stalk, hunting, elk
Stand Hunting Technique
Standard Source: wdfw01807
Procedure:

1. Position yourself at a place along a trail, feeding site, or other elk use area.
2. Use ground blinds or tree stands to remain hidden while waiting for an opportunity to shoot.
3. Some hunters simply **lean against a tree...
Procedure:

1. Position yourself at a place along a trail, feeding site, or other elk use area.
2. Use ground blinds or tree stands to remain hidden while waiting for an opportunity to shoot.
3. Some hunters simply lean against a tree or sit on a stump near a likely area or travel corridor.
4. This technique should only be used when you know animals are close and using the area.
5. Look for areas with water present and elk sign, as these would be good places to set up a stand or blind and hunt the water hole.
stand hunting, hunting, elk

Snaring (4) -

Recognizing Signs of Animal Movement for Snaring
Standard Source: Aids to Survival
Recognizing Signs of Animal Movement for Snaring

1. Look for signs of fur around the base of trees or along fence lines, as this can indicate where an animal has passed through.
2. Animals often return to the same place to sleep and will...
Recognizing Signs of Animal Movement for Snaring

1. Look for signs of fur around the base of trees or along fence lines, as this can indicate where an animal has passed through.
2. Animals often return to the same place to sleep and will continue to negotiate fences at the same spot.
3. Use these signs to determine where to set snares or traps for maximum effectiveness.
4. Observe the terrain carefully for any unusual markings, such as claw marks, broken branches, or disturbed vegetation.
5. Pay attention to animal tracks and droppings to identify which species may be in the area.
6. Use this information to strategically place snares where animals are likely to travel.

Important Notes:
• Be patient when setting snares, as it may take time for an animal to fall into one.
• Regularly check your snares and retrieve any caught animals promptly to avoid attracting predators or scavengers.
animal signs, snare placement, tracking animals, survival trapping, bushcraft skills
Snaring Animals Without Weapons or Traps
Standard Source: Aids to Survival
Snaring Animals Without Weapons or Traps

1. Locate a suitable area where animals are likely to pass through, such as near water sources, along fence lines, or under trees.
2. Use a wire noose and place it in a convenient position, such as the...
Snaring Animals Without Weapons or Traps

1. Locate a suitable area where animals are likely to pass through, such as near water sources, along fence lines, or under trees.
2. Use a wire noose and place it in a convenient position, such as the entrance of a hole or above an animal path between two trees.
3. Ensure that the noose slides freely and anchor the other end of the wire securely to a tree or post.
4. Wait for the animal to pass through the snare; once it does, the noose will tighten around its neck quickly, killing it as it tries to pull free.
5. Check the snare regularly and retrieve the animal when possible.
6. Avoid leaving any human scent on the wire or in the area where the snare is set, as this may deter animals from approaching.

Important Notes:
• Snaring should only be used when other methods of obtaining food fail.
• This method can be effective at night when animals are less likely to see the snare.
• Be aware that snaring is considered a cruel method and should be used as a last resort.
animal snaring, survival hunting, wire noose, trapping without weapons, bushcraft
Using the Possum Snare for Capturing Animals

Using the Possum Snare for Capturing Animals

1. Locate a suitable area where possums or other small animals are likely to pass through, such as near trees or under rocks.
2. Take a length of wire or strong cord and tie it into a loop using a...
Using the Possum Snare for Capturing Animals

1. Locate a suitable area where possums or other small animals are likely to pass through, such as near trees or under rocks.
2. Take a length of wire or strong cord and tie it into a loop using a specific method known as the possum snare technique.
3. Place the loop in a convenient position, such as the entrance of a hole or above an animal path between two trees.
4. Ensure that the noose slides freely and anchor the other end of the wire securely to a tree or post.
5. Wait for the animal to pass through the snare; once it does, the loop will tighten around its neck quickly, killing it as it tries to pull free.
6. Check the snare regularly and retrieve the animal when possible.

Important Notes:
• This method can be effective at night when animals are less likely to see the snare.
• Be aware that snaring is considered a cruel method and should be used as a last resort.
possum snare, survival trapping, wire noose, bushcraft skills, animal capture

Using the Reef Knot for Snaring

1. Locate a suitable area where an animal is likely to pass through, such as near water sources or along fence lines.
2. Take a length of wire or strong cord and tie it into a reef knot (also known as a square...
Using the Reef Knot for Snaring

1. Locate a suitable area where an animal is likely to pass through, such as near water sources or along fence lines.
2. Take a length of wire or strong cord and tie it into a reef knot (also known as a square knot) to create a secure loop.
3. Place the loop in a convenient position, such as the entrance of a hole or above an animal path between two trees.
4. Ensure that the noose slides freely and anchor the other end of the wire securely to a tree or post.
5. Wait for the animal to pass through the snare; once it does, the loop will tighten around its neck quickly, killing it as it tries to pull free.
6. Check the snare regularly and retrieve the animal when possible.

Important Notes:
• This method can be effective at night when animals are less likely to see the snare.
• Be aware that snaring is considered a cruel method and should be used as a last resort.

Trap Safety (4) -

Use Extreme Caution When Setting Body-Gripping Traps Under Water
Standard Source: Student manual 2005
To use extreme caution when setting body-gripping traps under water, follow these steps:

1. Assess the environment: Ensure that you are in a safe location and that there is no risk of falling into water or being trapped below the surface.
2....
To use extreme caution when setting body-gripping traps under water, follow these steps:

1. Assess the environment: Ensure that you are in a safe location and that there is no risk of falling into water or being trapped below the surface.
2. Use proper tools: Always use setting tongs and a Conibear™ safety gripper when working with large body-gripping traps.
3. Carry a self-rescue rope: Always carry a length of rope with a strong loop for self-rescue in case you get caught.
4. Work with a partner: Never set traps under water or below ice alone. Always work with an instructor, experienced trapper, or trusted companion.
5. Check the trap placement: Ensure that the trap is securely fastened and that all components are properly positioned.
6. Avoid risky situations: If your trap is fastened below the water's surface or below ice, you could have a difficult time freeing yourself if caught.

Important safety considerations:
• Always work with an instructor or experienced trapper when setting traps under water or below ice.
• Use gloves to protect your hands from injury and to avoid leaving human scent on the trap.
• Practice with the same type of gloves you will use when making sets on the trapline.
• Never attempt to set body-gripping traps under water without proper training, tools, and a partner.
body-gripping trap, underwater safety, self-rescue rope, trapper education
Use a Rope for Self-Rescue When Setting Large Body-Gripping Traps
Standard Source: Student manual 2005
To use a rope for self-rescue when setting large body-gripping traps (No. 220 and above), follow these steps:

1. Prepare the rope: Ensure that you have a length of rope with a strong loop in it.
2. Position the rope: Put one foot in the...
To use a rope for self-rescue when setting large body-gripping traps (No. 220 and above), follow these steps:

1. Prepare the rope: Ensure that you have a length of rope with a strong loop in it.
2. Position the rope: Put one foot in the loop of the rope.
3. Thread the rope through the spring eyes: Run the rope through the eyes of the spring, then around the eyes and up through the eyes again.
4. Secure the rope: Pull on the free end of the rope with your free hand or teeth until you can set the safety hook for that spring.
5. Repeat for the other spring: Repeat this procedure on the other spring until you relieve enough pressure to remove your hand.
6. Remove your foot from the loop: Once the trap is safely set, remove your foot from the loop and store the rope securely.

Important safety considerations:
• Always carry a length of rope with a strong loop when using No. 220 or larger body-gripping traps for self-rescue in case you get caught.
• Practice with an instructor or experienced trapper until you can do it correctly.
• Never attempt to set large body-gripping traps without proper tools and training.
self-rescue rope, body-gripping trap, safety procedures, trapper education
Understand the Use of a Safety Gripper in Trapping
Standard Source: Student manual 2005
A Safety Gripper is a device used to hold a body-gripping trap in the set position while it is being handled by a trapper.

• Purpose:
- Prevents accidental activation of the trap during handling.
- Ensures the safety of the trapper and any...
A Safety Gripper is a device used to hold a body-gripping trap in the set position while it is being handled by a trapper.

• Purpose:
- Prevents accidental activation of the trap during handling.
- Ensures the safety of the trapper and any nearby wildlife.
• How to use:
1. Attach the safety gripper to the trap before setting it.
2. Ensure the device is properly engaged to hold the trap in place.
3. Remove the safety gripper only when the trap is ready to be set.
safety gripper, trap handling, trapper safety, wildlife trapping

A Swivel is a device used at the ends and/or middle of a trap chain to reduce injury to a trapped animal.

• Purpose:
- Allows the trap chain to rotate freely, reducing pressure on the animal’s body.
- Helps prevent entanglement or injury during trapping.

Legal And Ethical Practices (4) -

Handling a Landowner's Request to Hunt Deer Without Proper Permit
Standard Source: Student manual 2005
When approached by a landowner who wants to hunt deer without proper permits:

1. Politely decline the request, explaining that you are there for trapping, not hunting.
2. Inform them that you are unaware of their permit status and that it...
When approached by a landowner who wants to hunt deer without proper permits:

1. Politely decline the request, explaining that you are there for trapping, not hunting.
2. Inform them that you are unaware of their permit status and that it is illegal to hunt without one.
3. Suggest they contact the local wildlife agency to confirm if they have a valid permit or need assistance with deer management.
4. If the landowner insists, reiterate your position clearly but respectfully, emphasizing that you cannot participate in any illegal activity.
5. Offer to help them find legal resources or alternatives, such as contacting the wildlife agency directly for guidance.
6. If necessary, seek support from local authorities if the situation becomes confrontational.
deer hunting,landowner,permit,illegal activity,wildlife agency
Addressing Misconceptions About Trapping with Friends
Reference Source: Student manual 2005
When friends express discomfort or disapproval about your trapping activities:

1. Acknowledge their feelings and listen to their concerns without becoming defensive.
2. Explain the purpose of trapping, such as fur harvesting, population...
When friends express discomfort or disapproval about your trapping activities:

1. Acknowledge their feelings and listen to their concerns without becoming defensive.
2. Explain the purpose of trapping, such as fur harvesting, population control, and conservation efforts.
3. Emphasize that you follow all legal and ethical guidelines, including proper trap placement, humane methods, and respect for wildlife.
4. Invite them to learn more about responsible trapping practices if they are interested.
5. If they remain offended, respect their opinion without pressuring them to change it.
6. Maintain a respectful and open dialogue, even if you disagree on the issue.
trapping,friendship,ethical trapping,conversation,opinion
Dealing with Another Trapper's Trap on Your Property
Reference Source: Student manual 2005
When you discover another trapper’s trap on your property:

1. Stay calm and avoid confrontation, even if the situation is frustrating.
2. Assess the location of the trap to determine whether it could be a threat to wildlife or other...
When you discover another trapper’s trap on your property:

1. Stay calm and avoid confrontation, even if the situation is frustrating.
2. Assess the location of the trap to determine whether it could be a threat to wildlife or other trappers.
3. If possible, contact the other trapper directly to inform them about the trap on your property.
4. If you cannot contact them, mark the trap clearly and leave it in place, ensuring that it does not pose an immediate danger to animals or people.
5. Document the location and time of discovery for future reference if needed.
6. Consider reporting the situation to local wildlife agencies or trapper associations if the issue persists or becomes a recurring problem.
trap conflict,property rights,trapper dispute,wildlife agency
Responding to Accusations of Stealing Fur from Traps
Reference Source: Student manual 2005
When accused of stealing fur from traps by individuals who claim ownership:

1. Remain calm and respectful, even if the accusation is false.
2. Explain that you have not stolen anything and that you are following legal trapping practices on...
When accused of stealing fur from traps by individuals who claim ownership:

1. Remain calm and respectful, even if the accusation is false.
2. Explain that you have not stolen anything and that you are following legal trapping practices on private land with permission.
3. State clearly that you have not seen any other traps on the property since the season opened, reinforcing your legitimacy as a trapper.
4. If necessary, offer to show trap locations or provide evidence of your own traps, such as tags or permits, if available.
5. Avoid confrontation or argument, and emphasize that you are following conservation and trapping laws.
6. If the situation escalates, seek assistance from local authorities or wildlife agencies to resolve the issue peacefully.
trapping,accusation,fur theft,response,trap ownership

Snare Construction (3) -

Construct a Drag Noose for Larger Animals
Standard Source: Animals For Food
Procedure:

1. Select a Location: Choose an animal run or trail where the target species is likely to pass.
2. Set Up Forked Sticks: Place forked sticks on either side of the run and lay a sturdy crossmember across them.
3. **Attach the...
Procedure:

1. Select a Location: Choose an animal run or trail where the target species is likely to pass.
2. Set Up Forked Sticks: Place forked sticks on either side of the run and lay a sturdy crossmember across them.
3. Attach the Noose: Tie the noose to the crossmember and hang it at a height above the animal's head (never low enough for the prey to step into with a foot).
4. Ensure Proper Height: The noose should be high enough so that when it tightens around the animal's neck, the crossmember is pulled from the forked sticks.
5. Test the Setup: Ensure that as the animal pulls the crossmember away, surrounding vegetation will catch it and entangle the animal.
drag noose, trapping, hunting, large game
Construct a Simple Snare for Small Game
Standard Source: Animals For Food
Procedure:

1. Gather Materials: Use wire or strong cordage to create the noose.
2. Position the Noose: Place the noose over a trail or den hole and secure it to a firmly planted stake.
3. Use Supports for Cordage: If using cordage,...
Procedure:

1. Gather Materials: Use wire or strong cordage to create the noose.
2. Position the Noose: Place the noose over a trail or den hole and secure it to a firmly planted stake.
3. Use Supports for Cordage: If using cordage, use small twigs or blades of grass to hold the noose upright on the trail.
4. Ensure Proper Size: Make sure the noose is large enough to pass freely over the animal's head.
5. Test the Snare: Ensure that as the animal moves, the noose tightens around its neck and does not slip off easily.
6. Use Wire for Better Results: Wire is more effective than cordage because it does not loosen and can hold the animal securely.
simple snare, wire, trapping, hunting, small game
Construct a Twitch-Up Snare Using a Bent Sapling
Standard Source: Animals For Food
Procedure:

1. Select a Suitable Sapling: Choose a hardwood sapling along the trail and remove all branches and foliage for better performance.
2. Prepare Forked Sticks: Use two forked sticks, each with a long and short leg.
3. **Bend...
Procedure:

1. Select a Suitable Sapling: Choose a hardwood sapling along the trail and remove all branches and foliage for better performance.
2. Prepare Forked Sticks: Use two forked sticks, each with a long and short leg.
3. Bend the Twitch-Up: Bend the sapling over and secure it to the ground using a triggering device.
4. Secure Stick Legs: Drive the long leg of one forked stick firmly into the ground at the marked trail point. Ensure the cut on the short leg is parallel to the ground.
5. Attach Cordage: Tie the long leg of the remaining forked stick to a piece of cordage secured to the twitch-up.
6. Set Up Trigger Mechanism: Cut the short leg so that it catches on the short leg of the other forked stick.
7. Place Noose Over Trail: Extend a noose over the trail and set the trap by bending the twitch-up and engaging the short legs of the forked sticks.
8. Avoid Green Sticks: Do not use green sticks for the trigger, as sap can cause them to stick together.
twitch-up snare, hardwood sapling, trapping, hunting

Materials Needed:
• Pins, needles, wire, small nails, or pieces of metal
• Wood, bone, coconut shell, thorns, flint, seashell, or tortoise shell
• Cordage

Steps:
1. Select a material such as pins, needles, wire, small nails, or any piece of metal to make the hook.
2. Alternatively, use wood, bone, coconut shell, thorns, flint, seashell, or tortoise shell.
3. To make a wooden hook:
- Cut a piece of hardwood about 2.5 centimeters long and 6 millimeters in diameter to form the shank.
- Cut a notch in one end to place the point (bone, wire, nail, etc.).
- Place the point in the notch and tie it securely so that it does not move out of position.
4. To make smaller hooks, use smaller material.

Materials Needed:

Steps:
1. Create a small shaft of wood, bone, metal, or other material to form the gorge.
2. Sharpen both ends of the shaft.
3. Cut a notch in the middle where you will tie cordage.
4. Tie the cordage securely around the notch so that it does not slip.
5. Bait the gorge by placing a piece of bait on it lengthwise.
6. When the fish swallows the bait, it also swallows the gorge.

Materials Needed:
• Two supple saplings
• Cordage
• Hooks or gorges
• Bait

Steps:
1. Drive two supple saplings into the bottom of a lake, pond, or stream with their tops just below the water surface.
2. Tie a cord between them and slightly below the surface.
3. Tie two short cords with hooks or gorges to this cord, ensuring that they cannot wrap around the poles or each other.
4. Ensure the cords do not slip along the long cord.
5. Bait the hooks or gorges.

Land Access And Permissions (3) -

Determine Public or Private Waterway Status
High Priority Source: Basics of Waterfowling
To determine if a waterway is public or private:
1. Assess navigability of the waterway:
- A river or stream is navigable if it has a bed or bank and can support floating a canoe or other small craft at some time during the year, even if only...
To determine if a waterway is public or private:
1. Assess navigability of the waterway:
- A river or stream is navigable if it has a bed or bank and can support floating a canoe or other small craft at some time during the year, even if only during spring floods.
2. Public access rules for navigable waterways:
- You may walk, fish, hunt, swim, or boat in any navigable lake, stream, or impoundment as long as you remain in the water (i.e., your feet must remain wet).
3. Shore area usage restrictions:
- The public may only use the exposed shore area of a stream located below the ordinary high-water mark without permission from the riparian landowner when it is necessary to exit the water to bypass an obstruction.
4. Upland and shore area access rules:
- It is not legal to enter uplands or exposed shore areas to hunt or retrieve game without permission of the landowner.
public waterway, private waterway, navigability, riparian rights, hunting access

Processing Game (3) -

Preparing Game Birds: Skinning and Plucking
Standard Source: The Complete Outdoorsmans Handbook
Skinned Game Birds:

• Some hunters prefer to skin their birds, mainly because it is easier.
• However, to purists, skinning game birds such as quail, ruffed grouse, pheasants, mallards, and canvasbacks is considered a bit of blasphemy.
-...
Skinned Game Birds:

• Some hunters prefer to skin their birds, mainly because it is easier.
• However, to purists, skinning game birds such as quail, ruffed grouse, pheasants, mallards, and canvasbacks is considered a bit of blasphemy.
• Skinning removes much of the fine flavor, particularly if the birds are to be roasted.
• The birds also dry out more when cooked.
• However, if the birds will be cooked in a sauce or gravy, or coated first, they can be skinned.
• Certainly such ducks as goldeneyes, bluebills, and the sea ducks are better skinned.
• Skinning removes some of the fat and the strong flavor with it.

How to Skin Game Birds:
1. Simply loosen the skin on the breast and start peeling.
2. Skinning ducks is another matter. You have to pull and cut.

Plucking Game Birds:

1. Prepare the bird: Ensure the bird has been shot cleanly and quickly, and handled properly after the shot.
2. Remove feathers manually: Take small "pinches" of feathers between the thumb and first two fingers and pull with the grain - the direction in which they lie - not against the grain to avoid tearing the skin.
3. Avoid large pinches: Do not take too large a pinch at once, as this can tear the skin.
4. Scalding for waterfowl (optional): For easier plucking of waterfowl, scald them in 145 degree water for about twenty seconds. Hold the bird by its head and dunk it into a bucket of scalding water.
5. Remove hair-like fuzz: After plucking, remove any remaining hair-like fuzz from waterfowl by singeing with a candle.
6. Wash and dry: After plucking and dressing, wash the birds carefully and dry them thoroughly.
7. Store or cook: The birds are then ready for the oven or freezer.

Note: Properly handled game can outclass supermarket birds in flavor and quality.
skinning, plucking, game birds, processing, hunting
Processing a Carcass for Meat Preservation
Standard Source: wdfw01807
Title: Processing a Carcass for Meat Preservation

Overview: Processing the carcass is the final step before you can enjoy some of the deliciousness that waits. You have two options: break it down yourself or take it to a local butcher shop...
Title: Processing a Carcass for Meat Preservation

Overview: Processing the carcass is the final step before you can enjoy some of the deliciousness that waits. You have two options: break it down yourself or take it to a local butcher shop that processes game animals.

### Option 1: Using a Butcher Shop
• There is a fee to have the carcass processed by the butcher shop, but it is cut just like beef.

### Option 2: Processing Yourself
• If you process it yourself, you may not get the beautiful steaks and roasts that come out of a butcher shop.
• Videos of how to process elk can be found online or purchased at local sporting goods stores and online stores.
• Remember that the meat has to be protected from the freezer and freezer burn. A vacuum sealer can help your game last for many months to come.
carcass processing, meat preservation, vacuum sealer, butcher shop, elk processing
Debone and Breast Out a Waterfowl
Reference Source: Basics of Waterfowling
### Procedure: Debone and Breast Out a Waterfowl

Step 1:
• Find a flat surface with good lighting.
• Place your duck on the cleaning surface with its head facing away from you and feet towards you.
• Gather a bag for feathers and carcass, along...
### Procedure: Debone and Breast Out a Waterfowl

Step 1:
• Find a flat surface with good lighting.
• Place your duck on the cleaning surface with its head facing away from you and feet towards you.
• Gather a bag for feathers and carcass, along with a sharp knife.
• Locate the base of the keel where the breast bone (sternum) and keel meet at the rear (caudal) of the bird.
• Pluck feathers from there to just beyond the wishbone (furcula) and out to where the first wing bone (humerus) attaches to the body. Alternatively, press firmly with your thumbs in the empty space above the wishbone and peel the skin away from the breast meat.
• Decide whether to leave the skin and fat attached or remove them entirely. This will determine how defeathered it needs to be.

Step 2:
• Pinch the skin between your thumb and pointer finger at the base of the keel.
• Slide your knife under the skin, following the keel up to the furcula.

Step 3:
• Remove the skin from the bird by peeling it away from the breast towards the wing. Use a knife if necessary or run your fingers underneath the skin in some cases.

Step 4:
• Starting at the furcula, cut down along the keel until you hit the sternum (breast bone).
• Cut along the keel until reaching the base of the keel where you started plucking.
• Turn your knife to begin cutting the breast meat away from the sternum. This cut will be away from the keel and remove the breast meat from the sternum.

Step 5:
• Continue cutting along with the breast bone up to the bone until reaching the wishbone (furcula).
• Finish by cutting along the wishbone to remove the breast meat.
• You should end up with the breast separate from the bone. There are two muscle groups: the breast (pectoralis muscle) and the tenderloin (supracoracoideus muscle), which is slightly lighter than the main breast meat. These can be loosely attached but are easily separated.
waterfowl processing, debone, breast out, hunting, field dressing, duck cleaning

Wildlife Biology (3) -

Understand Turkey Subspecies in Washington State
Standard Source: wdfw01803
Procedure:

Washington State is home to three turkey subspecies:

1. Eastern Wild Turkey:
- Transplanted from the eastern United States.
- Prefers mixed forests with evergreen and deciduous trees such as Douglas fir, western hemlock,...
Procedure:

Washington State is home to three turkey subspecies:

1. Eastern Wild Turkey:
- Transplanted from the eastern United States.
- Prefers mixed forests with evergreen and deciduous trees such as Douglas fir, western hemlock, western red cedar, red alder, and big leaf maple.
- Feeds on soft mast including Pacific dogwood, hawthorn, Oregon grapes, huckleberries, blackberries, cherries, and crab apples.
- Found in western Washington.
- Approximately 50 Eastern turkeys are harvested each year.
- Physical characteristics:
- Chestnut brown tail coverts.
- Tail feather tips are buff or chocolate brown.
- Adults may weigh 25 lbs. or more.

2. Merriam’s Turkey:
- Introduced to NE and south central Washington in 1960 for hunting purposes.

3. Rio Grande Turkey:
- Transplanted to suitable areas of the state after Merriam’s turkeys were introduced.

Distribution:
• Turkeys are found in five of the six regions recognized by WDFW.
• Region 4 is currently the only area without turkeys.
turkey subspecies, eastern turkey, merriam's turkey, rio grande turkey, washington state wildlife
Understand the Concept of Territory in Wildlife Management
Standard Source: Student manual 2005
Territory refers to the part of an animal’s home range that it will defend from others.

• This is important for:
- Understanding animal behavior and social structures.
- Managing wildlife populations effectively.
- Preventing conflicts...
Territory refers to the part of an animal’s home range that it will defend from others.

• This is important for:
- Understanding animal behavior and social structures.
- Managing wildlife populations effectively.
- Preventing conflicts between animals or with humans.
• Factors influencing territory size include:
- Availability of food and water.
- Breeding season.
- Species-specific behaviors.
• Monitoring territories helps in conservation efforts and habitat management.
territory definition, wildlife behavior, habitat management, animal defense
Understand the Definition of Species in Wildlife Management
Reference Source: Student manual 2005
A species is a group of like animals capable of interbreeding.

• This classification helps in:
- Identifying and protecting endangered species.
- Managing wildlife populations effectively.
- Ensuring genetic diversity within ecosystems.
-...
A species is a group of like animals capable of interbreeding.

• This classification helps in:
- Identifying and protecting endangered species.
- Managing wildlife populations effectively.
- Ensuring genetic diversity within ecosystems.
• Examples include:
- Different types of deer, birds, or fish that can interbreed.
- Species that are closely related but cannot interbreed are considered different species.
species definition, wildlife biology, interbreeding, conservation

Wildlife Identification (3) -

Identify Elk Species in Washington State
Reference Source: wdfw01807
Procedure:

1. Determine Location:
- Rocky Mountain elk are found east of the Cascade Crest.
- Roosevelt elk are located on the Olympic Peninsula.
- Hybrids of both species are common in the Cascade Range.

2. **Observe Physical...
Procedure:

1. Determine Location:
- Rocky Mountain elk are found east of the Cascade Crest.
- Roosevelt elk are located on the Olympic Peninsula.
- Hybrids of both species are common in the Cascade Range.

2. Observe Physical Characteristics:
- Rocky Mountain Elk: Slightly lighter in color, generally smaller than Roosevelt elk. Their antlers are more slender with longer tines and less palmation.
- Roosevelt Elk: Larger in size, darker legs and necks, and have a distinctive crown or three-point tip on their antlers.

3. Use Habitat Clues:
- Rocky Mountain elk ranges vary from 2,500 to 10,000 acres with distinct summering and wintering areas.
- Roosevelt elk ranges are smaller (1,500 to 4,000 acres) due to milder climates and more abundant food sources.
elk species,washington state,rocky mountain elk,roosevelt elk,habitat identification
Identify Merriam’s Turkey Habitat and Characteristics
Reference Source: wdfw01803
Merriam’s turkeys are native to the coniferous mountains and canyons of Colorado, New Mexico, and Arizona. They live in the canyons and forests of northeast and central Washington. These turkeys prefer forests that contain ponderosa pine, Douglas...
Merriam’s turkeys are native to the coniferous mountains and canyons of Colorado, New Mexico, and Arizona. They live in the canyons and forests of northeast and central Washington. These turkeys prefer forests that contain ponderosa pine, Douglas fir, western white pine, Engelman spruce, Douglas maple, willows, cottonwoods, and aspens.

In Washington, they eat grass leaves and seeds, ponderosa pine seeds, acorns, grasshoppers, forbs, and fruits like wild strawberries. They prefer to roost in Douglas fir and grand fir trees.

Physical characteristics:
• White or buff tail coverts
• Nearly white tail tips
• May weigh up to 25 lbs.
Merriam’s turkey, habitat, identification, characteristics, Washington
Identify Turkey Tracks and Footprints
Reference Source: wdfw01803
Turkey tracks can be found in snow, soft mud, or light dirt areas.

Track characteristics:
• About 4 inches long
• Three long toes that face forward, one small toe that faces back (about 1 inch)
• The middle toe of a **hen is...
Turkey tracks can be found in snow, soft mud, or light dirt areas.

Track characteristics:
• About 4 inches long
• Three long toes that face forward, one small toe that faces back (about 1 inch)
• The middle toe of a hen is smaller than 2 ½ inches, while the middle toe of a mature tom is more than 2 ½ inches.

Additional clues:
• When strutting, toms will leave wing drag marks.
turkey tracks, identification, footprints, hunting

Finding Hunting Access on Public Land in Washington State

To find hunting access on public land in Washington State:

1. Visit WDFW’s wildlife areas as a starting point for hunting.
2. Use the WDFW Hunting Access webpage at [https://wdfw.wa.gov/hunting/locations](https://wdfw.wa.gov/hunting/locations) to locate public hunting access on private lands.
3. Refer to the booklet on how to find hunting access, available through WDFW.
4. Use the Hunting Regulations Webmap at [https://geodataservices.wdfw.wa.gov/huntregs/](https://geodataservices.wdfw.wa.gov/huntregs/) to explore regulations data by selecting criteria or clicking on the map for hunt type information.
5. The webmap can also show public/private ownership, private lands hunting opportunities, water access sites, and satellite imagery.
6. This tool is available for use on smartphones.
7. Review the annual hunting prospects online at [https://wdfw.wa.gov/hunting/locations](https://wdfw.wa.gov/hunting/locations) to see WDFW biologist expectations for each of the 17 districts.

To gain access to private property for hunting:

1. Scout your desired area and locate lands you might want to hunt.
2. Knock on the door of the landowner to request permission.
3. If no house is visible, obtain landowner contact information from the county tax assessor office.
4. Be prepared for the possibility that a landowner may refuse access, and thank them for their time if they do.
5. Persist in your search to find lightly hunted areas and potentially make new friends.
6. Consider alternative options such as hunt clubs or hiring a guide who has access to private farms and ranches.

Reviewing Annual Hunting Prospects in Washington State

Understand the Differences Between Compound and Traditional Bows for Hunting
Reference Source: wdfw01807
Procedure:

1. Compound Bow Characteristics:
- These are the most popular hunting bows due to their ease of use.
- They have a mechanical let off, reducing draw weight by up to 85% at full draw.
- Compound bows are always strung and...
Procedure:

1. Compound Bow Characteristics:
- These are the most popular hunting bows due to their ease of use.
- They have a mechanical let off, reducing draw weight by up to 85% at full draw.
- Compound bows are always strung and ready to go.

2. Traditional Bow Characteristics:
- Traditional bows include longbows and recurve bows.
- These do not have mechanical let off when holding at full draw.
- They must be strung before use, as keeping them strung would cause the bow to lose power by forming to the strung position.

3. Effectiveness of Bows:
- Both compound and traditional bows are equally effective for harvesting animals if the shooter is proficient with them.
compound bow,traditional bow,hunting effectiveness

Muzzleloader Hunting (3) -

Legal Use of Projectiles and Buckshot in Muzzleloaders for Hunting in Washington State
Standard Source: wdfw01807
To legally hunt with a muzzleloader in Washington State:

Projectile Options:
• Any type of projectile is legal as long as it meets the caliber requirements mentioned above.
• Sabots are allowed, which can improve accuracy and...
To legally hunt with a muzzleloader in Washington State:

Projectile Options:
• Any type of projectile is legal as long as it meets the caliber requirements mentioned above.
• Sabots are allowed, which can improve accuracy and lethality.

Buckshot Use:
• If using a smooth-bored muzzleloader, you may shoot buckshot as long as the caliber is .60 or larger.
• Buckshot size #1 or larger is permitted for elk hunting in smoothbores of .60 caliber or larger.
projectile use, buckshot, muzzleloader legal requirements, washington state
Verify Muzzleloader Compliance for Big Game Hunting in Washington State
Standard Source: wdfw01807
To legally hunt big game with a muzzleloader in Washington State, ensure your firearm meets these specifications:

Muzzleloader Requirements:
1. The firearm must be loaded from the muzzle and use black powder or a black powder substitute as...
To legally hunt big game with a muzzleloader in Washington State, ensure your firearm meets these specifications:

Muzzleloader Requirements:
1. The firearm must be loaded from the muzzle and use black powder or a black powder substitute as recommended by the manufacturer.
2. A muzzleloading firearm is considered loaded if it contains a powder charge and projectile (shot or single projectile) with the barrel or breech capped or primed.

Caliber Requirements:
• For elk, the muzzleloader must be .40 caliber or larger. Buckshot size #1 or larger may be used in a smoothbore of .60 caliber or larger.
• For other big game, the muzzleloader must be .45 caliber or larger.

Shotgun Requirements:
• A double barrel, rifled or smooth-bored muzzleloading shotgun is allowed for all big game hunting except elk (where it must be .60 caliber or larger with buckshot size #1 or larger).
• Persons lawfully hunting small game may keep both barrels of a double barrel muzzleloader loaded.

Handgun Requirements:
• A muzzleloading handgun must have a single or double barrel of at least eight inches, be rifled, and be capable of being loaded with forty-five grains or more of black powder or substitute per the manufacturer's recommendations.
• For big game hunting, it must be .45 caliber or larger.

Legal Projectiles:
• Any type of projectile is allowed as long as it meets the caliber requirements mentioned above.
• Sabots are allowed.
muzzleloader, hunting regulations, washington state, big game, caliber requirements
Verify Muzzleloader Ignition and Sight Specifications for Hunting in Washington State
Standard Source: wdfw01807
To legally hunt with a muzzleloader during a muzzleloading season in Washington State, the firearm must meet these specifications:

Ignition Requirements:
• The ignition system must be wheel lock, matchlock, flintlock, or percussion. Primers...
To legally hunt with a muzzleloader during a muzzleloading season in Washington State, the firearm must meet these specifications:

Ignition Requirements:
• The ignition system must be wheel lock, matchlock, flintlock, or percussion. Primers designed for modern cartridges are legal.

Sight Requirements:
• Sights must be open, peep, or other open sight design. Fiber optic sights are allowed.
• Telescopic sights or any sight containing glass are prohibited.

Electrical Device Restrictions:
• It is unlawful to attach any electrical device or equipment to a muzzleloading firearm while hunting.

Double Barrel Restrictions:
• Persons lawfully hunting big game with a double barrel muzzleloader may only keep one barrel loaded at a time.
ignition system, sight requirements, washington state hunting regulations, muzzleloader

Crafting Darts for an Apocalyptic Blowgun

### Crafting Dart Shafts
Dart shafts can be made from a variety of materials, including:
• Bamboo skewers
• Small nails
• Sewing needles (for use on tiny game and/or with poison)
• Bike spokes
• Metal skewers
• Pencils
• Awls
• Scavenged wood or twigs

### Creating Fletching for Darts
If feather-like fletching is desired, you can utilize the following materials:
• Feathers
• Yarn
• Frayed cigarette filters
• Wadded up cloth of any kind
• Thistle down
• Down feathers
• Clematis fluff
• Usnea wads
• Fur wads

Any light, fluffy, and easily compacted natural or synthetic material can be used for fletching.

### Creating Cones for Darts
If cones are desired rather than fletching, materials include:
• Thimbles
• Duct tape cones (super easy and very durable)
• Paper cones
• Dart bodies that match the exact diameter of the blowgun, which require neither fletching nor a cone to propel them

For this type of dart, you can use:
• Small pieces of glue stick with needles or nails shoved through them
• Pencil erasers with needles embedded in the rubber
• Wooden dowels cut into small pieces with a nail or other stabbing implement attached

### Creating Broadhead Darts
If broadhead darts are desired in place of simple straight points, you can use:
• X-acto knife blades
• Razor blades
• Some dremel attachments
• Any of a thousand other small, broad, sharp civil objects
darts,blowgun,crafting,hunting,apocalyptic,feathering,cones,broadhead

### Steps to Create a Cone
1. Take a piece of duct tape and fold it into a cone shape.

### Materials Needed
• Duct tape

### Steps to Create a Cone
1. Take a piece of duct tape and fold it into a cone shape.
2. Secure the edges with additional duct tape or by pressing them together firmly.
3. Ensure that the cone is sturdy and compact enough to fit onto a dart shaft.
4. Attach the cone to the dart using glue, tape, or any other binding material available.

### Notes
• Duct tape cones are super easy to make and very durable.
• They provide excellent aerodynamics for blow darts.
duct tape,blow dart,cone,hunting,apocalyptic
Modifying Slingshots to Shoot Arrows
Standard Source: Paleo Pocalypse
### Materials Needed
• A ring or a large enough diameter for an arrow to pass through (metal, plastic, or any other material)
• Binding materials such as rubber tubing, strips of bicycle innertube, zip ties, rubber bands, stiff cordage, or similar...
### Materials Needed
• A ring or a large enough diameter for an arrow to pass through (metal, plastic, or any other material)
• Binding materials such as rubber tubing, strips of bicycle innertube, zip ties, rubber bands, stiff cordage, or similar items

### Steps to Modify the Slingshot
1. Find a ring or a large enough diameter for an arrow to pass through.
2. Situate and fix the ring in the center of the two forked arms of the slingshot using binding materials such as rubber tubing, strips of bicycle innertube, zip ties, rubber bands, stiff cordage, or any other suitable material.
3. The ring will act as an arrow rest, allowing you to draw the arrow to its full length without dropping it.

### Alternative Arrow Rests
• Thick, stiff wire can be used to create a custom arrow rest.
• Coat hangers or other pseudo-flexible wiry materials can also be fashioned into arrow rests.

### Notes
• This modification allows slingshots to fire full-size arrows and hunt larger game.
• The modified slingshot is sometimes referred to as a 'slingbow.'
slingshot,arrow modification,hunting,apocalyptic,slingbow

Trap Management (3) -

Commit to Checking Traps at Least Once Every Day
High Priority Source: Student manual 2005
Check live-restraining traps at first light each morning, as most furbearers are nocturnal.

If checking at daylight is not possible, check them as early in the day as possible.

Daily trap checks are a key responsibility of trappers....
Check live-restraining traps at first light each morning, as most furbearers are nocturnal.

If checking at daylight is not possible, check them as early in the day as possible.

Daily trap checks are a key responsibility of trappers. This differs from hunting, where hunters can choose when to hunt.

Do not skip trap checks due to bad weather or other problems. Consistency is critical for animal welfare.

If illness prevents you from checking traps, have another licensed trapper check your line.
• Ensure a licensed friend or family member knows:
- Where your sets are located
- How to check or remove your traps
• Keep notes and sketches showing trap locations
trap checking, animal welfare, daily checks, trapping responsibilities
Understand the Concept of a Trapline
Standard Source: Student manual 2005
Definition: All of the traps and sets in use at a given time by a single trapper.

Action Steps:
1. Plan and organize all trap placements for a specific trapping season or period.
2. Ensure that each trap is set according to legal...
Definition: All of the traps and sets in use at a given time by a single trapper.

Action Steps:
1. Plan and organize all trap placements for a specific trapping season or period.
2. Ensure that each trap is set according to legal regulations and ethical guidelines.
3. Monitor and maintain all traps within the trapline regularly.
4. Keep records of trap locations, types, and check times for effective management.
5. Adjust the trapline as needed based on wildlife activity and environmental conditions.
trapline, hunting trapping, trap management, wildlife monitoring
Check Traps Early Each Morning for Animal Welfare and Success
Standard Source: Student manual 2005
Reasons to check your traps early each morning:

• Animal welfare: Ensures trapped animals are not suffering unnecessarily.
• Prevent escape from live-restraining traps: Animals can be released if they are non-target species or if the...
Reasons to check your traps early each morning:

• Animal welfare: Ensures trapped animals are not suffering unnecessarily.
• Prevent escape from live-restraining traps: Animals can be released if they are non-target species or if the trap is malfunctioning.
• Release non-target animals: Allows for humane handling of unintended catches.
• Increase success rate: If you remove an animal and reset the trap, you may catch another one.
• Most furbearers are nocturnal: Checking traps early in the day increases your chances of catching them before they become active again.
• Reduce chances of fur or trap theft: Frequent checks make it less likely for others to steal your catch or equipment.
• Reduce chances of predation on your catch: Prevents predators from discovering and attacking your trapped animals.
• Shows responsibility to landowners: Demonstrates that you are a responsible trapper, which can help maintain good relationships with landowners and the public.
• Provides time for processing fur: Gives you time during the day or evening to skin or sell your catch.
• Allows for trap set remakes: If traps have been disturbed, you can reset them properly.

Additional benefits:
• Less chance of animals or traps being stolen.
• Less chance of predation on your catch.
• Less chance of an animal escaping from a restraining device.
• Less chance of an animal injuring itself or damaging its pelt.
check traps, early morning, trap checking, animal welfare, trapping success

Bird Hunting (2) -

Capturing Birds at Night
Standard Source: Animals For Food
Procedure:

1. Identify species of birds that can be taken by hand from their roost at night.
2. Approach the roost carefully and quietly to avoid startling the birds.
3. Use gloves or thick cloth to handle the birds to prevent injury.
4. Skin...
Procedure:

1. Identify species of birds that can be taken by hand from their roost at night.
2. Approach the roost carefully and quietly to avoid startling the birds.
3. Use gloves or thick cloth to handle the birds to prevent injury.
4. Skin fish-eating birds to improve their flavor.
5. Be aware of nesting seasons, as some species will not leave the nest even when approached.
6. Observe bird behavior to identify regular flyways from roosts to feeding areas, water sources, etc.
7. Set up nets across these flyways for trapping birds.
8. Use roosting sites and waterholes as prime locations for trapping or snaring birds.
capturing,birds,night,hunting,trapping
Harvesting Bird Eggs
Standard Source: Animals For Food
Procedure:

1. Locate a nesting site where birds are laying eggs.
2. Carefully approach the nest without disturbing it.
3. Remove all but two or three eggs from the clutch.
4. Mark the remaining eggs (e.g., with a small notch or using a...
Procedure:

1. Locate a nesting site where birds are laying eggs.
2. Carefully approach the nest without disturbing it.
3. Remove all but two or three eggs from the clutch.
4. Mark the remaining eggs (e.g., with a small notch or using a non-toxic marker).
5. The bird will continue to lay more eggs to fill the clutch.
6. Continue removing fresh eggs, leaving only the marked ones until no new eggs are being laid.
7. Collect the harvested eggs promptly and store them in a cool, dry place.
harvesting,bird,eggs,nesting

Traps For Small Game (2) -

Construct and Use a Squirrel Pole
Standard Source: Animals For Food
Title: Construct and Use a Squirrel Pole

Procedure:

1. Select Location: Place the squirrel pole in an area showing high squirrel activity.
2. Prepare the Pole: Use a long pole (length not specified) and position it against a...
Title: Construct and Use a Squirrel Pole

Procedure:

1. Select Location: Place the squirrel pole in an area showing high squirrel activity.
2. Prepare the Pole: Use a long pole (length not specified) and position it against a tree.
3. Install Wire Nooses:
- Attach several wire nooses along the top and sides of the pole.
- Ensure each noose is approximately 5 to 6 centimeters in diameter.
- Position the nooses about 2.5 centimeters away from the pole surface.
4. Position Nooses Strategically:
- Place the top and bottom wire nooses 45 centimeters from the top and bottom of the pole respectively.
- This prevents squirrels from getting their feet on a solid surface, which could allow them to chew through the wire.
5. Operation:
- Squirrels will eventually attempt to climb up or down the pole and get caught in one of the nooses.
- The struggling squirrel will fall from the pole and strangle.
6. Capture Multiple Squirrels:
- Once a squirrel is captured, other squirrels may follow due to curiosity and will also be caught.
squirrel pole,wire noose,trapping squirrels,hunting small game
Construct and Use a Treadle Spring Snare
Standard Source: Animals For Food
Title: Construct and Use a Treadle Spring Snare

Procedure:

1. Select Location: Set up the snare on a trail where small game is likely to pass.
2. Dig Hole in Trail: Dig a shallow hole in the trail.
3. Install Forked Sticks:
...
Title: Construct and Use a Treadle Spring Snare

Procedure:

1. Select Location: Set up the snare on a trail where small game is likely to pass.
2. Dig Hole in Trail: Dig a shallow hole in the trail.
3. Install Forked Sticks:
- Drive forked sticks (fork down) into the ground on each side of the hole, on the same side of the trail.
4. Span with Straight Sticks:
- Use two fairly straight sticks to span the two forks.
5. Position Sticks Over Hole:
- Place several sticks over the hole in the trail by positioning one end over the lower horizontal stick and the other on the ground on the opposite side of the hole.
6. Cover Hole with Sticks:
- Cover the hole with enough sticks so that prey must step on at least one to set off the snare.
7. Set Up Cordage System:
- Tie one end of a piece of cordage to a twitch-up or weight suspended over a tree limb.
- Bend the twitch-up or raise the suspended weight to determine where to tie a 5 centimeter long trigger.
8. Form Noose:
- Form a noose with the other end of the cordage and route it over the top of the sticks covering the hole.
9. Secure Trigger Stick:
- Place the trigger stick against the horizontal sticks and route the cordage behind the sticks so that tension from the power source holds it in place.
10. Adjust Horizontal Stick:
- Adjust the bottom horizontal stick to barely hold against the trigger.
11. Operation:
- When an animal steps on a stick across the hole, the bottom horizontal stick moves down, releasing the trigger and allowing the noose to catch the animal by the foot.
12. Use Channelization:
- Because of disturbances on the trail, animals may become wary. Use channelization techniques to guide them toward the snare.
treadle spring snare,hunting small game,trapping techniques
