Bow And Arrow (2) -

Construct Field-Expedient Arrows
Standard Source: Animals For Food
Materials Needed:
• Straight dry sticks
• Hot coals
• Cordage
• Arrowheads (bone, glass, metal, or rock)

Steps:
1. Select arrows from the straightest dry sticks available. The arrows should be about half as long as the bow.
2. Scrape each...
Materials Needed:
• Straight dry sticks
• Hot coals
• Cordage
• Arrowheads (bone, glass, metal, or rock)

Steps:
1. Select arrows from the straightest dry sticks available. The arrows should be about half as long as the bow.
2. Scrape each shaft smooth all around.
3. Straighten the shaft if necessary by heating it over hot coals and holding it straight until it cools.
4. Make arrowheads from bone, glass, metal, or pieces of rock.
5. Sharpen and fire harden the end of the shaft by holding it over hot coals without scorching or burning the wood.
6. Notch the ends of the arrows for the bowstring. Cut or file the notch; do not split it.
7. Optionally, add fletching (feathers) to the notched end of an arrow to improve flight characteristics.
arrow,hunting,trapping,survival,field-expedient
Construct a Field-Expedient Bow
Standard Source: Animals For Food
Materials Needed:
• A hardwood stick about one meter long, free of knots or limbs
• Cordage

Steps:
1. Select a hardwood stick about one meter long that is free of knots or limbs.
2. Carefully scrape the large end down until it has the same...
Materials Needed:
• A hardwood stick about one meter long, free of knots or limbs
• Cordage

Steps:
1. Select a hardwood stick about one meter long that is free of knots or limbs.
2. Carefully scrape the large end down until it has the same pull as the small end.
3. Examine the natural curve of the stick and ensure you scrape from the side that faces you to avoid breaking the bow on first use.
4. Prefer dead, dry wood over green wood for better performance.
5. To increase the pull, lash a second bow to the first, front to front, forming an "X" when viewed from the side.
6. Attach the tips of the bows with cordage and only use a bowstring on one bow.
bow,arrow,hunting,trapping,survival,field-expedient

Field Dressing (2) -

Dressing Big Game Immediately After Shooting
Standard Source: The Complete Outdoorsmans Handbook
Procedure:

1. Position the Animal: Lay the animal on its back with its head uphill if possible. Anchor it with rocks or tie its legs apart to trees.
2. Avoid Musk Glands: Do not touch the musk glands below the hocks, which are marked by...
Procedure:

1. Position the Animal: Lay the animal on its back with its head uphill if possible. Anchor it with rocks or tie its legs apart to trees.
2. Avoid Musk Glands: Do not touch the musk glands below the hocks, which are marked by tufts of dark hair and can taint your hands with an oily odor.
3. Loosen Anus and Rectum: Cut around the rump near the anus and rectum, then tie them firmly.
4. Make Abdominal Incision: Make a cut from the pelvic bone up to the rib cage, slowly with the edge of the knife pointing upwards. Use two fingers to lift the skin and abdominal wall away from internal organs as you cut. Avoid puncturing the intestines.
5. Loosen Penis: Cut around the penis and loosen it at this time.
6. Cut Diaphragm: Loosen the diaphragm by cutting close to the ribs, separating the stomach and intestines from the lungs and heart.
7. Remove Windpipe and Gullet: In deer-sized animals, reach up and grasp the windpipe and gullet, then cut them off as high as possible with your other hand. For elk or moose-sized animals, split the ribs with an ax or hatchet to access these organs.
8. Strip Viscera: Pull the windpipe and gullet backward to strip all viscera and organs from the carcass. Use occasional knife cuts to free internal organs from mesentery tissues as you pull.
9. Finish Rectum: Cut from the inside of the carcass near the pelvis and pull the anus through to finish loosening it.
10. Remove Offal: Drag the offal away, keeping the heart and liver.

Additional Steps:
• Wipe up any spilled matter from intestines and blood inside the carcass using a dry rag, moss, or leaves. If washing with water is necessary, dry the cavity thoroughly afterward to prevent spoilage.
• Cut away musk glands on the hind legs by pinching and pulling them upward while sliding a sharp knife under them and cutting along the base. Dispose of the removed glands and clean your hands and knife before touching meat.
• In mild weather (early fall), quarter moose or elk carcasses to allow quicker cooling. Hang the quarters in a cool, breezy spot away from the sun.
• Skin and quarter every big game animal while it is still warm for optimal meat preservation. Place the meat in loose cheesecloth and let it cool.
• Trim away badly shot meat to prevent spoilage.
• Keep all dressed carcasses off the ground to allow proper cooling. Prop elk or moose carcasses up with logs and poles if necessary.
• Hang deer-sized animals whole for a few days to fourteen days in a cool, breezy spot depending on temperature:
- If above fifty degrees: four to five days is fine.
- If between twenty and forty degrees: two weeks is excellent.
• Age wild meat properly like beef (aged at thirty-five degrees for two weeks) for tenderness.
• Trim away as much fat as possible, as it contains much of the gamy flavor that many people find objectionable.
dressing big game, field dressing, skinning, quartering, preserving meat, musk glands, removing viscera
Field Dressing an Elk
Standard Source: wdfw01807
Field Dressing is removing the internal organs from the animal. This should be done as soon as practical after the animal is recovered. Doing this sooner decreases the chances that the intestines, stomachs, and other areas of the elk that have...
Field Dressing is removing the internal organs from the animal. This should be done as soon as practical after the animal is recovered. Doing this sooner decreases the chances that the intestines, stomachs, and other areas of the elk that have digestive juices may rupture. Below are the steps for a recommended way to field dress an elk:

1. Roll the elk onto its back.
2. Find the bottom of the ribcage.
3. Insert your knife just below the bottom of the ribcage, angled towards the head.
4. Cut up the sternum towards the head, splitting the breastbone to the base of the neck.
5. Return to the bottom of the ribcage.
6. Place the knife between your index and middle fingers, while keeping the blade tip covered.
7. Slowly cut down the belly to the base of the anus.
- You can also use a knife with a gut hook if you have one.
- Make sure to leave proof of sex naturally attached to the carcass.
8. Cut around the anus and pull it out slightly.
9. Tie the anus in a knot or use some string around the exposed lower intestine, to make sure no droppings get on your meat.
10. Cut into the meat to expose the pelvis.
11. Using a bone saw or sharp hatchet, carefully split the pelvis where the lower intestine goes through the bone.
12. Move back up to the neck area.
13. Sever the windpipe as high as you can.
14. Tie this into a knot or close it with string.
15. If you want to keep and eat the heart, move down to the chest cavity and remove it.
- The heart and liver are eaten by some hunters. Place these organs into a game bag to keep them clean and cool.
16. Slowly cut the diaphragm that separated the heart and lungs from the intestines and stomach, making sure not to puncture any organs.
17. Move along the spine and detach the innards as close to the backbone as possible.
18. Gently pull the lower intestines out from between the split bone.
19. If you want to keep the liver, collect it from the entrails now.
20. Once all connections are severed, roll the animal on to its side and slide the entrails out.
field dressing, elk, hunting, organ removal, gutting, preservation

Waterfowling Permits (2) -

Obtain a Federal Duck Stamp
Standard Source: Basics of Waterfowling
Purpose: The federal duck stamp is required to hunt waterfowl in the United States since 1934. Ninety-eight cents of every duck stamp dollar goes directly into the Migratory Bird Conservation Fund to purchase vital wetland and wildlife habitat...
Purpose: The federal duck stamp is required to hunt waterfowl in the United States since 1934. Ninety-eight cents of every duck stamp dollar goes directly into the Migratory Bird Conservation Fund to purchase vital wetland and wildlife habitat or acquire conservation easements for inclusion in the National Wildlife Refuge System.

Procedure:
1. Purchase a federal duck stamp at a US Post Office.
2. If a physical stamp is not available, it will be sent to you by mail.
3. Ensure the stamp is properly affixed to your hunting license or documentation as required by law.
duck stamp, waterfowl hunting permit, federal duck stamp, Migratory Bird Conservation Fund
Obtain a Goose Permit

Procedure:
1. Purchase the goose permit for $3.
2. Ensure the permit is properly affixed to your hunting license...
Purpose: The goose permit allows you to pursue Canada geese, Snow Geese, Ross’ geese, Greater White-fronted geese, and Brant.

Procedure:
1. Purchase the goose permit for $3.
2. Ensure the permit is properly affixed to your hunting license or documentation as required by law.
3. Follow all state and federal regulations regarding waterfowl hunting seasons and bag limits.
goose permit, waterfowl hunting license, Canada geese permit, Snow Geese permit

Objective: Remember and apply the T-A-B-K principle to ensure safe hunting practices.

Steps:
1. Take time to learn about your surroundings, including terrain, weather conditions, and potential hazards.
2. Always be aware of your position relative to other hunters, landowners, and non-hunters in the field.
3. Be prepared with proper equipment (e.g., firearms, safety gear, first aid kit) and knowledge of local regulations.
4. Know what you are shooting at by using identification guides or practicing regularly to improve your ability to recognize game species.

Details:
• The T-A-B-K principle is a key component of hunter education programs and promotes safe hunting practices.
• Being aware of your environment helps prevent accidents and ensures that all users of the land are respected.
• Regular practice with identification guides improves accuracy and reduces the risk of shooting non-target species.

Equipment Selection (2) -

Choose Appropriate Boats for Waterfowl Hunting
Standard Source: Basics of Waterfowling
Consider the following when selecting a boat for waterfowl hunting:

• Canoes and Kayaks: Ideal for 1 or 2 persons on smaller water bodies, allowing access to areas larger boats or people on foot cannot reach. They are also useful for packing...
Consider the following when selecting a boat for waterfowl hunting:

• Canoes and Kayaks: Ideal for 1 or 2 persons on smaller water bodies, allowing access to areas larger boats or people on foot cannot reach. They are also useful for packing gear and retrieving birds in deep water.
• Larger Boats with Motors: Better suited for hunting with larger groups or on larger bodies of water. These boats allow hunters to haul more equipment.

Safety Tip: Always wear a life vest when using any type of watercraft.
boat selection, canoe, kayak, safety, life vest
Select Appropriate Hunting Boots Based on Climate and Activity
Reference Source: wdfw01803
Choosing the Right Hunting Boots:

1. Consider Insulation Needs:
- For cold weather hunting (e.g., eastern Washington), select boots with 2000 grams of insulation to keep feet warm.
- Avoid overly insulated boots for warmer conditions,...
Choosing the Right Hunting Boots:

1. Consider Insulation Needs:
- For cold weather hunting (e.g., eastern Washington), select boots with 2000 grams of insulation to keep feet warm.
- Avoid overly insulated boots for warmer conditions, such as turkey hunting in May, to prevent hot foot issues.

2. Prioritize Comfort and Support:
- Choose boots that provide ankle support to reduce fatigue during long hunts.
- Ensure the boots are waterproof to keep feet dry in wet or muddy conditions.

3. Match Boots to Hunting Conditions:
- If hunting in varied climates, consider versatile boots that can handle both cold and warm environments.
- Test boots before purchasing to ensure they fit well and allow for proper movement.
boots,hunting,insulation,warmth,cold weather

Game Processing (2) -

Processing Duck Breast Meat After Harvesting
Reference Source: Basics of Waterfowling
1. Bag the feathers and carcass and properly dispose of them.
2. Clean the breast meat under cold running water before eating or freezing.
3. To remove remaining feathers or down from a breast with skin still attached:
- Use a **flame...
1. Bag the feathers and carcass and properly dispose of them.
2. Clean the breast meat under cold running water before eating or freezing.
3. To remove remaining feathers or down from a breast with skin still attached:
- Use a flame (usually from a torch) to burn them off.
4. Check the meat for pellets and remove them, to avoid an unscheduled dentist appointment (many nontoxic loads are very hard and can chip a tooth if bitten too hard).
5. Properly store your meat for future meals.
duck processing, breast meat cleaning, feather removal, pellet removal, game storage

Deadfalls (2) -

Constructing Marten Trap Triggers
Standard Source: deadfallssnaresb00harduoft
Materials Required:
• A sturdy pole or log (approximately 6 to 8 feet long)
• Rope or chain
• Bait

Steps:
1. Select the Location: Choose an area where martens are likely to travel, such as near trails or feeding areas.
2. **Set Up the...
Materials Required:
• A sturdy pole or log (approximately 6 to 8 feet long)
• Rope or chain
• Bait

Steps:
1. Select the Location: Choose an area where martens are likely to travel, such as near trails or feeding areas.
2. Set Up the Pole: Insert the pole into the ground at an angle so that it is slightly leaning towards the bait location. The top of the pole should be about 3 feet above the ground.
3. Place Bait: Put the bait in a small hole dug into the ground near the base of the pole, about 1 foot away from the pole.
4. Set the Trigger Mechanism: Use a tripwire or stick to create a trigger mechanism that will release the deadfall bar when an animal steps on it.
5. Test the Trap: Gently pull the trigger to ensure that the deadfall bar falls correctly and lands on the bait.

Notes:
• Ensure the trap is camouflaged to avoid detection by animals or humans.
• Check the trap regularly to prevent unnecessary suffering to non-target animals.
marten trap triggers, hunting trap, animal trapping

Materials Required:
• A sturdy pole or log (approximately 6 to 8 feet long)

Steps:
1. Select the Location: Choose an area where animals are likely to travel, such as near trails or feeding areas.

4. Place Bait: Put the bait in a small hole dug into the ground near the base of the pole, about 1 foot away from the pole.
5. Set the Trigger Mechanism: Use a tripwire or stick to create a trigger mechanism that will release the deadfall bar when an animal steps on it.
6. Test the Trap: Gently pull the trigger to ensure that the deadfall bar falls correctly and lands on the bait.

• Ensure the trap is camouflaged to avoid detection by animals or humans.
• Check the trap regularly to prevent unnecessary suffering to non-target animals.

License Requirements (2) -

Selecting a Washington State Elk Hunting License and Transport Tag
Standard Source: wdfw01807
Procedure for Selecting a Washington State Elk Hunting License and Transport Tag:

1. Choose Your License Type:
- Consider the following license options:
- Deer, Elk, Bear, and Cougar License
- Deer, Elk, Bear, and Cougar...
Procedure for Selecting a Washington State Elk Hunting License and Transport Tag:

1. Choose Your License Type:
- Consider the following license options:
- Deer, Elk, Bear, and Cougar License
- Deer, Elk, Bear, and Cougar License with discounted Small game
- Deer and Elk License
- Deer and Elk License with discounted Small game license
- Deer License
- Deer License with discounted Small game license
- Elk License
- Elk License with discounted Small game license
- Bear License
- Bear License with discounted Small game license
- Cougar License
- Cougar License with discounted Small game license
- Discounts are available if you purchase a small game license and big game license together at the same time.

2. Declare Weapon Type and Area:
- When purchasing your elk license, declare:
- Weapon type: Archery, Muzzleloader, or Modern Firearm.
- Area: Eastern or Western Washington.
- This declaration is required to receive your transport tag.

3. Transport Tag Options:
- Transport tags are included in the cost of the license.
- If you haven’t decided on a weapon type or area when purchasing your license, you can choose to have your tag printed later.
- Ensure that you obtain your transport tag before heading out to hunt.

4. Season Restrictions:
- Hunters must only hunt during the season that matches their tag:
- Archery Season: Only archery equipment is allowed.
- Muzzleloader Season: Archery or muzzleloader equipment is allowed.
- Modern Firearm Season: Archery, muzzleloader, or modern firearm equipment is allowed.
- The hunting seasons for elk are divided by weapon type and area to prevent overcrowding.

5. Season Length Determination:
- WDFW sets the season length based on:
- Harvest rates
- Seasonal animal surplus projections.
Washington State, elk hunting license, transport tag, weapon type, area selection, season restrictions
Obtain Required Hunting Licenses for Turkey Hunting in Washington State
Standard Source: wdfw01803
Procedure:

1. Purchase a Small Game License:
- This license allows hunting of small game such as upland birds, rabbits, and unclassified animals like coyote.
2. Purchase a Turkey Transport Tag:
- Required to legally harvest...
Procedure:

1. Purchase a Small Game License:
- This license allows hunting of small game such as upland birds, rabbits, and unclassified animals like coyote.
2. Purchase a Turkey Transport Tag:
- Required to legally harvest turkeys in Washington State.
3. Consider purchasing a Combination License (Small Game + Big Game):
- This provides a discount if purchased together at the same time.
- Allows hunting of big game species, small game, and unclassified animals.

Limits on Harvesting Turkeys:
• You may harvest up to seven turkeys during the entire hunting season.
• Spring Season (April–May):
- Up to three turkeys can be harvested.
- In eastern Washington, only two turkeys may be taken, with one turkey limited to Chelan, Kittitas, and Yakima counties combined.
- In western Washington (outside Klickitat County), only one turkey may be taken per year.
- In Klickitat County, you may take up to two turkeys during the spring season.
• Early Fall Season:
- Additional turkeys can be harvested in some GMUs (Game Management Units), with a limit of three additional turkeys.
• Late Fall Season:
- One more turkey may be taken in the late fall season.

Important Notes:
• Tags are not tied to specific seasons, so tags purchased during spring can be used for fall hunts.
• Once purchased, tags cannot be refunded, so it is recommended to purchase as you hunt.
• All turkey tag purchasers must report their harvest by January 31 each year. Reporting instructions are provided at the end of this booklet.
turkey hunting license, small game license, turkey transport tag, combination license, hunting regulations

Rare species are animals that are very uncommon, even in their favored habitat.

• These species require special attention to ensure their survival.
• If you encounter a rare species:
1. Do not attempt to trap or handle it.
2. Keep your distance and avoid disturbing its environment.
3. Report the sighting to local wildlife authorities.
• Rare species may be protected by law, so it is important to follow regulations.
• Avoid activities that could harm their habitat or disrupt their natural behavior.
rare species, wildlife protection, legal compliance, conservation

To ensure ethical hunting practices:

1. Reflect on your personal ethics and compare them to what the public may consider ethical.
2. Strive to raise your ethical standards to the highest level, as encouraged by WDFW.
3. Avoid unethical behaviors such as:
- Shooting at birds outside your equipment’s effective range.
- Shooting birds while on land, water, or foliage.
- Hogging all of the shots from your hunting party.
- Shooting a deer or elk at 1000 yards.
- Using a bow or firearm that is not properly sighted in.
4. Ethical hunting improves public perception and benefits the sport.

Muzzleloader Safety (2) -

Safe Handling and Storage of Muzzleloader Equipment
High Priority Source: wdfw01807
To ensure safe handling and storage of your muzzleloader equipment:

Safety Practices:
1. Never fill the muzzleloader directly from the powder can flask or powder horn, as it could spark and ignite the powder in the container.
2. **Mark your...
To ensure safe handling and storage of your muzzleloader equipment:

Safety Practices:
1. Never fill the muzzleloader directly from the powder can flask or powder horn, as it could spark and ignite the powder in the container.
2. Mark your ramrod when the muzzleloader is empty so you can confirm it is empty upon storage.
3. Seat the wad and shot directly on top of the powder charge to ensure proper loading.
4. Store powder and percussion caps separately, in dry and cool places.

Handling a Hang Fire:
• If a hang fire occurs (the trigger is pulled, the percussion cap ignites, but the firearm does not fire), keep the muzzle pointed in a safe direction for at least 30 seconds.
• If it still does not fire after 30 seconds, put on another cap or re-prime the pan, and fire again.
• Ensure the nipple is clean on percussion locks to prevent hang fires.
muzzleloader safety, hang fire, powder storage, safe handling

Powder Selection:

**Loading...
To ensure the proper function and safety of your muzzleloader:

Powder Selection:
• Use only black powder or a black powder substitute that is rated for your muzzleloader. Using the wrong type of powder can be disastrous.

Loading Practices:
• Always follow the manufacturer's recommendations for loading procedures, including the amount and type of powder to use.
• Ensure the powder charge is properly seated, followed by a wad and projectile.
black powder, muzzleloader loading, powder substitute, safe use

Firearms (2) -

Regulations for Using Handguns to Hunt Big Game in Washington
Standard Source: wdfw01807
Procedure:

1. Use a handgun with a minimum barrel length of 4 inches per manufacturer’s specification.
2. Fire a minimum .24 caliber centerfire cartridge for big game (except cougar).
3. Cougar may be hunted with a .22 caliber centerfire...
Procedure:

1. Use a handgun with a minimum barrel length of 4 inches per manufacturer’s specification.
2. Fire a minimum .24 caliber centerfire cartridge for big game (except cougar).
3. Cougar may be hunted with a .22 caliber centerfire handgun.
4. Rimfire handguns are not legal for big game hunting.
5. Modern handguns cannot be used to hunt big game during muzzleloading firearm seasons or to dispatch wounded big game during those seasons.
6. Handguns can be carried for personal protection, but they must not be used for hunting purposes outside the specified regulations.
handgun regulations, big game hunting, washington hunting laws

2. Popular calibers for elk hunting include:
- .243
- .270
- 7mm mag
- .308
- 30-06
- .300 mag
3. Ensure the rifle is capable of firing...
Procedure:

1. Choose a rifle with a minimum .24 caliber (6mm) centerfire cartridge.
2. Popular calibers for elk hunting include:
- .243
- .270
- 7mm mag
- .308
- 30-06
- .300 mag
3. Ensure the rifle is capable of firing bullets that transfer sufficient energy to the target.
4. For elk hunting, use bullets weighing between 180 grains or more for optimal performance.
5. Consider bullet design:
- Polymer-tipped bullets may mushroom faster and wider than lead-tipped bullets.
- All-copper bullets are popular due to their effectiveness in big game hunting.
6. Ensure the rifle is legal for use during modern firearm seasons.
rifle caliber, elk hunting, bullet weight, bullet design, firearms regulations

Selecting and Maintaining a Hunting Knife for Field Dressing Elk
Standard Source: wdfw01807
Materials Needed:
• Sharp knife
• Knife sharpener
• Bone saw (optional)

Steps:
1. Choose the Right Knife: Select a knife that is comfortable in your hand and suitable for the tasks you will be performing, such as field dressing or...
Materials Needed:
• Sharp knife
• Knife sharpener
• Bone saw (optional)

Steps:
1. Choose the Right Knife: Select a knife that is comfortable in your hand and suitable for the tasks you will be performing, such as field dressing or skinning.
2. Keep the Blade Sharp: A sharp knife is essential for efficient cutting. Use a knife sharpener regularly to maintain the edge of the blade.
3. Use Appropriate Knives for Specific Tasks:
- Skinning Knives: Choose a knife with a deep belly in the blade to avoid cutting through the hide. Some may come with a gut hook for easy access to the chest cavity.
- Boning Knives: Use straighter knives with thinner blades for deboning meat from bones.
- Caping Knives: Opt for shorter blades for fine detail work when removing skin from the head region of big game animals.
4. Use a Bone Saw for Pelvis Cuts: A bone saw is recommended for cutting through the pelvis to facilitate easier removal of internal organs without pulling them through the body cavity.

Game Hunting (2) -

Create a Deer Dummy for Hunting Games
Standard Source: Boy Scout Handbook 1911
To create a realistic deer dummy for hunting games:

1. Construct the frame: Use a wire frame to form the basic shape of a deer.
2. Wrap with hay: Wrap soft hay around the wire frame until it reaches the proper size and shape.
3. **Cover...
To create a realistic deer dummy for hunting games:

1. Construct the frame: Use a wire frame to form the basic shape of a deer.
2. Wrap with hay: Wrap soft hay around the wire frame until it reaches the proper size and shape.
3. Cover with burlap: Cover the entire structure with open burlap for a natural texture.
4. Add details: Use touches of white and black paint or fabric to make the dummy more realistic.

Alternative method if time is limited:
• Use a sack stuffed with hay as the body.
• Add a smaller sack at one end to represent the head and neck.
• Place the dummy on four thin sticks for support.
• Mark the side of the dummy with a large oval (representing the body) and a smaller oval over the heart area.

Note: Bows and arrows only are used to shoot this deer dummy.
deer dummy,hunting game,boy scout handbook,wire frame,burlap,hay
Safety Rules for Deer Hunting Game
Standard Source: Boy Scout Handbook 1911
To ensure safety during the deer hunting game:

1. No standing in front of firing line: It is strictly forbidden for any hunter to stand in front of the firing line.
2. Positioning: All hunters must be behind the line on which the shooter...
To ensure safety during the deer hunting game:

1. No standing in front of firing line: It is strictly forbidden for any hunter to stand in front of the firing line.
2. Positioning: All hunters must be behind the line on which the shooter stands.
3. Dummy placement: The dummy can be left standing or lying, and may even be hidden behind a solid tree trunk.
4. Skill development: As players become more skilled in following trails, the use of corn can be reduced or eliminated, relying solely on iron hoof marks for realism.

Improvised Weapons (2) -

Creating Improvised Arrowheads and Fletching
Standard Source: Paleo Pocalypse
Materials Needed:
• Wooden dowels, tent poles, walking/skiing poles, or any straight, durable, lightweight material for shafts
• Duct tape, masking tape, or scavenged feathers for fletching
• Knapped glass, ceramic (tiles, toilets), flattened...
Materials Needed:
• Wooden dowels, tent poles, walking/skiing poles, or any straight, durable, lightweight material for shafts
• Duct tape, masking tape, or scavenged feathers for fletching
• Knapped glass, ceramic (tiles, toilets), flattened and sharpened spoons, nails, coins, bullet casings, or scrap metal for arrowheads

Steps to Create Improvised Arrows:
1. Select the Shaft Material: Choose a straight, durable, lightweight material such as wooden dowels, tent poles, walking/skiing poles, or any similar object.
2. Prepare the Arrowhead:
- For glass or ceramic arrowheads: Knap (sharpen) the material to create a pointed tip.
- For metal-based arrowheads: Flatten and sharpen spoons, nails, coins, or bullet casings into a tapered point.
- Alternatively, use scrap metal materials to form an improvised arrowhead.
3. Attach the Arrowhead: Securely attach the arrowhead to one end of the shaft using strong adhesive (e.g., epoxy) or by wrapping with duct tape.
4. Add Fletching:
- Use duct tape, masking tape, or scavenged feathers to create fletching at the other end of the shaft.
- Wrap the tape around the shaft in a helical pattern or attach feathers using adhesive for better aerodynamics.
5. Test and Adjust: Ensure that the arrow is balanced and flies straight when tested with a bow or by blowing through it (for blowguns).

Notes:
• Use caution when handling sharp objects like glass, ceramic, or metal to avoid injury.
• For fletching, use materials that provide good aerodynamic properties for accuracy.
arrowheads,fletching,improvised weapons,hunting,post-apocalyptic survival
Fabricating a Blowgun from Scavenged Materials
Standard Source: Paleo Pocalypse
Materials Needed:
• Hollow pipes or tubes (e.g., copper pipe, PVC, steel pipe)
• Paints, pigments, camouflage tape (optional for camouflaging)

Steps to Fabricate a Blowgun:
1. Select the Tube Material: Choose a firm, hollow tube such as...
Materials Needed:
• Hollow pipes or tubes (e.g., copper pipe, PVC, steel pipe)
• Paints, pigments, camouflage tape (optional for camouflaging)

Steps to Fabricate a Blowgun:
1. Select the Tube Material: Choose a firm, hollow tube such as copper pipe, PVC, or steel pipe.
2. Cut the Tube to Desired Length: Use a saw or cutting tool to cut the tube to your preferred length (typically around 30-40 inches for effective use).
3. Smooth Edges: Ensure that the cut edges are smooth and free of sharp burrs to avoid injury when using the blowgun.
4. Camouflage (Optional):
- Paint or apply pigments to the tube to match your surroundings.
- Wrap the blowgun with camouflage tape, such as Real-tree duct tape, for better concealment in forested areas.
5. Test the Blowgun: Insert a dart into one end and blow through the other to ensure it functions properly.

Notes:
• Use caution when handling sharp tools during the cutting process.
• Ensure that the tube is not too narrow or too wide for the darts you intend to use.
blowgun,fabrication,post-apocalyptic survival,hunting,improvised weapons

Objective: Use your state’s trapping regulation brochure to determine legal restrictions for trapping nuisance animals.

Procedure:
1. Obtain a copy of your state’s current trapping regulation brochure.
2. Locate the section that discusses regulations related to trapping nuisance animals (e.g., animals damaging crops, livestock, or property).
3. Identify and record the following information:
- Under what conditions can nuisance animals be trapped or removed?
- Who may trap nuisance animals (landowners, licensed trappers, etc.)
- What special licenses or training are required for trapping nuisance animals
- What record keeping and reporting is required after trapping
- What type of fees may be charged for trapping nuisance animals
4. Note any recommendations provided in the brochure (e.g., handling problems during trapping season when pelts are prime).
5. Record this information in your notes or documentation for future reference.

Important: Always ask for permission before trapping on private land, and treat landowners with respect.

Trap Evaluation (2) -

Evaluate BMP Traps Based on Animal Welfare Criteria
Standard Source: Student manual 2005
To evaluate BMP (Best Management Practices) traps based on animal welfare, follow these steps:

1. Assess Injury Rates: Researchers tested live restraining traps for injuries to furbearers using two methods:
- One system evaluated...
To evaluate BMP (Best Management Practices) traps based on animal welfare, follow these steps:

1. Assess Injury Rates: Researchers tested live restraining traps for injuries to furbearers using two methods:
- One system evaluated specific injuries.
- The other grouped the injuries into categories from mild to severe.
2. Injury Thresholds: BMP approved traps must have a low rate of injuries to the furbearing animals being studied.
3. Veterinary Examination: Wildlife veterinarians examined thousands of trapped furbearers for different types of injuries.
4. Acceptable Injury Levels: Traps that resulted in no, little, or moderate injury to at least 70% of the animals trapped were considered acceptable.

Note: Traps that caused severe injuries or failed to meet these standards were not recommended.
BMP,animal welfare,trap evaluation,injury rates,furbearers

Trap Identification (2) -

Identify Kill-Type and Live-Restraining Traps
Standard Source: Student manual 2005
Identify kill-type and live-restraining traps:

• Kill-type traps are designed to kill furbearers. The most common type is the *body-gripping trap*, also known as a *Conibear™ type trap*.
• Live-restraining traps are designed to capture...
Identify kill-type and live-restraining traps:

• Kill-type traps are designed to kill furbearers. The most common type is the *body-gripping trap*, also known as a *Conibear™ type trap*.
• Live-restraining traps are designed to capture an animal alive and unharmed. Common types include:
- Foothold traps
- Cable devices
- Cage traps

These traps allow for the release of non-target animals.

Note: State regulations may place limits on trap sizes and locations where sets can be made. Always know and follow your state's regulations.

Live-restraining cage traps are used to capture animals without causing harm, making them ideal for trapping near buildings or in areas frequented by pets. Animals such as opossums, fisher, and weasels can be caught in these traps, although foxes and coyotes may avoid them.

Kill-type submarine or colony traps are a type of cage trap designed specifically for use in submersion sets, typically for muskrats and mink. These traps are called *colony traps* because they allow the capture of an entire family, or colony, of muskrats at one time.

Important Considerations:
• Always check your state's regulations before using a colony trap.
• Use caution when handling these traps to ensure safe and effective trapping.
kill-type traps, live-restraining traps, body-gripping trap, Conibear™ type trap, cage traps, colony traps, submersion sets

1. Understand Legal Variations: Legal traps vary from state to state. Trapping regulations may change each year within a state.
2. Learn Trap Types: Students must...
To identify types of traps and prepare them for use, follow these steps:

1. Understand Legal Variations: Legal traps vary from state to state. Trapping regulations may change each year within a state.
2. Learn Trap Types: Students must demonstrate the ability to identify different types of traps used in trapping practices.
3. Prepare Traps for Use:
- Ensure that all components are intact and functioning properly.
- Check for wear or damage that could affect performance or safety.
4. Safely Operate Traps:
- Follow proper handling procedures to avoid injury.
- Set traps in appropriate locations based on target species and BMP guidelines.

Note: Proper identification, preparation, and safe operation of traps are essential for effective trapping practices.

Trap Setups (2) -

Set Up a Submersion Trap for Muskrats, Mink, River Otters, Nutria, and Beaver
Standard Source: Student manual 2005
Submersion Set Instructions:

To ensure death of trapped animals (muskrats, mink, river otters, nutria, and beaver), use a submersion set with slide-wire “drowners”:

1. Use aircraft cable (3/32” or 1/8”) or strong wire (11 or 12 gauge) with a...
Submersion Set Instructions:

To ensure death of trapped animals (muskrats, mink, river otters, nutria, and beaver), use a submersion set with slide-wire “drowners”:

1. Use aircraft cable (3/32” or 1/8”) or strong wire (11 or 12 gauge) with a one-way sliding lock.
2. Stake one end of the cable near shore where the trap is set.
3. Stake or anchor the other end in deep water:
- Minimum 24 inches for muskrats
- Minimum 42 inches for beaver
4. The one-way sliding lock allows the animal to swim toward deeper water but not back to shore.
5. The weight of the trap forces it to submerge.

Alternative Method for Muskrat Trapping:
• Stake the trap in deep water. The weight of a long-spring trap will submerge a muskrat.

Note: Animals do not drown; they asphyxiate due to inability to breathe underwater.
submersion trap, drowning set, muskrat trapping, mink trapping, river otter trapping, nutria trapping, beaver trapping

Trap Anchoring (2) -

Anchor Traps Using Stakes, Crossstakes, Cable Stakes, Drags, and Grapples
Standard Source: Student manual 2005
Proper Anchoring of Traps on Land

Trappers must know how to anchor their traps properly to hold furbearers and prevent injury. Traps are normally anchored with stakes, but sometimes drags or grapples are used.

• Steel Stakes: Recommended...
Proper Anchoring of Traps on Land

Trappers must know how to anchor their traps properly to hold furbearers and prevent injury. Traps are normally anchored with stakes, but sometimes drags or grapples are used.

• Steel Stakes: Recommended for anchoring. They should be 18–24 inches in length under most conditions. In sandy soils, even longer stakes may be required.
• Cross-stake Method: A more secure method for fox and coyotes to prevent the animal from pulling it out. Use a double-stake swivel with the cross-staking method for better hold.
• Cable Stakes: Another option, but they take more time to dig out when removing your set.
• Drags and Grapples: Used in some terrain where animals can find cover nearby.

Swivels: Stake swivels and two or more chain swivels allow an animal to move freely without twisting the chain down to a point where it is easy for the animal to pull out of the trap.

Shock Springs: Used on trap chains to help hold animals and prevent injuries. Use high-quality shock springs of sufficient strength for the animals being trapped.
stake, cross-stake, cable stake, drag, grapple, trap anchoring, trapper education, hunting
Identify Trap Anchoring Systems
Standard Source: Student manual 2005
Trap anchoring systems are used to secure traps and prevent animals from pulling them free. Common methods include:

• Single stakes: Made of metal, typically 18–24 inches in length, suitable for most conditions. Longer stakes may be...
Trap anchoring systems are used to secure traps and prevent animals from pulling them free. Common methods include:

• Single stakes: Made of metal, typically 18–24 inches in length, suitable for most conditions. Longer stakes may be needed in sandy soils.
• Cross stakes: Used for stronger animals like foxes and coyotes to prevent them from pulling the trap free.
• Earth anchors: Attached to a cable, driven into the ground with a tool. They are very strong but must be dug out when removing the set.
• Drags: Allow an animal to move a short distance. Common types include heavy tree limbs.
• Grapples: Metal devices secured to traps with chains. They work like drags but are lighter and dig into the ground or vegetation, restricting movement.

Shock springs can be used in combination with chains, stakes, drags, and grapples. One or two shock springs help hold animals such as coyotes, which may lunge when trapped.
trap anchoring systems, single stakes, cross stakes, earth anchors, drags, grapples, shock springs, coyote trapping, trap security

Setting a Blocking Pole Above a Channel Set for Beaver
Standard Source: Student manual 2005
Procedure:

1. Identify the location of the channel where beaver activity is observed.
2. Select a sturdy pole or branch that can withstand water pressure and ice conditions.
3. Position the blocking pole above the channel set to guide beaver...
Procedure:

1. Identify the location of the channel where beaver activity is observed.
2. Select a sturdy pole or branch that can withstand water pressure and ice conditions.
3. Position the blocking pole above the channel set to guide beaver movement toward the trap.
4. Ensure the pole is securely placed so it does not shift or fall into the water.
5. Check the placement regularly to ensure it remains effective and safe for both trapper and animal.

Details:
• Use a straight, strong limb that can withstand ice pressure.
• The pole should be positioned at an angle that directs beaver toward the trap without obstructing their natural path.
• Avoid using poles that may decay quickly in water or ice conditions.

blocking pole,channel set,trap setting,beaver trapping
Use Sticks and Rocks to Guide Target Animals to a Trap
Standard Source: Student manual 2005
Purpose: Use natural materials to guide target animals toward traps while deterring non-target species.

Procedure:
1. Muskrat Set at Stream Edge: Place branches vertically above the trap on the stream bank so that muskrats can pass...
Purpose: Use natural materials to guide target animals toward traps while deterring non-target species.

Procedure:
1. Muskrat Set at Stream Edge: Place branches vertically above the trap on the stream bank so that muskrats can pass below them, but ducks and water birds are deterred by the obstruction.
2. Raccoon Rock Cubby: Create a small rock cubby or enclosure around the trap to keep dogs from approaching it.
3. Jump Pole for Deer: Install a jump pole immediately above a cable device so that deer are forced to jump over it. Ensure the pole is not too high, as deer may go underneath if it's too tall.
4. Foot Guides for Land Sets: Use small stones placed near the trap to guide animals to step on the trigger mechanism.

Notes:
• Jump poles should be designed to fall over after use to prevent entanglement.
• Ensure that all materials used are secure and do not pose a risk of injury to target or non-target animals.
sticks, rocks, trap guidance, selective trapping, jump pole, foot guides, raccoon cubby, deer diversion

Trapping Practices (2) -

Advantages of Starting with Water Sets for New Trappers
Standard Source: Student manual 2005
Why New Trappers Should Start with Water Sets

New trappers should begin with water sets using kill-type traps or submersion trapping techniques for the following reasons:

1. Lower Startup Costs: Water sets require less expensive...
Why New Trappers Should Start with Water Sets

New trappers should begin with water sets using kill-type traps or submersion trapping techniques for the following reasons:

1. Lower Startup Costs: Water sets require less expensive equipment compared to dry land sets.
2. Selective Targeting of Semi-Aquatic Species: Water sets are more selective, targeting species like muskrats and mink while avoiding most non-target animals.
3. Humane and Efficient Capture: Using body-gripping traps or properly constructed submersion sets ensures the furbearer’s death, eliminating the need to dispatch animals manually. These methods also reduce the likelihood of escape.

Additionally, water trapping avoids the use of foothold traps that may require dispatching live animals, making it a more humane and efficient method for beginners.
water sets, new trappers, kill-type traps, submersion trapping, humane capture
Benefits of Using BMP-Specific Traps for Water Sets
Standard Source: Student manual 2005
Why Use BMP-Specific Traps for Water Sets?

Trappers, biologists, veterinarians, and researchers have evaluated many traps. BMP (Best Management Practice) traps have been tested for the following key factors:

1. Animal Welfare: Ensures...
Why Use BMP-Specific Traps for Water Sets?

Trappers, biologists, veterinarians, and researchers have evaluated many traps. BMP (Best Management Practice) traps have been tested for the following key factors:

1. Animal Welfare: Ensures humane treatment of furbearers during trapping.
2. Selectivity for Furbearers: Increases the likelihood of catching target species while minimizing non-target captures.
3. Trapper Safety Concerns: Reduces risks to trappers during trap setting and retrieval.

Using BMP-compliant traps is essential for ethical trapping, conservation efforts, and ensuring compliance with wildlife management regulations.
BMP traps, water sets, animal welfare, trapper safety, furbearer selectivity

Ice Trapping Techniques (2) -

Making a Set Under the Ice for Beaver
High Priority Source: Student manual 2005
Procedure:

1. Ensure you have experience with ice trapping or work with an experienced trapper.
2. Always trap with a friend or family member, especially when using large body-gripping traps on ice.
3. Locate a suitable area of open water where...
Procedure:

1. Ensure you have experience with ice trapping or work with an experienced trapper.
2. Always trap with a friend or family member, especially when using large body-gripping traps on ice.
3. Locate a suitable area of open water where beaver activity is observed.
4. Drill holes in the ice to create access points for setting the trap.
5. Use a stabilizer or support system to secure the trap under the ice.
6. Place the trap so that it is fully submerged and positioned to catch beaver as they swim through the area.
7. Secure the trap with crosspoles above the ice to prevent loss of the trap.
8. Bait the trap with fresh poplar or other suitable bait for beaver.
9. Ensure the trigger is set on the bottom of the trap to reduce fur damage.
10. Monitor the trap regularly and check it frequently to ensure it remains functional and safe.

Details:
• This technique should not be attempted by beginners without proper guidance.
• Use a dry limb for the mounting pole to prevent beaver from eating it.
• Ensure that all components are securely fastened to avoid loss of equipment or injury to the animal.
• Always prioritize safety and follow local trapping regulations.
ice trapping,beaver set,trap setting,safety guidelines

Procedure:

1. Construct the teepee set on land using sturdy materials such as poles and branches.
2. Securely attach a small platform to a large limb or pole.
3. Wire a double long-spring to the board, ensuring it is properly attached and secure.
4. Attach the trap chain near the bottom of the limb to prevent beaver from reaching the surface.
5. Run a wire from the chain to the crosspole above the ice to ensure stability.
6. Bait the trap with fresh poplar or other suitable bait for beaver.
7. Position the teepee set under the ice in an area where beaver activity is observed.
8. Ensure that the trigger of the trap is set on the bottom to reduce fur damage.
9. Monitor the trap regularly and check it frequently to ensure it remains functional and safe.

Details:
• The teepee set should be constructed with strong materials to withstand ice pressure.
• Use a dry limb for the mounting pole to prevent beaver from eating it.
• Ensure that all components are securely fastened to avoid loss of equipment or injury to the animal.
• Always prioritize safety and follow local trapping regulations.

Humanely Kill a Furbearing Animal Using a Rifle
High Priority Source: Student manual 2005
Procedure:

1. Select the appropriate method based on circumstances:
- If the animal has bitten someone or rabies is suspected, choose a heart shot.
- For skunks, always shoot in the heart to avoid potential rabies risk from the...
Procedure:

1. Select the appropriate method based on circumstances:
- If the animal has bitten someone or rabies is suspected, choose a heart shot.
- For skunks, always shoot in the heart to avoid potential rabies risk from the brain.
- For other furbearers, shooting in the head with a .22 rifle is generally acceptable.

2. Ensure safety and compliance:
- Trappers must be graduates of hunter education courses.
- Follow all firearm safety rules on the trapline.

3. Use proper technique:
- Aim for the head or heart depending on the situation.
- Ensure a quick, humane kill to minimize suffering.

4. Plan ahead:
- Decide on the method of dispatch before setting traps to reduce stress and ensure efficiency.

Procedure:

1. Select the appropriate tool:
- Use a heavy wooden or metal object for striking.

- Strike smaller furbearers such as raccoons, opossums, and foxes at the base of the skull.

- Apply sufficient force to kill or render the animal unconscious immediately.

4. Alternative method (if needed):
- Place your foot over the heart and chest area and compress these organs to cause death.

Procedure:

1. Prepare the tool:
- Use a large piece of plywood with a notch cut in the side.

- Keep the plywood between you and the animal for protection.

- Place the notch over the animal’s leg to hold it steady.

Release a Non-Target Animal from a Foothold Trap Using a Catchpole
Standard Source: Student manual 2005
Procedure:

1. Ensure personal safety:
- Prioritize your own safety above all else.

2. Use the catchpole correctly:
- Slip the noose over the animal’s head and pull it down snugly to prevent escape.

3. **Steer the animal away...
Procedure:

1. Ensure personal safety:
- Prioritize your own safety above all else.

2. Use the catchpole correctly:
- Slip the noose over the animal’s head and pull it down snugly to prevent escape.

3. Steer the animal away from the trap:
- Use the pole to guide the animal’s head away from the trap while you depress the levers or springs with your feet.

4. Release the noose:
- Once the animal’s foot is free, position yourself behind the animal and release the noose.

5. Handle special cases carefully:
- If a bird of prey is caught, examine it for injury.
- If injured, contact a wildlife officer for assistance in releasing it to a rehabilitator.
- If unharmed, cover the bird with a blanket or coat while depressing the trap’s springs.

6. Release safely:
- Remove the covering and allow the bird to fly away.

7. Avoid injury:
- Be extremely cautious of birds’ strong talons and beaks.

Engage in open discussions on the ethics and responsibilities associated with trapping:

• Consider what you could do in various scenarios.
• Reflect on what you should do based on ethical principles.
• Think about what you would do in real-life situations.

Example Scenarios for Discussion:

1. Scenario 1 - Your older cousin invites you to go trapping, but you come across a fence with “No Trespassing” signs. He suggests ignoring the sign. Consider: What should you do? What would you do?

2. Scenario 2 - You have set up a trapline and are invited to a party. Consider: Should you leave your traps unattended? What is the responsible choice here?

3. Scenario 3 - A friend introduces you to Mr. Smith, who allows trapping on his property. Later, another trapper accuses you of trespassing. Consider: How should you handle this situation ethically and legally?

- Minimizing harm to non-target animals.
- Ensuring compliance with wildlife regulations.

Selectivity refers to the tendency for a trap or trap set to target a single species.

• This is important for:
- Minimizing harm to non-target animals.
- Ensuring compliance with wildlife regulations.
- Improving trapping efficiency by focusing on specific species.
• Selective traps may include:
- Species-specific bait or lures.
- Trap designs that are less likely to catch unintended animals.
• Always ensure your trap is set in a way that minimizes harm to non-target wildlife.

Mammal Hunting (1) -

Obtaining Mammals for Food
Standard Source: Animals For Food
Procedure:

1. Identify mammal species in the area that are suitable for consumption.
2. Be aware of potential risks, such as disease transmission from scavenging mammals like opossums.
3. Set up traps or snares in areas where mammals are likely...
Procedure:

1. Identify mammal species in the area that are suitable for consumption.
2. Be aware of potential risks, such as disease transmission from scavenging mammals like opossums.
3. Set up traps or snares in areas where mammals are likely to be found (e.g., near water sources, feeding grounds).
4. Avoid placing traps or snares in areas where enemies may detect them in hostile environments.
5. Be cautious when handling trapped animals due to the risk of injury from their teeth and claws.
6. Note that some mammals, such as polar bears and bearded seals, have toxic levels of vitamin A in their livers and should not be consumed.
7. The platypus is an egg-laying mammal with poisonous glands and should not be eaten.
obtaining,mammals,food,hunting,trapping

Procedure:

Traps For Birds (1) -

Construct and Use an Ojibwa Bird Pole
Standard Source: Animals For Food
Title: Construct and Use an Ojibwa Bird Pole

Procedure:

1. Select Location: Place the bird pole in a relatively open area away from tall trees, near feeding areas, dusting areas, or watering holes for best results.
2. **Prepare...
Title: Construct and Use an Ojibwa Bird Pole

Procedure:

1. Select Location: Place the bird pole in a relatively open area away from tall trees, near feeding areas, dusting areas, or watering holes for best results.
2. Prepare Materials:
- Cut a pole 1.8 to 2.1 meters long and trim all limbs and foliage.
- Avoid using resinous wood such as pine.
3. Drill Hole in Pole:
- Drill a small-diameter hole about 5 to 7.5 centimeters down from the top of the pole.
4. Create Perch:
- Cut a small stick 10 to 15 centimeters long and shape one end so that it almost fits into the drilled hole.
5. Assemble Components:
- Plant the pole in the ground with the pointed end up.
- Tie a small weight (equal to the targeted bird species' weight) to a length of cordage.
- Pass the free end of the cordage through the hole and tie a slip noose that covers the perch.
6. Secure Perch:
- Tie a single overhand knot in the cordage and place the perch against the hole.
- Allow the cordage to slip through the hole until the overhand knot rests against the pole and the top of the perch.
7. Set Up Noose:
- Spread the noose over the perch, ensuring it covers the perch and drapes on both sides.
8. Operation:
- Birds will land on the perch to rest.
- As soon as a bird lands, the perch will fall, releasing the overhand knot and allowing the weight to drop.
- The noose tightens around the bird's feet, capturing it.
9. Adjust Weight Carefully:
- If the weight is too heavy, it may cut the bird’s feet off, allowing it to escape.
ojibwa bird pole,bird trap,hunting birds,trapping techniques

Tools For Hunting (1) -

Construct and Use a Noosing Wand
Standard Source: Animals For Food
Title: Construct and Use a Noosing Wand

Procedure:

1. Prepare the Wand:
- Create a pole as long as you can effectively handle.
- Attach a slip noose made of wire or stiff cordage at one end (the small end).
2. **Capture Roosting...
Title: Construct and Use a Noosing Wand

Procedure:

1. Prepare the Wand:
- Create a pole as long as you can effectively handle.
- Attach a slip noose made of wire or stiff cordage at one end (the small end).
2. Capture Roosting Birds:
- Slip the noose over the neck of a roosting bird and pull it tight to capture the animal.
3. Use for Small Mammals:
- Place the wand over a den hole and hide in a nearby blind.
- When an animal emerges, jerk the pole to tighten the noose and capture the animal.
4. Kill Prey:
- Carry a stout club to kill the prey after capturing it.
noosing wand,trapping birds,hunting mammals,capturing animals

Sling (1) -

Construct a Sling
Standard Source: Animals For Food
Materials Needed:
• Two pieces of cordage about sixty centimeters long
• A palmsized piece of leather or cloth
• Small rocks

Steps:
1. Tie two pieces of cordage, each about sixty centimeters long, at opposite ends of a palmsized piece of...
Materials Needed:
• Two pieces of cordage about sixty centimeters long
• A palmsized piece of leather or cloth
• Small rocks

Steps:
1. Tie two pieces of cordage, each about sixty centimeters long, at opposite ends of a palmsized piece of leather or cloth.
2. Place a rock in the cloth and wrap one cord around the middle finger and hold it in your palm.
3. Hold the other cord between the forefinger and thumb.
4. To throw the rock, spin the sling several times in a circle and release the cord between the thumb and forefinger.
5. Practice to gain proficiency with the sling.
sling,hunting,trapping,survival,field-expedient

Archery (1) -

Selecting an Appropriate Bow for Beginners
Reference Source: The Complete Outdoorsmans Handbook
Selecting an Appropriate Bow for Beginners

When choosing a bow, consider your physical strength and experience level:

1. For average-sized males: Start with a bow that draws between 30 to 40 pounds, ideally closer to 30 pounds.
2. **For...
Selecting an Appropriate Bow for Beginners

When choosing a bow, consider your physical strength and experience level:

1. For average-sized males: Start with a bow that draws between 30 to 40 pounds, ideally closer to 30 pounds.
2. For women or boys: A lighter bow is more suitable, ranging from 15 to 25 pounds.

Considerations for Choosing a Bow:
• The weight of the bow is classified based on the amount of energy required to draw a 28-inch arrow (the standard length for adult males).
• If you use shorter arrows (e.g., 26 inches), the effective draw weight may be less, but the bow is still referred to by its original classification.
• The bow should feel comfortable in your hand with a full grip and provide smooth performance during drawing and releasing the arrow without any shocking recoil.

Material Options for Bows:
• Traditional materials: Wood (yew, osage, lemonwood), bone, bamboo, sinew, leather.
• Modern materials: Fiberglass (virtually indestructible) and wood-fiberglass laminates (preferred by most archers).

Bow Styles:
• Recurved: Provides added power and ease of draw due to the extra bends on the tips. Ideal for beginners.
• Straight: Less common, but suitable for specific purposes.
• Semi-recurved: A middle ground between recurved and straight bows.

Bow Components:
• Upper and lower limbs: The flexible parts of the bow that store energy when drawn.
• Handle section: Contains the grip, sight window, and arrow rest.
• Bow string: Made from dacron strands. Features a 'serving' (a tightly wound thread) to protect against fraying and a nocking point for attaching the arrow.

Adjustments:
• The distance between the bow handle and the serving is called the *fistmele* and should be approximately equal to the distance from the bottom of your hand to your outstretched thumb. This can be adjusted by twisting the bow string before stringing the bow.
bow selection, archery for beginners, recurved bow, bow weight, fistmele, bow materials

• A good knife and honing stone

Notes:
The type of equipment needed depends on the type of game being hunted.

Wildlife Catching (1) -

Catching Wood-Pigeons
Reference Source: The Household Cyclopedia
Procedure:
1. In hard weather (especially when snow is on the ground), sweep the snow aside to create a clearing about 12 yards long and 3 feet wide.
2. Lag 20 small eel-hooks into the ground, each fastened by a peg and with a small bean on...
Procedure:
1. In hard weather (especially when snow is on the ground), sweep the snow aside to create a clearing about 12 yards long and 3 feet wide.
2. Lag 20 small eel-hooks into the ground, each fastened by a peg and with a small bean on top.
3. Ensure that only the point of the hook goes through the top of the bean, leaving the barb exposed on one side to prevent it from being pulled out when the bird struggles.
4. Alternatively, punch 2 or 3 holes in horse-beans using an iron bodkin.
5. Boil these beans in some common gin.
6. Many birds will become so drunk that they cannot fly up; others may perch on nearby trees.
7. Watch for them and wait until they fall from the trees.
wood_pigeon,capturing,hunting,trapping

Trapping Wild Fowl (1) -

How to Catch Wild-fowl Using Rabbit Traps in Shallow Water
Standard Source: The Household Cyclopedia
Procedure:

1. Identify shallow water areas (about 1 ft. deep) where wild fowl are observed feeding.
2. Lay a few rabbit traps in these areas.
3. Place a small amount of beans on the bridge of each trap, under the water.
4. This method is...
Procedure:

1. Identify shallow water areas (about 1 ft. deep) where wild fowl are observed feeding.
2. Lay a few rabbit traps in these areas.
3. Place a small amount of beans on the bridge of each trap, under the water.
4. This method is effective for catching wild fowl.

For deeper water (about 2 ft. deep):
1. Insert a long stick into the bottom of the pond, with about 1 foot above the water level.
2. Cut a slit at the top of the stick.
3. Tie a strong piece of pack-thread around a brick-bat or large stone.
4. Allow the string to be approximately 1 foot longer than the length from the stone to the water's surface.
5. Attach a small eel-hook, baited with bullock's lights, sheep's paunch, or a horse-bean, to the end of the string.
6. About 3-4 inches from the brick-bat, fasten a short stick (about the size of your little finger and 4 inches long), tying it securely to the string at its center with a single knot.
7. Place the section of string between the brick-bat and the short stick into the slit at the top of the long stick.
8. The short stick will prevent the weight of the brick-bat from pulling the string through the slit, keeping the hook hanging just above the water's surface.
9. When a wild fowl takes the baited hook, it pulls the short stick and brick-bat, which in turn pulls the bird underwater and drowns it.
wild fowl trapping, rabbit traps, fishing, pond trapping, waterfowl capture

Licensing Permits (1) -

Obtain Required Hunting Licenses and Stamps for Waterfowl Hunting in Wisconsin
Standard Source: Basics of Waterfowling
Steps to Obtain Required Licenses and Stamps:

1. Purchase a Small Game License:
- *Cost*: $18 for in-state residents, $85 or $55 for out-of-state or non-resident 5-day licenses.
- *Allowed Activities*: Hunting waterfowl, upland game...
Steps to Obtain Required Licenses and Stamps:

1. Purchase a Small Game License:
- *Cost*: $18 for in-state residents, $85 or $55 for out-of-state or non-resident 5-day licenses.
- *Allowed Activities*: Hunting waterfowl, upland game birds, squirrels, rabbits, coyotes, foxes, etc.

2. Purchase a State Duck Stamp:
- *Cost*: $7 for both in-state and out-of-state residents.
- *Purpose*: Two-thirds of the revenue is used for in-state waterfowl habitat and/or wetland restoration. The remaining one-third supports waterfowl habitat and/or wetland restoration in Manitoba, Canada.
- *Availability*: Can be purchased physically at a DNR service center or online via the Online Order Form.

3. Obtain HIP Certification:
- *Cost*: Free ($0).
- *Purpose*: Part of a national program to monitor hunter numbers and harvest, required for anyone hunting migratory game birds (ducks, geese, woodcock, mourning dove, snipe, rails, gallinules [moorhens], and coots).
- *Benefits*: Places you on a federal list of hunters who may receive a federal hunting or wing survey.

4. Purchase a Federal Duck Stamp:
- *Cost*: $25.
- *Purpose*: Required for waterfowl hunting in the United States, with proceeds supporting the Migratory Bird Conservation Act and wetland conservation efforts.

Where to Purchase: Licenses and stamps can be obtained online at GOWILD or at a DNR service center (http://dnr.wi.gov/Contact/SSbyRegion.html).
Wisconsin hunting license, duck stamp, HIP certification, waterfowl hunting permits, small game license

Waterfowling Legislation (1) -

Understand the History of Waterfowl Hunting Legislation
Reference Source: Basics of Waterfowling
Purpose: To understand the historical context and legal framework that supports waterfowl conservation and hunting.

Procedure:
1. Study the Lacey Act (1900), which gave federal authority to prohibit illegal take, possession,...
Purpose: To understand the historical context and legal framework that supports waterfowl conservation and hunting.

Procedure:
1. Study the Lacey Act (1900), which gave federal authority to prohibit illegal take, possession, transportation, or sale of wildlife and established penalties for violations.
2. Learn about the Migratory Bird Treaty (1916), signed by the United States and Canada, followed by other countries like Mexico, Japan, and Russia.
3. Understand the Migratory Bird Treaty Act (1918), which regulates migratory bird hunting.
4. Review the Migratory Bird Hunting & Conservation Stamp Act (1934), which funds habitat protection through duck stamp sales.
5. Study the Pittman-Robertson Act (1937), which provides funding to state wildlife management programs.
6. Learn about the Endangered Species Act (1973), which protects species at risk of extinction.
7. Understand the impact of the Clean Water Act and Swamp Busters, both of which have saved critical waterfowl breeding habitat.
Lacey Act, Migratory Bird Treaty, Pittman-Robertson Act, Endangered Species Act, waterfowl legislation

Waterfowling Surveys (1) -

Participate in Waterfowl Breeding Population and Habitat Survey
Reference Source: Basics of Waterfowling
Purpose: To contribute to the annual survey that monitors waterfowl populations and wetland habitats.

Procedure:
1. Learn about the Waterfowl Breeding Population and Habitat Survey, which has been conducted annually since 1955.
2....
Purpose: To contribute to the annual survey that monitors waterfowl populations and wetland habitats.

Procedure:
1. Learn about the Waterfowl Breeding Population and Habitat Survey, which has been conducted annually since 1955.
2. Understand how biologists fly approximately 80,000 miles of transects each year to count waterfowl and wetlands across over 2 million square miles in North America.
3. If you are in Wisconsin, participate in the Wisconsin Waterfowl Survey, which has been conducted since 1973 using protocols similar to the national survey.
4. Provide data collected during the survey to the US Fish and Wildlife Service or appropriate state agencies as required.
Waterfowl Breeding Survey, Wisconsin waterfowl survey, wetland monitoring, waterfowl population tracking

Purpose: To access opportunities provided by the Wisconsin DNR to start or enhance your waterfowl hunting experience.

Procedure:
1. Research and identify WDNR-sponsored programs, such as youth hunting events, mentorship programs for new hunters, and shotgun training sessions.

Equipment Usage (1) -

Choose Appropriate Choke and Ammo Combinations for Your Gun
Standard Source: Basics of Waterfowling
Objective: Select the best choke and ammunition combinations to enhance shooting performance.

Steps:
1. Consult your shotgun's manufacturer specifications or a qualified firearms expert to determine compatible chokes (e.g., full, modified,...
Objective: Select the best choke and ammunition combinations to enhance shooting performance.

Steps:
1. Consult your shotgun's manufacturer specifications or a qualified firearms expert to determine compatible chokes (e.g., full, modified, skeet) and appropriate ammunition types.
2. Consider the type of game you are hunting (e.g., waterfowl, upland birds) and the distance at which you typically shoot.
3. Select a choke that provides optimal pattern density for your shooting range and target size.
4. Choose ammunition with the correct shot size and load for your gun's barrel diameter and intended use.
5. Test different combinations in a safe environment to determine what works best for your shooting style.

Details:
• Use non-toxic shot (e.g., steel, bismuth) as required by law or ethical hunting standards.
• Ensure that the ammunition is compatible with your gun's action and barrel length.
• Always follow local regulations regarding choke usage and ammunition types.
choke selection, ammo combinations, shotgun performance, waterfowl hunting

Objective: Gain experience and confidence through guided hunts led by skilled mentors.

Steps:
1. Contact local wildlife agencies or conservation organizations that offer "Learn to Hunt" programs.
2. Register for a program that includes experienced hunting mentors, local DNR wildlife biologists, or conservation wardens.
3. Attend the program and follow the guidance of your mentor during the hunt.
4. Practice shooting techniques, range estimation, and ethical hunting practices under supervision.
5. Learn about firearm safety, wildlife identification, and legal hunting regulations.

• Mentors will assist with equipment setup, target acquisition, and safe shooting practices.
• Programs may be organized by local groups or organizations in conjunction with the Wisconsin DNR.

Ethics And Laws (1) -

Practice Hunting Ethics to Promote Responsible Behavior in the Field
Reference Source: Basics of Waterfowling
Objective: Uphold ethical hunting practices that respect wildlife, other hunters, and landowners.

Steps:
1. Hunt with fair chase principles in mind, avoiding methods that give an unfair advantage over game.
2. Pursue game responsibly by...
Objective: Uphold ethical hunting practices that respect wildlife, other hunters, and landowners.

Steps:
1. Hunt with fair chase principles in mind, avoiding methods that give an unfair advantage over game.
2. Pursue game responsibly by following local regulations and ensuring that you are legally allowed to hunt the species and area.
3. Always respect the rights of other hunters, landowners, and non-hunters who use or enjoy the resource.
4. Avoid actions that could harm wildlife or damage the environment (e.g., littering, disturbing nesting areas).
5. Pick up trash and leave the hunting area as you found it to maintain a clean and safe environment for all users.

Details:
• Ethical hunting helps preserve wildlife populations and maintains public support for hunting activities.
• Hunters should be aware of their impact on the land and take steps to minimize disturbances.
• Use non-toxic shot (e.g., steel, bismuth) as required by law or ethical standards.
hunting ethics, fair chase, wildlife conservation, responsible hunting

Identification Skills (1) -

Improve Waterfowl Identification Skills for Better Hunting Accuracy
Reference Source: Basics of Waterfowling
Objective: Enhance your ability to identify waterfowl at various distances and improve shooting accuracy.

Steps:
1. Spend time in the marsh or other natural habitats where waterfowl are commonly found to observe their behavior and...
Objective: Enhance your ability to identify waterfowl at various distances and improve shooting accuracy.

Steps:
1. Spend time in the marsh or other natural habitats where waterfowl are commonly found to observe their behavior and appearance.
2. Use field guides such as *Ducks at a Distance: Waterfowl Identification Guide* or *Delta Waterfowl: Waterfowl Identification Guide* to study different species.
3. Practice identifying ducks based on size, shape, color patterns, and flight characteristics from various distances.
4. Compare your observations with the information in identification guides to refine your skills.
5. Use this knowledge during hunting sessions to improve accuracy and reduce the risk of shooting non-target species.

Details:
• Regular practice is essential for developing strong waterfowl identification skills.
• Field guides are valuable tools that provide detailed descriptions, illustrations, and range-specific information.
• Understanding how different species look at various distances helps hunters make better decisions in the field.
waterfowl identification, hunting accuracy, field guides, duck species recognition

Ammunition Selection (1) -

Select Appropriate Shot Size for Waterfowl Hunting
High Priority Source: Basics of Waterfowling
To determine the appropriate shot size for hunting large ducks over decoys, follow these steps:

1. Pattern your shotgun by shooting at a 30" circle target from 30 yards using an Improved Cylinder choke.
2. Use shot sizes between 2-6.
3. Count the...
To determine the appropriate shot size for hunting large ducks over decoys, follow these steps:

1. Pattern your shotgun by shooting at a 30" circle target from 30 yards using an Improved Cylinder choke.
2. Use shot sizes between 2-6.
3. Count the number of pellets that land within the 30" circle.
4. If you find approximately 85-90 pellets, your setup is suitable for hunting at this range.
5. If not, consider changing the shot size, brand of shell, or choke (modified if more pellets are needed, skeet if fewer pellets are needed).
shot size selection, patterning, improved cylinder choke, lethality table

Environmental Compliance (1) -

Prevent the Spread of Aquatic Invasive Species
Standard Source: Basics of Waterfowling
To help prevent the spread of aquatic invasive species, follow these steps:

• Inspect watercraft, waders, equipment, and gear (including dogs) before moving to a new waterbody.
• Remove any attached aquatic plants, animals, or mud from all...
To help prevent the spread of aquatic invasive species, follow these steps:

• Inspect watercraft, waders, equipment, and gear (including dogs) before moving to a new waterbody.
• Remove any attached aquatic plants, animals, or mud from all items.
• Drain all water from boats, motors, and other equipment.

These actions are required by law to protect the environment and economy.
aquatic invasive species, inspection, removal, drainage, environmental protection

Blinds Construction (1) -

Constructing and Using Hunting Blinds
Standard Source: Basics of Waterfowling
Purpose: To conceal hunters and equipment while waterfowl hunting.

Materials Needed:
• Woody vegetation, wood pallets, chicken wire, burlap, synthetic brush material, or hard-sided blind materials.
• Name, address, and DNR customer ID...
Purpose: To conceal hunters and equipment while waterfowl hunting.

Materials Needed:
• Woody vegetation, wood pallets, chicken wire, burlap, synthetic brush material, or hard-sided blind materials.
• Name, address, and DNR customer ID printed in one square inch or larger print (for public lands).

Steps for Building a Blind on Public Lands:
1. Choose a location that is 7 days prior to the waterfowl season and must be removed 7 days after the season closes.
2. Construct the blind using materials such as wood pallets, chicken wire, or synthetic brush material.
3. Ensure the blind owner’s name, address, and DNR customer ID are printed in one square inch or larger print and are visible at all times.
4. Remove all materials from the blind site once the season ends.

Alternative Blind Options:
• Use natural cover such as woody vegetation stuck in the ground.
• Utilize a boat as a mobile blind by covering it with burlap or synthetic brush material.
• Construct layout blinds using residual stubble or synthetic materials to blend into agricultural fields.

Tips for Concealment:
• Use synthetic or natural brush materials to "brush" the blind and help conceal yourself in areas with little cover.
• Position the blind so that it keeps decoys and decoying birds within range, especially in agricultural fields.
blind construction, hunting blinds, concealment, waterfowl hunting, public land blind rules

Duck Calling (1) -

Using Duck and Goose Calls Effectively
Reference Source: Basics of Waterfowling
Purpose: To attract ducks and geese during waterfowl hunting.

Call Types and Usage:
• Basic Quack: Whit, dwit, or kwit (quit)
• Greeting Call: 5 quacks in a row
• Feeding Call: Ticka, ticka, ticka, or dugga, dugga, dugga in a...
Purpose: To attract ducks and geese during waterfowl hunting.

Call Types and Usage:
• Basic Quack: Whit, dwit, or kwit (quit)
• Greeting Call: 5 quacks in a row
• Feeding Call: Ticka, ticka, ticka, or dugga, dugga, dugga in a long strung-out string

Tips for Success:
• Practice calling regularly to improve your technique.
• Listen to real ducks and geese in the marsh to understand their sounds better.
• Know where ducks want to be; if you know that, you may not need to call at all.
• Use a video demonstration on how to call ducks for additional guidance (if available).

Note: A duck call in the hands of an unskilled hunter is still one of conservation’s greatest assets. Even with limited calling ability, knowing where the birds want to be can lead to success.
duck call, goose call, waterfowl hunting, calling techniques

Purpose: To locate and retrieve downed birds during waterfowl hunting.

Benefits of a Well-Trained Dog:
• Even an untrained dog can be more effective than humans in finding birds in thick vegetated cover.
• A trained dog becomes a valuable asset for retrieving birds efficiently.

Training Tips:
1. Find training areas near you using the DOG TRAINING website (especially in Wisconsin).
2. Practice with your dog regularly, even if it is not specifically trained for waterfowl hunting.
3. Some facilities are focused on upland game birds, but any practice will improve your dog’s ability to hunt in the field.

Note: Dogs can be used in both marsh and field environments. Ensure that your dog is comfortable with retrieving in different terrains.
hunting dogs, bird retrieval, dog training, waterfowl hunting

Wild turkeys will eat a wide variety of food that provides them with nutrition.

Diet includes:
• Fresh green vegetation
• Seeds
• Fruit
• Invertebrates (e.g., grasshoppers)
• Plants
• Small lizards or frogs

Feeding behavior:

• A field full of grasshoppers is sure to attract turkeys.
